Distinguishing Legitimate Optimism from Forbidden Divination
Distinguishing types of divination and permissible optimism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between fa'l (legitimate optimism) and istiqsam/tayara (forbidden divination) based on the presence of tawakkul.

Core Idea
Optimism strengthens trust in God; divination replaces trust with manipulation.

Ibn Taymiyya draws a sharp line between the practice of optimism (fa'l) that the Prophet approved of, and all forms of divination that involve manipulating objects to gain hidden knowledge. The key difference lies in one's reliance on God versus reliance on the method. This lesson examines that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Define istiqsam
Find the author's definition: any action that changes physical things to extract hidden knowledge. This includes using arrows, pebbles, or letters.
The author broadens it beyond the specific pre-Islamic practice.
2
Define fa'l
Legitimate optimism: going out trusting in God and hearing a good word. It does not involve manipulation.
The Prophet liked fa'l because it reinforces tawakkul.
3
Contrast the two
Istisqam seeks knowledge through human action; fa'l is passive and strengthens reliance on God. Pessimism (tayara) is the opposite of tawakkul and harms only the pessimist.
4
Apply the distinction
Consider a practice like reading coffee grounds: it involves manipulation of a physical object to gain knowledge. It falls under istiqsam, not fa'l.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والشعير والقُرعة بأبجد ونحو ذلك مما هو من جنس الاستقسام بالأزلام، فإنهم يطلبون علم الحوادث بما يفعلونه من الاستقسام بها، سواء علم ما كانت قداحًا أو حصى أو غير ذلك مما ذكره أهل العلم بالتفسير. فكل ما يُحدِثه الإنسان بحركة من تغيير شيء من الأجسام ليستخرج به ما يجهله فهو من هذا الجنس، بخلاف الفأل الشرعي، وهو الذي كان يُعْجِبُ النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم، وهو أن يخرج متوكلا على الله، فيسمع الكلمة الطيبة وكان يُعْجِبُه الفأل، ويكره الطَّيَرة؛ لأن الفأل تقوية لما فعله بإذن الله والتوكَّل عليه، والطَّيَرةُ معارضة لذلك، فكره للإنسان أن يتطيَّر، وإنما تضرُّ الطيرةُ مَن تطيَّر لأنه ضرَّ نفسَه، فأما المتوكل على الله فلا. وليس المقصود ذكر هذه الأمور وسبب إصابتها تارةً وخطئها تارات وإنما الغرض أنهم يتعمَّدون فيها كذبًا كثيرًا من غير أن تكون دلت على ذلك، كما يتعمد خلق كثير الكذب في الرؤيا الصالحة وهي جزء من ستة وأربعين جزءا من النبوَّة، وكما كانت الجن تخلط بالكلمة تَسْمَعُها من السماء مئة كذبة. ولهذا ثبت في صحيح مسلم عن معاوية بن الحكم السلمي قال: قلت: يا رسول الله، إني حديث عهد بجاهلية، وقد جاء الله بالإسلام، وإن منا رجالا يأتون الكُهَّان؟ قال: «فلا تأتِهم». قال: قلت: ومنا رجال يتطيَّرون؟ قال: «ذاك شيء يجدونه في صدورهم فلا يصدنهم، قال: قلت: ومنا رجالٌ يَخُطُون؟ قال: كان نبي من الأنبياء يَخُطّ، فمن وافق خطّه فذاك». فإذا كان ما هو من أجزاء النبوَّة ومن أخبار الملائكة قد يُتعَمَّدُ فيه الكذبُ الكثير، فكيف بما هو في نفسه مضطرب لا يستقر على أصل ؟! لهذا تجد عامة من في دينه فساد يدخُل في الأكاذيب الكونية، مثـل أهـل الاتحاد، فإن أبن عربي في كتاب «عنقاء مُغْرِب» وغيره أخبر بمستقبلاتٍ كثيرة عامتها كذب، وكذلك أبن سبعين. وكذلك الذين استخرجوا مدَّة بقاء هذه الأمة من حساب الجُمَّل من حروف المعجم الذي وَرِثُوه من اليهود ومن حركات الكواكب الذي وَرِثُوه
And [the like of] barley and the lot by abjad and similar things that are of the kind of divination by arrows, for they seek knowledge of events by what they do of divination with them, whether they know [the items] were arrow-shafts or pebbles or other than that, which the people of knowledge of exegesis mentioned. So everything that a man produces by a movement of changing something of bodies to extract by it what he is ignorant of, it is of this kind, contrary to the legitimate optimism, which used to please the Prophet, peace be upon him, and it is that he goes out trusting in God, then hears a good word, and he used to like optimism and dislike pessimism; because optimism strengthens what he does by permission of God and trust in Him, and pessimism opposes that, so it is disliked for a person to be pessimistic, and indeed pessimism harms only the one who is pessimistic because he harms himself; as for the one who trusts in God, then no. And the intent is not to mention these matters and the reason for their hitting sometimes and their missing other times, but rather the purpose is that they intentionally [commit] in them much lying without that [the means] having indicated that, just as many people intentionally lie in the righteous vision, and it is a part of forty-six parts of prophethood, and just as the jinn used to mix with the word they hear from the sky a hundred lies. And for this it is established in Sahih Muslim from Mu'awiya ibn al-Hakam al-Sulami, he said: I said, O Messenger of God, I am recently from the time of ignorance, and God has brought Islam, and among us are men who come to soothsayers? He said: 'Do not come to them.' He said: I said, And among us are men who practice pessimism? He said: 'That is something they find in their chests, so let it not deter them.' He said: I said, And among us are men who draw lines? He said: 'There was a prophet among the prophets who drew lines, so whoever's line coincides, then that is it.' So if what is of the parts of prophethood and of the reports of the angels may be intentionally lied in much, then how about what is in itself unstable, not established on a foundation?! For this you find generally those in whose religion there is corruption enter into cosmic lies, like the proponents of monism (ittihad), for Ibn 'Arabi in his book 'Anqa' Mughrib and others informed of many futures, most of which are lies, and likewise Ibn Sab'in. And likewise those who extracted the duration of the remaining of this nation from the calculation of the 'jammal' (alphanumerical) of the letters of the alphabet that they inherited from the Jews, and from the movements of the planets that they inherited...
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage is a critical exposition by Ibn Taymiyya (or the author of Naqd Mantiq) against various forms of divination and false claims to knowledge of the unseen. The author begins by classifying practices like using barley, lots marked with letters (abjad), and similar methods as types of divination (istiqsam) akin to the pre-Islamic practice of drawing arrows. He argues that any human action that alters physical objects to extract hidden knowledge falls into this condemned category. He then distinguishes this from legitimate optimism (fa'l), which the Prophet approved of, because fa'l is based on trust in God and hearing good words, whereas pessimism (tayara) is disliked and harms only the one who indulges in it. The author states his main point: not to discuss why these methods sometimes hit or miss, but to highlight that practitioners deliberately mix in many lies, just as people lie about dreams (which are a part of prophethood) and the jinn mix lies with the truth they steal from heaven. He then cites the hadith from Sahih Muslim where the Prophet forbids consulting soothsayers, allows that pessimism is a feeling in the chest but should not deter one, and permits line-drawing (khatt) only if it coincidentally matches a previous prophet's method. The key argument is: if even true prophecy and angelic reports can be mixed with many lies, then how much more unreliable are inherently unstable methods like astrology and numerology? The author concludes by criticizing monistic mystics (like Ibn 'Arabi and Ibn Sab'in) who fabricated many false predictions, and those who calculate the lifespan of the Islamic community using Jewish numerology and planetary motions. The commentary thus underscores the epistemological critique: these methods are not valid sources of knowledge because they are prone to deliberate falsehood and lack a sound foundation.

Key Terms
استقسام
فأل
طيرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes fa'l (optimism) from istiqsam (divination)?
A) Fa'l involves manipulation of objects; istiqsam does not.
B) Fa'l strengthens trust in God; istiqsam seeks knowledge by altering physical things.
C) Both are forbidden.
D) Fa'l is only for prophets.

Key Takeaway
The legitimacy of a practice for seeking hidden knowledge depends on whether it reinforces tawakkul or replaces it with manipulation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Explain why the Prophet disliked tayara (pessimism) even though it sometimes occurs. Use the passage's reasoning.
?
Classify the practice of throwing dice to make a decision as istiqsam or fa'l. Justify your answer.

Ibn Taymiyya's Epistemological Argument Against Divination
Epistemological critique of divination using an a fortiori argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's a fortiori argument that divination is epistemically invalid due to intentional falsehood.

Core Idea
Even true prophecy can be mixed with lies; unstable methods are even more unreliable.

The passage's core argument is that practitioners of divination deliberately mix lies with whatever truth they might occasionally hit. To prove this, Ibn Taymiyya points out that even righteous visions (a part of prophethood) are often lied about, and the jinn mix a hundred lies with each word they hear from heaven. If such reliable sources can be corrupted, then how much more corruptible are inherently unstable methods? This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
The author says: 'they intentionally commit much lying without the means having indicated that.' Practitioners add lies to whatever little truth there may be.
2
Find the supporting evidence
He gives two examples: people lie about righteous visions (part of prophethood), and the jinn mix a hundred lies with each true word they hear from heaven.
The hadith about jinn is alluded to.
3
Understand the logical structure
This is an argument a fortiori (qiyas awla): if even the most reliable sources (prophecy, angelic reports) are corrupted by falsehood, then less stable methods (divination) are even more prone to falsehood.
The conclusion is that divination cannot be a reliable source of knowledge.
4
Apply to an example
Consider an astrologer who says his predictions sometimes come true. According to this argument, those successes are coincidental; the systematic addition of lies invalidates the method.

Refuting Mystical and Numerological Predictions
Application of earlier critique to mystics and numerologists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical fallacy in using numerology (abjad) or astrology to predict the future, specifically the lifespan of the Islamic community.

Core Idea
Predictions based on letters and stars are fabrications inherited from non-Muslims, not from revelation.

Ibn Taymiyya applies his general critique to specific groups: monistic mystics like Ibn 'Arabi and those who calculate the end of the ummah using letter values and planetary motions. He dismisses their predictions as mostly lies, noting that they have been misled by Jewish and pagan sources. This lesson focuses on why such methods are invalid and how they corrupt religion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the groups criticized
The author mentions proponents of monism (ittihad) like Ibn 'Arabi and Ibn Sab'in, and those who use letter calculations (abjad) from Jews and planetary movements to predict the duration of the ummah.
2
Understand their claims
They claim to know specific future events, e.g., Ibn 'Arabi's book 'Anqa' Mughrib contained many predictions. Others calculated the remaining time of the Islamic nation.
3
Link to the general critique
These practices fall under the definition of istiqsam: they manipulate symbols (letters, numbers, planets) to extract hidden knowledge.
4
Evaluate the foundation
The author says these methods are 'unstable, not established on an original' (i.e., not based on revelation). They are inherited from non-Muslims and are mostly lies.
Even if some predictions appear true, they are coincidental or fabricated.

The author begins by classifying practices like using barley, lots marked with letters (abjad), and similar methods as types of divination (istiqsam) akin to the pre-Islamic practice of drawing arrows. He argues that any human action that alters physical objects to extract hidden knowledge falls into this condemned category. He then distinguishes this from legitimate optimism (fa'l), which the Prophet approved of, because fa'l is based on trust in God and hearing good words, whereas pessimism (tayara) is disliked and harms only the one who indulges in it. The author states his main point: not to discuss why these methods sometimes hit or miss, but to highlight that practitioners deliberately mix in many lies, just as people lie about dreams (which are a part of prophethood) and the jinn mix lies with the truth they steal from heaven. He then cites the hadith from Sahih Muslim where the Prophet forbids consulting soothsayers, allows that pessimism is a feeling in the chest but should not deter one, and permits line-drawing (khatt) only if it coincidentally matches a previous prophet's method. The key argument is: if even true prophecy and angelic reports can be mixed with many lies, then how much more unreliable are inherently unstable methods like astrology and numerology? The author concludes by criticizing monistic mystics (like Ibn 'Arabi and Ibn Sab'in) who fabricated many false predictions, and those who calculate the lifespan of the Islamic community using Jewish numerology and planetary motions. The commentary thus underscores the epistemological critique: these methods are not valid sources of knowledge because they are prone to deliberate falsehood and lack a sound foundation.

Key Terms
اتحاد
حساب الجمل
كذب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) From the Jews and from planetary movements inherited from them
C) From the revelation of the Quran
D) From the early Sufi masters

Key Takeaway
Predictions based on numerology and astrology are invalid because they are inherited from non-Islamic sources and are not grounded in revelation, despite occasional coincidences.

Ibn Taymiyya on Deliberate Falsehood Mixed with Truth in Predictions
Ibn Taymiyya argues that falsehood is deliberately mixed with truth in various predictive practices.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's a fortiori argument that even prophethood-related sources are deliberately corrupted, making unreliable methods like astrology even more suspect.

Core Idea
Even prophethood-related knowledge can be mixed with lies; other methods are even worse.

Ibn Taymiyya attacks all forms of predicting the unseen—astrology, numerology, mystical foretelling—by showing that deliberate falsehood is mixed even in the most reliable sources. This lesson walks through his evidence from hadith and his extension to various misguided groups, enabling you to evaluate the epistemological basis of any claim to knowledge of the future.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key premise about lies in prophethood
Ibn Taymiyya states that many lies are deliberately made in righteous dreams (which are part of prophethood) and that jinn mix a hundred lies with the single word of truth they hear from heaven. This establishes that even authentic sources can be corrupted.
The hadith about jinn is from Sahih Muslim.
2
Analyze the hadith from Sahih Muslim
In the hadith, the Prophet forbids going to soothsayers, permits ignoring augury (tayyara) as something found in hearts, and allows line-drawing only if it coincides with a previous prophet's method. This shows that some practices are prohibited or unreliable.
The hadith classifies soothsayers and diviners as unreliable, while line-drawing has an exception but not a general endorsement.
3
Extract the inferential leap
Ibn Taymiyya argues: if even parts of prophethood (dreams, angelic reports) have deliberate lies mixed in, then any method that lacks a solid foundation—such as astrology or numerology—is even more susceptible to falsehood. This is an a fortiori (qiyas al-awlawi) argument.
This is the core of his critique.
4
List the criticized groups and their methods
Ibn Taymiyya names: ahl al-ittihad (Ibn Arabi and Ibn Sab'in) who make future predictions; those using hisab al-jummal (letter numerical values) inherited from Jews; astrologers using planetary motions inherited from Sabians; philosophers like al-Kindi; Quran interpreters relying on opinion; and ascetics leaning toward Shi'ism.
These methods are all considered unreliable because they lack a stable, revealed basis.
5
Evaluate the argument's strength
The argument is logically valid: if P (authentic sources) are sometimes false, then Q (less authentic sources) are even more likely false. The hadith supports the premise. However, one could question whether all practices mentioned are equally lacking foundation. Ibn Taymiyya's extensive list shows the breadth of his critique.
The critique is empirical (based on observed lies) and epistemological (based on the weakness of the sources).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذلك (١)، كما يتعمد خلق كثير الكذب في الرؤيا الصالحة وهي جزء من ستة وأربعين جزءا من النبوَّة (۲) ، وكما كانت الجن تخلط بالكلمة تَسْمَعُها من السماء مئة كذبة (۳). ولهذا ثبت في صحيح مسلم» (٤) عن معاوية بن الحكم السلمي قال: قلت: يا رسول الله، إني حديث عهد بجاهلية، وقد جاء الله بالإسلام، وإن منا رجالا يأتون الكُهَّان؟ قال: «فلا تأتِهم». قال: قلت: ومنا رجال يتطيَّرون؟ قال: «ذاك شيء يجدونه في صدورهم فلا يصدنهم، قال: قلت: ومنا رجالٌ يَخُطُون؟ قال: كان نبي من الأنبياء يَخُطّ، فمن وافق خطّه فذاك». فإذا كان ما هو من أجزاء النبوَّة ومن أخبار الملائكة قد يُتعَمَّدُ فيه الكذبُ الكثير، فكيف بما هو في نفسه مضطرب لا يستقر على أصل ؟! لهذا تجد عامة من في دينه فساد يدخُل في الأكاذيب الكونية، مثـل أهـل الاتحاد، فإن أبن عربي في كتاب «عنقاء مُغْرِب» (٥) وغيره أخبر بمستقبلاتٍ كثيرة عامتها كذب، وكذلك أبن سبعين. وكذلك الذين استخرجوا مدَّة بقاء هذه الأمة من حساب الجُمَّل من حروف المعجم الذي وَرِثُوه من اليهود ومن حركات الكواكب الذي وَرِثُوه من الصابئة (١)، كما فعل أبو نصر الكندي (٢) وغيره من الفلاسفة، وكما فعل بعض من تكلَّم في تفسير القرآن من أصحاب الرأي(۳)، ومن تكلم في تأويل وقائع النساك(٤) من المائلين إلى التشيع.
That (1), just as many lies are deliberately made in righteous dreams, and it is a part of forty-six parts of prophethood (2), and just as the jinn mix a hundred lies with the word they hear from the sky (3). For this reason, it is confirmed in Sahih Muslim (4) from Mu'awiya ibn al-Hakam al-Sulami who said: I said: O Messenger of Allah, I am recently from the Jahiliyyah, and Allah has brought Islam, and among us there are men who go to soothsayers? He said: 'Do not go to them.' He said: I said: And among us there are men who practice augury (tayyara)? He said: 'That is something they find in their hearts, so let it not deter them.' He said: I said: And among us there are men who draw lines (khatt)? He said: 'There was a prophet among the prophets who used to draw lines; whoever's line coincides with his, then that is it.' So if what is of the parts of prophethood and of the reports of the angels can have many lies deliberately placed in it, then what about that which is in itself disturbed, not established upon a foundation?! For this reason you will find that most of those whose religion is corrupted enter into cosmic lies, like the people of union (ittihad), for Ibn Arabi in his book 'Anqa' Mughrib (5) and elsewhere reported many future events, most of which are lies, and likewise Ibn Sab'in. And likewise those who extracted the duration of the remaining time of this nation from the arithmetic (hisab al-jummal) of the letters of the alphabet which they inherited from the Jews, and from the motions of the planets which they inherited from the Sabians (1), as did Abu Nasr al-Kindi (2) and other philosophers, and as did some of those who spoke about the interpretation of the Qur'an from the people of opinion (ra'y) (3), and those who spoke about the interpretation of the events of ascetics (nusak) (4) from those inclined to Shi'ism.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument by Ibn Taymiyya against those who claim knowledge of future events through questionable means. He begins by citing authentic hadiths to establish a key principle: even in things that are part of prophethood—like true dreams and angelic communications—lies are deliberately mixed. For example, righteous dreams are one part of prophethood, yet many lies are fabricated in them. Similarly, the jinn mix a hundred lies with a single word of truth heard from heaven. Moreover, the Prophet запретил going to soothsayers and diviners, though he acknowledged that a previous prophet used line-drawing, but only if one's line matches his exactly. From these premises, Ibn Taymiyya draws a powerful conclusion: if even those sources that are genuinely from the angels or prophethood can be corrupted with deliberate falsehood, then what about systems that are inherently unreliable and have no stable foundation? He then identifies several groups that engage in such false predictions: the pantheistic monists (ahl al-ittihad) like Ibn Arabi and Ibn Sab'in, who made many false forecasts; those who use arithmetic based on letter values (hisab al-jummal) inherited from Jews; astrologers who use planetary motions inherited from Sabians; philosophers like al-Kindi; certain Quran interpreters who rely on personal opinion; and some who interpret the experiences of ascetics, leaning toward Shi'ism. All these, he argues, produce 'cosmic lies' because their methods lack a solid basis. The overall argument is that falsehood is deliberately mixed with truth in various predictive arts, and therefore they cannot be relied upon for certain knowledge. This is a typical Ibn Taymiyyan critique of astrology, numerology, and speculative interpretation, grounded in hadith and sound reasoning.

الرؤيا الصالحة
الكهان
الخط
حساب الجُمَّل
حركات الكواكب
أهل الاتحاد

Key Takeaway
Falsehood is deliberately mixed with truth not only in forbidden arts but even in authentic sources like dreams, so any predictive method lacking a solid foundation is untrustworthy.

Ibn Taymiyya's Identification of Deviant Groups
Author lists groups (Sabians, philosophers, interpreters) who rely on weak evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four groups Ibn Taymiyya criticizes in this passage and explain his general charge of relying on weak evidence.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya exposes specific groups that base their knowledge on weak evidence rather than revelation.

In this critical passage, Ibn Taymiyya begins his attack by listing the types of people who are guilty of misinterpreting texts and experiences. He does not name all possible heretics, but a select few that represent key epistemological errors: pre-Islamic star worship, Greek rationalism, subjective opinion, and sectarian bias. By identifying these groups, he sets the stage for his broader critique of those who rely on weak evidence.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the framework
Ibn Taymiyya starts with 'Min al-Sabi'a...' ('From the Sabians...'). This shows he is giving examples from a larger set. Notice the pattern: each group is introduced with 'as did...' (kama fa‘ala).
The passage is a single sentence listing examples.
2
Identify the first group
The Sabians (al-Sabi'a) – a pre-Islamic group associated with star worship. Ibn Taymiyya consistently links them to philosophers and heretics who prioritize cosmic observation over revelation.
Sabians are a polemical category in Ibn Taymiyya's works.
3
Identify the second group
Abu Nasr al-Kindi and other philosophers (al-falasifa). Al-Kindi is a key figure in the translation of Greek philosophy. Ibn Taymiyya criticizes his reliance on Aristotelian logic.
Al-Kindi represents the broader philosophical tradition.
4
Identify the third group
Some who spoke about Quran interpretation from among 'ahl al-ra'y' (people of opinion). These are rationalist theologians who prioritize personal reasoning over transmitted tafsir.
Ahl al-ra'y often refers to Mu'tazila or other rationalist groups.
5
Identify the fourth group
Some who interpret the events (or states) of ascetics (nussak) from those inclined to Shi'ism. This points to those who read mystical experiences through a sectarian lens.
This group mixes ascetic practice with Shi'i doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من الصابئة، كما فعل أبو نصر الكندي وغيره من الفلاسفة، وكما فعل بعض من تكلَّم في تفسير القرآن من أصحاب الرأي، ومن تكلم في تأويل وقائع النساك من المائلين إلى التشيع.
From the Sabians, as did Abu Nasr al-Kindi and other philosophers, and as did some of those who spoke about the interpretation of the Quran from the people of opinion, and some who spoke about the interpretation of the events of the ascetics from those inclined to Shi'ism.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is a critical enumeration from Ibn Taymiyya's Naqd al-Mantiq. He identifies several groups he considers deviant in their methodology: the Sabians (a pre-Islamic religious group), the philosopher al-Kindi, certain Quran interpreters from the ahl al-ra'y (rationalist theologians), and those who interpret the experiences of ascetics from a Shi'i perspective. This list sets the stage for his broader critique of those who rely on weak evidence and misinterpretation.

Refuting False Esoteric Claims: Ibn Taymiyya's Method
Author recounts his encounter with followers who claimed their shaykh was the Messiah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and critique claims of secret esoteric knowledge by applying the principles of scriptural consistency and logical contradiction, as demonstrated by Ibn Taymiyya.

Core Idea
False secret knowledge claims collapse when tested against clear revelation.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya recounts his confrontation with a group that claimed their shaykh was the Messiah and possessed hidden secrets. Instead of debating esoteric theories, he used a simple but powerful tool: swearing oaths and challenging them to mutual cursing (mubahala). This lesson extracts the logical method behind his approach: any claim that contradicts the clear, established doctrines of Islam must be rejected as false, regardless of its packaging as 'secret knowledge'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Claim
Find the exact assertion being made by the group. Here, they claimed their shaykh was the Messiah who would unite the three religions and that this was part of hidden secrets.
Ensure you have the claim precisely, not paraphrased.
2
Compare with Core Doctrine
State the relevant Islamic teaching that this claim touches upon. For the Messiah, Islamic eschatology teaches that Isa (Jesus) will descend, not a living shaykh. Also, the finality of prophethood is clear.
Use widely accepted sources like Quran and authentic hadith.
3
Identify Contradiction
Determine if the claim directly opposes the clear teaching. If it does, the claim is false regardless of any esoteric justification. Ibn Taymiyya swore it was a 'fabricated lie'.
Contradiction must be unambiguous; ambiguous texts require interpretation, not automatic rejection.
4
Apply Refutation Method
Once contradiction is clear, reject the claim publicly. Ibn Taymiyya did not engage in lengthy debate; he swore oaths and challenged to mubahala, a powerful tool that forces the claimant to prove their truthfulness under divine curse.
Mubahala is a last resort; its use here shows the severity of the falsehood.
5
Assess Epistemological Basis
Recognize that the claim relies on 'hidden secrets' inaccessible to normal verification. Ibn Taymiyya's method shows that such claims cannot override clear revealed knowledge. This is a key principle in Islamic epistemology.
Hidden knowledge claims are often used to avoid scrutiny; they must be tested against the revealed sources.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد رأيتُ من أتباع هؤلاء طوائف يدعون أن هذه الأمور من الأسرار المخزونة والعلوم المَصُونة، وخاطبتُ في ذلك طوائف منهم، وكنتُ أحلفُ لهم أن هذا كذب مفترى وأنه لا يجري من هذه الأمور شيء، وطلبتُ مباهلة بعضهم لأن ذلك كان متعلقا بأصول الدين، وكانوا من الاتحادية الذين يطول وصف دعاويهم. فإن شيخهم الذي هو عارفُ وقته وزاهده عندهم، كانوا يزعمون أنه هو المسيح الذي يَنْزِل، وأن معنى ذلك نزول روحانية عيسى عليه السلام عليه، وأن أمه أسمها مريم، وأنه يقوم بجمع الملل الثلاث، وأنه يظهر مظهرًا أكمل من مظهر محمد وغيره من المرسلين. ولهم مقالات من أعظم المنكرات يطولُ ذِكرُها ووصفها.
And I have seen followers of these people, groups claiming that these matters are from the hidden secrets and protected sciences, and I addressed groups among them, and I used to swear to them that this is a fabricated lie and that nothing of these matters occurs, and I requested a mutual cursing from some of them because that was related to the foundations of the religion, and they were from the unionists whose claims are lengthy to describe. For their shaykh who was the knower of his time and their ascetic, they claimed that he is the Messiah who will descend, and that the meaning of that is the descent of the spirituality of Jesus upon him, and that his mother's name is Mary, and that he will gather the three religions, and that he will appear in a more complete manifestation than that of Muhammad and other messengers. And they have statements from the greatest objectionable things, whose mention and description are lengthy.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya narrates a personal encounter with a group he calls 'al-Ittihadiyya' (unionists, referring to followers of Ibn 'Arabi's doctrine of wahdat al-wujud). He emphasizes that he swore oaths to them that their claims were lies and challenged them to mubahala (invoking God's curse on the liar). He then details the extreme claims they made about their shaykh: that he is the Messiah who will descend, that he will unify the three religions (Islam, Christianity, Judaism), and that his station is even higher than that of Prophet Muhammad. This serves as an example of the dangerous consequences of abandoning sound scriptural interpretation.

المباهلة

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya demonstrates that claims of secret esoteric knowledge must be rejected if they contradict the clear and established doctrines of Islam, using oaths and mubahala as a final arbiter.

Critique of Using Fabricated and Ambiguous Hadith as Proof
Author criticizes these groups for relying on weak or forged hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two types of weak hadith (mawdu' and mujmal) that Ibn Taymiyya criticizes as evidence in theological arguments.

Core Idea
Fabricated and ambiguous hadith cannot be used as evidence in Islamic theology.

Ibn Taymiyya exposes a critical methodological flaw in the arguments of certain theological groups: they rely on fabricated (mawdu') or ambiguous (mujmal) hadith without proper understanding. This lesson examines his critique and its implications for hadith-based reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of hadith criticized
Read the passage and find the two terms: 'mawdu' (fabricated) and 'mujmal' (ambiguous). These are the types of hadith Ibn Taymiyya accuses the groups of using.
Mawdu' means a hadith that is forged and not from the Prophet. Mujmal means a hadith that is unclear in meaning.
2
Understand the problem with each category
For mawdu': such hadith are not authentic and cannot be attributed to the Prophet. For mujmal: even if the chain is sound, the meaning is ambiguous and requires proper interpretation, which the groups lack.
Ibn Taymiyya says they 'do not understand its meaning' and 'interpret it' arbitrarily.
3
Recognize the contrast with proper methodology
Ibn Taymiyya implies that the correct approach is that of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a, who rely on authentic (sahih) and clear (muhkam) texts, and interpret ambiguous ones according to sound principles.
This contrast is central to his critique.
4
Apply to a real example
Consider a fabricated hadith used by some groups to support a theological claim. Using the criteria of hadith criticism (sanad and matn), we can determine its weakness and reject it as evidence.
This demonstrates the practical importance of this critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن من عجيب الأمر أن هؤلاء المتكلمين المدعين لحقائق الأمور العلميّة والدينيَّة المخالفين للسنة والجماعة يحتج كل منهم بما يقعُ له من حديث موضوع أو مجمل لا يفهم معناه، وكلما وجد أثرًا فيه إجمال نزله
Then among the amazing things is that these speakers, the claimants to the realities of scientific and religious matters, the opponents of the Sunnah and the community, each of them argues with whatever occurs to him of a fabricated or ambiguous hadith whose meaning he does not understand, and whenever he finds a tradition containing ambiguity, he interprets it
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya identifies a key methodological error of the groups he is criticizing. They use fabricated (mawdu') or ambiguous (mujmal) hadith as evidence, without proper understanding. He contrasts them with the ahl al-sunna wa al-jama'a, who follow sound principles of hadith criticism and interpretation. This critique underscores the importance of relying on authentic and clear texts.

Key Terms

Ibn Taymiyya warns against using corrupted textual evidence in theological discourse; only authentic and clear texts should be used as proof.

?

Ibn Taymiyya's Argument for Ahl al-Sunnah's Epistemic Authority
Author critiques esotericists and asserts Ahl al-Sunnah are most knowledgeable and truthful.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that Ahl al-Sunnah are the most knowledgeable and truthful, and identify his criteria for epistemological authority based on proximity to prophethood.

Core Idea
True knowledge flows from prophethood; those closest to the Prophet are most truthful.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya defends Ahl al-Sunnah wal-Hadith against those who claim esoteric knowledge outside the Sunnah. He builds an argument based on the principle that prophethood is the source of all verification and knowledge. You will learn how he uses this principle to establish that Ahl al-Sunnah, as the preservers of the Prophet's words and deeds, are more knowledgeable than any claimant to hidden secrets.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
Find where the author states that Ahl al-Sunnah are most deserving of truth, knowledge, faith, and verification. Look for the rhetorical question at the end.
The claim contrasts Ahl al-Sunnah with those who oppose them.
2
Extract the foundational principle
Locate the sentence that begins 'فمن المعلوم أن العقل والدين يقتضيان...' This states that prophethood is more deserving of every verification and knowledge.
This principle is presented as undeniable to any believer.
3
Trace the reasoning from principle to conclusion
The author argues that if prophethood is the source, then the most knowledgeable are those most specific to the Prophet: his companions, those who know his sayings, actions, etc.
He then identifies these as Ahl al-Sunnah wal-Hadith.
4
Analyze the polemical contrast
Notice how the author contrasts Ahl al-Sunnah with esotericists who claim exclusive secrets but 'exited from the Sunnah and the community.'
The contrast strengthens the argument by showing the alternative is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يروى عن علي بن الحسين أنه قال: يارُبَّ جَوْهِرِ عِلْمٍ لو أبوحُ به لقيل لي: أنت ممن يعبد الوثنـا ولاسْتَحَلَّ رجال مسلمون دَمِي يَرَوْنَ أَقبحَ مــا يَأْتُونَه حَسَنا فإذا كانت هذه طرق هؤلاء الذين يدعون من التحقيق وعلوم الأسرار ما خَرَجوا به عن السنة والجماعة، وزعموا أن تلك العلوم الدينية أو الكونية مختصة بهم فآمنوا بمجملها ومتشابهها ، وأنهم منحـوا مـن حقائق العبادات وخالص الديانات ما لم يُمْنَح الصَّدرُ الأول حفاظ الإسلام وبدورُ المِلة، ولم يتجرؤوا عليها برد وتكذيب، مع ظهور الباطل فيها تارة وخفائه أخرى فمن المعلوم أن العقل والدين يقتضيان أن جانب النبوة والرسالة أحق بكل تحقيق وعلم ومعرفة وإحاطة بأسرار الأمور وبواطنهـا، هذا لا ينازع فيه مؤمن، ونحن الآن في مخاطبة من فيه إيمان. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك فأعلم الناس بذلك أخصهم بالرسول وأعلمهم بأقواله وأفعاله، وحركاته وسكناته ، ومَدْخَله ومَخْرَجه، وباطنه وظاهره، وأعلمهم بأصحابه وسيرته وأيامه، وأعظمهم بحثًا عن ذلك وعن نَقَلَتِه، وأعظمهم تدينا به واتباعا له واقتفاء به. وهؤلاء هم أهل السنة والحديث، حفظا له، ومعرفة بصحيحه وسقيمه، وفقها فيه، وفهما يؤتيه الله إياهم في معانيه، وإيمانا وتصديقا، وطاعة وانقيادا، واقتداءً واتباعًا، مع ما يقترن بذلك من قوة عقلهم وقياسهم و تمييزهم، وعظيم مكاشفاتهم و مخاطباتهم، فإنهم أسد الناس نظرًا وقياسًا ورايا، وأصدق الناس رؤيا وكشفًا. أفلا يعلم من له أدنى عقل ودين أن هؤلاء أحقُّ بالصدق والعلم والإيمان والتحقيق ممن يخالفهم، وأن عندهم من العلوم ما ينكرها الجاهل والمبتدع، والذي عندهم هو الحق المبين، وأن الجاهل بأمرهم والمخالف لهم هو الذي معه من الحَشو ما معه من الضلال؟! وهذا باب يطول شرحه؛ فإن النفوسَ لها من الأقوال والأفعال ما لا يحصره إلا ذو الجلال.
It is narrated from Ali ibn al-Husayn that he said: 'How many a jewel of knowledge if I were to disclose it, it would be said to me: You are among those who worship idols, and Muslim men would consider my blood permissible, seeing the ugliest of what they do as beautiful.' So if these are the ways of those who claim from verification and sciences of secrets that which they exited from the Sunnah and the community, and they claim that those religious or cosmic sciences are exclusive to them, so they believe in their generalities and ambiguities, and that they have been granted from the realities of worship and pure religions what the first generation, the preservers of Islam and the full moons of the nation, were not granted, and they did not dare to reject and falsify them, despite the appearance of falsehood in them at times and its concealment at others—then it is known that reason and religion require that the side of prophethood and messengership is more deserving of every verification, knowledge, cognition, and encompassing of the secrets of affairs and their internals. This no believer disputes, and we are now addressing whoever has faith. And if the matter is so, then the most knowledgeable of people of that are the most specific to the Messenger and the most knowledgeable of his sayings, actions, movements, stillnesses, his entrance and exit, his internal and external, and the most knowledgeable of his companions, his biography, and his days, and the greatest in research about that and about its transmitters, and the greatest in religion through him, following him, and emulating him. And these are the people of the Sunnah and Hadith: preserving it, knowing its authentic and weak, jurisprudence in it, understanding that Allah gives them in its meanings, faith and affirmation, obedience and submission, emulation and following, along with what accompanies that of the strength of their intellect, their analogical reasoning, their discernment, and the greatness of their unveilings and communications. For they are the most penetrating of people in consideration, analogical reasoning, and opinion, and the most truthful of people in visions and unveiling. Does not he who has the least intellect and religion know that these are more deserving of truth, knowledge, faith, and verification than those who oppose them, and that they possess sciences that the ignorant and innovator deny, and that what they have is the manifest truth, and that the ignorant of their affair and the opponent of them is the one who has of the rubbish what he has of misguidance? This is a chapter whose explanation would be lengthy, for souls have of sayings and actions that none can enumerate except the Possessor of Majesty.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage is a polemical defense of Ahl al-Sunnah wal-Hadith against those who claim esoteric knowledge and deviate from the Sunnah. The author begins by quoting a poetic line about concealing a jewel of knowledge, implying that the esotericists hide their true doctrines. He then criticizes those who claim exclusive access to religious and cosmic secrets, asserting that they have abandoned the Sunnah and the community. The author argues that reason and religion dictate that prophethood is the true source of all verification and knowledge, and that no believer disputes this. Consequently, the most knowledgeable people are those closest to the Prophet—his companions and the early generations. These are the Ahl al-Sunnah, who preserve the Hadith, distinguish authentic from weak, and possess deep understanding. They combine reason and analogical reasoning with spiritual unveilings. The author rhetorically asks whether anyone with intellect can doubt that these people are more truthful and knowledgeable than their opponents. He concludes that the topic is vast, as human sayings and actions are countless.

Key Terms
أهل السنة والحديث
تحقيق
أسرار
مكاشفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, who are the most knowledgeable of the Prophet's teachings?
A) The philosophers and logicians
B) The esotericists and Sufis
C) The Ahl al-Sunnah wal-Hadith
D) The Mu'tazila

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya argues that true knowledge is rooted in proximity to prophethood, not esoteric claims.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

State in one sentence the key principle Ibn Taymiyya uses to argue that Ahl al-Sunnah are the most knowledgeable.
?
Imagine someone today claims to have esoteric knowledge that contradicts a well-established hadith. How would Ibn Taymiyya respond using the argument from this passage? Write a short response.

The Polemical Label 'Hashwiyya'
Author explains the polemical origins of the term 'Hashwiyya' and its use against Ahl al-Hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the polemical function of the term 'Hashwiyya' in Islamic intellectual history and explain why Ibn Taymiyya considers it a sign of heresy.

Core Idea
The label 'Hashwiyya' reveals more about the accuser than the accused.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya tackles a term used to marginalize Ahl al-Hadith. He shows that the label 'Hashwiyya' is not a neutral description but a weapon wielded by heretical groups against those who adhere to the revealed texts. Understanding this helps us see how labels can distort intellectual discourse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the groups that use the label 'Hashwiyya'
Note the list: Qaramita, philosophers, Mu'tazila, and even some later theologians like Abu al-Ma'ali and Abu Hamid.
2
Determine the common thread among the accused
What do all those called 'Hashwiyya' share? They affirm the texts and follow them in matters of belief.
3
Examine Ibn Taymiyya's source
He cites Abu Hatim that the sign of heretics is labeling Ahl al-Hadith as 'Hashwiyya'. Why does he choose this authority?
4
Contrast the label with undisputed terms
Ibn Taymiyya shifts to neutral terms like 'affirmation' and 'negation' to reframe the debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالصَّلوات، وأهل الذكر والدعوات، وحجَّاجُ البيت العتيق، والمجاهدون في سبيل الله، وأهل الصدق والأمانة وكل خير في العالم. فقد تبين لك أنهم أحقُّ بوجوه (۱) الذَّم، وأن هؤلاء أبعد عنها، وأن الواجب على الخلق أن يرجعوا إليهم فيما أختصَّهم الله به من الوِراثة النبوية التي لا توجد إلا عندهم. وأيضًا (۲)، فينبغي النظر في الموسومين بهذا الاسم، وفي الواسمين لهم به، أيهما أحق؟ وقد عُلِمَ أن هذا الاسم مما أشتهر عن النفاة ممن هم مَظنَّةُ الزندقة، كما ذكر العلماء ـ كأبي حاتم وغيره ـ أن علامة الزنادقة تسميتهم لأهـل الحديث: حشوية (۳). ونحن نتكلم بالأسماء التي لا نزاع فيها، مثل لفظ الإثبات والنفي. فنقول: من المعلوم أن هذا من تلقيب بعض الناس لأهل الحديث الذين يُقِرُّونه على ظاهره، فكلُّ من كان عنـه أبـعـد كـان أعظم ذمـا بذلك، كالقرامطة، ثم الفلاسفة، ثم المعتزلة، وهم يذمون بذلك المتكلمة الصفاتية من الكلابية والكرامية والأشعرية والفقهاء والصوفية وغيرهم، فكلُّ من أتبع النصوص وأقرها سموه بذلك. ومن قال بالصفات العقلية مثل: العلم والقدرة، دون الخبرية ونحو ذلك، يسمّى مُثبتة الصفات الخبرية حشوية، كما يفعل أبو المعالي وأبو حامد (۱) ونحوهما. ولطريقة أبي المعالي كان أبو محمَّدٍ (۲) يَتْبَعُ في فقهه وكلامه، لكن أبو محمد كان أعلم بالحديث وأتبع له من أبي المعالي وبمذاهب الفقهاء، وأبو المعالي أكثرُ أتباعًا للكلام، وهما في العربية متقاربان. وهؤلاء يعيبون منازعَهم إما لجَمْعِه حَشْوَ الحديث من غير تمييز بين صحيحه وضعيفه، أو لكون أتباع الحديث في مسائل الأصول من مذهب الحشو لأنها مسائل علمية والحديثُ لا يفيد ذلك، أو لأن أتباع النصوص مطلقا في المباحث الأصولية الكلامية حشو لأن النصوص لا تفي بذلك.
With prayers, and the people of remembrance and supplications, and the pilgrims of the ancient House, and the mujahideen in the way of Allah, and the people of truth and trustworthiness, and every good in the world. Thus it has become clear to you that they are more deserving of aspects (1) of blame, and that these are further from it, and that the duty upon creation is to refer to them in what Allah has specially entrusted them with of prophetic inheritance which is found only with them. And also (2), one should consider those labeled with this name, and those who label them with it, which of the two is more deserving? And it is known that this name is among what is famous from the deniers who are suspected of heresy, as the scholars mentioned — like Abu Hatim and others — that the sign of the heretics is their labeling of the people of hadith as 'Hashwiyya' (3). And we speak of names about which there is no dispute, such as the words 'affirmation' and 'negation'. So we say: It is known that this is from the labeling of some people for the people of hadith who affirm it according to its apparent meaning; so everyone who is farther from it is more severely blamed by that, like the Qaramita, then the philosophers, then the Mu'tazila, and they blame by that the speculative theologians who affirm attributes from the Kullabiyya, Karramiyya, Ash'ariyya, jurists, Sufis and others; so everyone who follows the texts and affirms them, they call them by that. And whoever affirms the rational attributes like knowledge and power, without the scriptural attributes and the like, calls the affirmers of scriptural attributes 'Hashwiyya', as does Abu al-Ma'ali and Abu Hamid (1) and their likes. And in the way of Abu al-Ma'ali, Abu Muhammad (2) used to follow in his fiqh and his speech, but Abu Muhammad was more knowledgeable of hadith and more adherent to it than Abu al-Ma'ali, and more knowledgeable of the schools of the jurists, while Abu al-Ma'ali was more of a follower of kalam, and the two were close in Arabic. And these criticize their opponents either for collecting the 'hashw' (fodder) of hadith without distinguishing between its authentic and weak, or for considering the followers of hadith in matters of usul to be of the madhhab of hashw because they are scientific matters and hadith does not provide that, or for considering following the texts absolutely in theological foundational investigations to be hashw because the texts do not suffice for that.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of the pejorative term 'Hashwiyya' as applied to Ahl al-Hadith. The author begins by establishing the virtue of those who adhere to the prophetic inheritance (al-wiratha al-nabawiyya), implying that the label is unjustly applied. He then sets up a comparison between those labeled and those labeling, asking which is more deserving of blame. The author notes that the term 'Hashwiyya' is famously used by deniers (al-nufat) who are suspected of heresy (zandaqa), citing Abu Hatim's statement that the sign of heretics is their labeling of Ahl al-Hadith as 'Hashwiyya'. The author then focuses on names that are undisputed, like 'affirmation' (ithbat) and 'negation' (nafy), implying that the dispute is about the label itself. He explains that the label is used by groups increasingly distant from the truth: the Qaramita, then philosophers, then Mu'tazila, and they apply it to anyone who follows the texts (nusus) and affirms them, including various theological schools (Kullabiyya, Karramiyya, Ash'ariyya) and others. Notably, even those who affirm only rational attributes (like knowledge and power) but deny scriptural attributes (like the hand or face) call those who affirm the latter 'Hashwiyya', as does Abu al-Ma'ali al-Juwayni and Abu Hamid al-Ghazali. The author then compares Abu al-Ma'ali with Abu Muhammad (al-'Izz ibn 'Abd al-Salam), noting that while the former followed kalam more, the latter was more knowledgeable of hadith and the schools of fiqh. Finally, the author lists three specific criticisms that these detractors level against their opponents: (1) they collect hadith indiscriminately without distinguishing authentic from weak; (2) they follow hadith in usul matters, which are scientific and not provided by hadith; (3) they follow texts absolutely in theological investigations, claiming that texts are insufficient. In each case, the author reframes the critique as a reflection of the detractors' own inadequacies.

Key Terms
حشوية
النصوص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which group does NOT use the label 'Hashwiyya' against Ahl al-Hadith?
A) Qaramita
B) Philosophers
C) Mu'tazila
D) Sufis

Key Takeaway
The label 'Hashwiyya' is a rhetorical weapon used by heretical groups to dismiss those who adhere to the revealed texts, and it says more about the accuser than the accused.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify a modern or historical example where a group is dismissed with a derogatory label (e.g., 'Ghulat', 'Mushabbiha'). Analyse whether the label reflects actual doctrinal error or merely a strategy of marginalization, following Ibn Taymiyya's method.

The Three Criticisms of Ahl al-Hadith
Author lists three specific criticisms used to justify the 'Hashwiyya' label.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three main criticisms leveled against Ahl al-Hadith by their detractors and assess Ibn Taymiyya's implicit refutation.

Core Idea
The three criticisms of Ahl al-Hadith actually expose the weaknesses of their critics.

After identifying who uses the label 'Hashwiyya', Ibn Taymiyya lists the specific charges brought against the followers of hadith. These criticisms—indiscriminate collection of hadith, reliance on hadith in matters of usul, and following texts in theological investigations—are presented as the reasons why the label is applied. Ibn Taymiyya's response is to imply that these very practices are strengths, not weaknesses.

Guided Path
1
List the three criticisms explicitly mentioned
1) collecting hadith without distinguishing authentic from weak; 2) following hadith in usul matters; 3) following texts absolutely in theological investigations.
2
For each criticism, identify the assumption behind it
The critics assume hadith needs rational critique, usul is purely rational, and texts are insufficient for theology.
3
Consider Ibn Taymiyya's implicit response
He has already established that Ahl al-Hadith are inheritors of prophecy; thus their reliance on hadith is a virtue.
4
Evaluate who benefits from the label
The critics use these charges to justify their own rationalist approaches.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالصَّلوات، وأهل الذكر والدعوات، وحجَّاجُ البيت العتيق، والمجاهدون في سبيل الله، وأهل الصدق والأمانة وكل خير في العالم. فقد تبين لك أنهم أحقُّ بوجوه (۱) الذَّم، وأن هؤلاء أبعد عنها، وأن الواجب على الخلق أن يرجعوا إليهم فيما أختصَّهم الله به من الوِراثة النبوية التي لا توجد إلا عندهم. وأيضًا (۲)، فينبغي النظر في الموسومين بهذا الاسم، وفي الواسمين لهم به، أيهما أحق؟ وقد عُلِمَ أن هذا الاسم مما أشتهر عن النفاة ممن هم مَظنَّةُ الزندقة، كما ذكر العلماء ـ كأبي حاتم وغيره ـ أن علامة الزنادقة تسميتهم لأهـل الحديث: حشوية (۳). ونحن نتكلم بالأسماء التي لا نزاع فيها، مثل لفظ الإثبات والنفي. فنقول: من المعلوم أن هذا من تلقيب بعض الناس لأهل الحديث الذين يُقِرُّونه على ظاهره، فكلُّ من كان عنـه أبـعـد كـان أعظم ذمـا بذلك، كالقرامطة، ثم الفلاسفة، ثم المعتزلة، وهم يذمون بذلك المتكلمة الصفاتية من الكلابية والكرامية والأشعرية والفقهاء والصوفية وغيرهم، فكلُّ من أتبع النصوص وأقرها سموه بذلك. ومن قال بالصفات العقلية مثل: العلم والقدرة، دون الخبرية ونحو ذلك، يسمّى مُثبتة الصفات الخبرية حشوية، كما يفعل أبو المعالي وأبو حامد (۱) ونحوهما. ولطريقة أبي المعالي كان أبو محمَّدٍ (۲) يَتْبَعُ في فقهه وكلامه، لكن أبو محمد كان أعلم بالحديث وأتبع له من أبي المعالي وبمذاهب الفقهاء، وأبو المعالي أكثرُ أتباعًا للكلام، وهما في العربية متقاربان. وهؤلاء يعيبون منازعَهم إما لجَمْعِه حَشْوَ الحديث من غير تمييز بين صحيحه وضعيفه، أو لكون أتباع الحديث في مسائل الأصول من مذهب الحشو لأنها مسائل علمية والحديثُ لا يفيد ذلك، أو لأن أتباع النصوص مطلقا في المباحث الأصولية الكلامية حشو لأن النصوص لا تفي بذلك.
With prayers, and the people of remembrance and supplications, and the pilgrims of the ancient House, and the mujahideen in the way of Allah, and the people of truth and trustworthiness, and every good in the world. Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the second criticism leveled against followers of hadith?
A) They collect hadith without distinguishing authentic from weak.
B) They follow hadith in matters of usul, which are scientific and hadith does not suffice.

Key Takeaway
The three criticisms of Ahl al-Hadith—indiscriminate collection, reliance on hadith in usul, and absolute textualism—reveal the rationalist assumptions of their critics.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Two Doubts That Undermine Prophethood
Ibn Taymiyya exposes the epistemic skepticism that undermines prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two types of epistemic doubt (isnad and matn) that Ibn Taymiyya identifies as the foundation of attacks on prophetic knowledge, and explain how they empty prophethood of authority.

Core Idea
Skepticism about prophetic texts attacks prophethood itself.

Ibn Taymiyya here dissects the polemical label 'Hashwiyya' but quickly reveals that the real enemy is deeper: an epistemological skepticism that denies any certain knowledge from the words of the Prophet. He shows how critics operate through two fundamental doubts—about the chain of transmission and about the meaning of the text—and argues that this strategy effectively empties prophethood of its essential function. This passage is a masterclass in identifying the logical structure of an attack on revelation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the label and its users
Find who uses the term 'Hashwiyya' and whom they target. Ibn Taymiyya lists the Qaramita, philosophers, Mu'tazila, and even some theologians who attack those who follow the apparent meaning of texts.
Note that the label is applied broadly to anyone who affirms transmitted attributes or follows hadith in theological matters.
2
Distinguish the three criticisms
Ibn Taymiyya groups the critics' objections into three: (1) indiscriminate collection of hadith without distinguishing sound from weak; (2) the claim that hadith cannot provide knowledge in intellectual matters; (3) the general view that following texts in speculative theology is 'hashw' (filler).
These criticisms range from methodological to epistemological.
3
Identify the two fundamental doubts
Ibn Taymiyya reduces all criticisms to two core doubts: doubt in the chain of transmission (isnad) and doubt in the textual meaning (matn). The first questions whether the Prophet actually said it; the second questions whether we can be certain of the intended meaning.
These two doubts are the 'two spears' that skeptics use to ward off prophetic arguments.
4
Analyze Ibn Taymiyya's conclusion
Ibn Taymiyya declares that this skepticism is an attack on prophethood itself. If neither the words nor the meanings of the prophets can be known, then the messenger becomes a mere figurehead—acknowledged in name but lacking real authority.
He compares this to a caliph who has the formal symbols of power but no actual command.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول: من المعلوم أن هذا من تلقيب بعض الناس لأهل الحديث الذين يُقِرُّونه على ظاهره، فكلُّ من كان عنه أبعد كان أعظم ذما بذلك، كالقرامطة، ثم الفلاسفة، ثم المعتزلة، وهم يذمون بذلك المتكلمة الصفاتية من الكلابية والكرامية والأشعرية والفقهاء والصوفية وغيرهم، فكلُّ من أتبع النصوص وأقرها سموه بذلك. ومن قال بالصفات العقلية مثل: العلم والقدرة، دون الخبرية ونحو ذلك، يسمّى مُثبتة الصفات الخبرية حشوية، كما يفعل أبو المعالي وأبو حامد ونحوهما. ولطريقة أبي المعالي كان أبو محمد يتبع في فقهه وكلامه، لكن أبو محمد كان أعلم بالحديث وأتبع له من أبي المعالي وبمذاهب الفقهاء، وأبو المعالي أكثر أتباعا للكلام، وهما في العربية متقاربان. وهؤلاء يعيبون منازعهم إما لجمعهم حشو الحديث من غير تمييز بين صحيحه وضعيفه، أو لكون أتباع الحديث في مسائل الأصول من مذهب الحشو لأنها مسائل علمية والحديث لا يفيد ذلك، أو لأن أتباع النصوص مطلقا في المباحث الأصولية الكلامية حشو لأن النصوص لا تفي بذلك. فالأمر راجع إلى أحد أمرين: إما ريب في الإسناد أو في المتن إما لأنهم يضيفون إلى الرسول ما لم يعلم أنه قاله، كأخبار الآحاد، ويجعلون مقتضاها العلم، وإما لأنهم يجعلون ما فهموه من اللفظ معلوما وليس هو بمعلوم؛ لما في الأدلة اللفظية من الاحتمال. ولا ريب أن هذا عمدة كل زنديق ومنافق يبطل العلم بما بعث الله به رسله، تارة يقول: لا نعلم أنهم قالوا ذلك، وتارة يقول: لا نعلم ما أرادوا بهذا القول. ومتى انتفى العلم بقولهم أو بمعناه لم يستفد من جهتهم علم، فيتمكن بعد ذلك أن يقول ما يقول من المقالات وقد أمن على نفسه أن يعارض بآثار الأنبياء؛ لأنه قد وكل ثغرها بذينك الرمحين الدافعين لجنود الرسل عنه، الطاعنين لمن احتج بها. وهذا القدر بعينه عين الطعن في نفس النبوة، بل يقر بتعظيمهم وكمالهم إقرار من لا يتلقى من جهتهم علم، فيكون الرسول عنده بمنزلة خليفة يعطى السكة والخطبة رسما ولفظا، كتابة وقولا، من غير أن يكون له أمر أو نهي مطاع، فله صورة الإمامة بما جعل له من السكة والخطبة وليس له حقيقتها. وهذا القدر وإن استجازه كثير من الملوك لعجز بعض الخلفاء عن القيام بواجبات الإمارة من الجهاد والسياسة، كما يفعل ذلك كثير من نواب الولاة لضعف مستنيبه وعجزه، فيتركب من تقدم ذي المنصب والبيت
We say: It is known that this is a label given by some people to the people of hadith who affirm it in its apparent meaning. Everyone who is more distant from it is more severely blamed by that, like the Qaramita, then the philosophers, then the Mu'tazila. They blame by that the speculative theologians who affirm attributes, from the Kullabiyya, Karamiyya, Ash'ariyya, jurists, Sufis, and others. So everyone who follows the texts and affirms them, they call them by that. And whoever affirms the intellectual attributes like knowledge and power but not the transmitted attributes and similar, they call those who affirm the transmitted attributes 'Hashwiyya,' as Abu al-Ma'ali and Abu Hamid and their like do. In the method of Abu al-Ma'ali, Abu Muhammad followed in his fiqh and kalam, but Abu Muhammad was more knowledgeable of hadith and more following of it than Abu al-Ma'ali, and more knowledgeable of the schools of the jurists, while Abu al-Ma'ali was more a follower of kalam, and they were close in Arabic. And these people criticize their opponents either for collecting the hashw (filler) of hadith without distinguishing between its authentic and weak, or because the followers of hadith in the foundational issues are of the hashw school because these are intellectual issues and hadith does not provide that, or because following texts absolutely in the speculative theological investigations is hashw because the texts do not suffice for that. So the matter reduces to one of two things: either doubt in the chain of transmission or in the text—either because they attribute to the Messenger what is not known that he said, like solitary reports, and they take their implication as certain knowledge, or because they take what they understood from the wording as known while it is not known, due to the probability in verbal arguments. And there is no doubt that this is the foundation of every heretic and hypocrite who nullifies knowledge of what Allah sent His messengers with. Sometimes he says: We do not know that they said that, and sometimes he says: We do not know what they meant by this statement. And when knowledge of their statements or their meaning is negated, no knowledge is obtained from them. Then after that he is able to say what he says of opinions, feeling safe from being opposed by the prophetic traditions because he has secured their gap with those two spears that repel the soldiers of the messengers from him, that wound those who argue by them. And this very thing is the very attack on prophethood itself. Rather, he acknowledges their greatness and perfection as an acknowledgment of one who does not receive knowledge from them. So the messenger is in his view like a caliph who is given the minting of coins and the sermon as a formality and a word, writing and speech, without having any command or prohibition that is obeyed. So he has the form of imamate by what was given him of coin-minting and sermon, but not its reality. And this matter, even if many kings have permitted it due to the inability of some caliphs to fulfill the duties of leadership in jihad and politics, as many deputies of governors do due to the weakness and incapacity of their appointer, it results from the precedence of the office holder and the house.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis by Ibn Taymiyya of the polemical label 'Hashwiyya' and the deeper epistemological skepticism that he identifies as the real root of attacks on prophethood. He begins by noting that the term 'Hashwiyya' is used by various groups (Qaramita, philosophers, Mu'tazila) to condemn those who follow the apparent meaning of texts, including the speculative theologians who affirm attributes (Kullabiyya, Karamiyya, Ash'ariyya), jurists, and Sufis. He then contrasts the methods of Abu al-Ma'ali (al-Juwayni) and Abu Muhammad (al-'Izz ibn 'Abd al-Salam), noting that while both follow a similar theological approach, Abu Muhammad was more grounded in hadith and fiqh. The critics of the hadith followers base their criticism on three grounds: (1) collecting hadith indiscriminately without distinguishing sound from weak; (2) the belief that hadith cannot provide knowledge in intellectual (theological) matters because it is not suited for that; (3) the general view that following texts in speculative theology is 'hashw' (filler) because texts are insufficient. Ibn Taymiyya then reduces these critiques to two fundamental doubts: doubt in the chain of transmission (isnad) and doubt in the textual meaning (matn). The first doubt arises from claiming that we do not know whether the Prophet actually said something, especially with solitary reports. The second doubt arises from claiming that we cannot be certain of the intended meaning due to linguistic ambiguity. He declares that this two-pronged skepticism is the mainstay of every heretic (zindiq) and hypocrite (munafiq) who seeks to nullify knowledge from divine revelation. By this method, they first deny certainty regarding the prophetic statements or their meanings, thereby cutting off any knowledge derived from the prophets. Once that is achieved, they can freely promote their own views without fear of being refuted by prophetic traditions, having 'secured the gap' with these two spears. Ibn Taymiyya then delivers his strongest critique: this approach is nothing less than an attack on prophethood itself. It reduces the messenger to a figurehead—like a caliph who formally has the coin struck in his name and the sermon delivered for him, but who wields no actual authority or command. The messenger is acknowledged in name and form, but his sayings carry no real epistemic weight. Ibn Taymiyya analogizes this to weak rulers who retain the title but not the power. Thus, the passage exposes the logical consequence of rejecting the epistemic value of prophetic texts: it empties prophethood of its essential function as a source of knowledge and guidance.

Key Terms
حشو
حشوية
إسناد
متن
زنديق

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what are the two types of doubt that skeptics use to undermine the epistemic value of prophetic traditions?
A) Doubt in the narrators’ reliability and doubt in the hadith's legal ruling
B) Doubt in the chain of transmission (isnad) and doubt in the textual meaning (matn)
C) Doubt in the Prophet's infallibility and doubt in the Quran's preservation
D) Doubt in the authenticity of mutawatir hadith and doubt in the consensus of scholars

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya exposes that doubt in isnad and matn is the weapon of every heretic who seeks to nullify prophetic knowledge, effectively attacking prophethood itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
A skeptic says: 'We can never be sure that the Prophet actually said this hadith because it is a solitary report, and even if he did, the words are ambiguous.' Identify the two doubts this statement uses and explain how Ibn Taymiyya would respond.
?
Ibn Taymiyya compares a messenger without epistemic authority to a caliph who has the title but no power. Find a contemporary argument that similarly reduces religious texts to mere symbols and explain why this is, in his view, an attack on prophethood.

Ibn Taymiyya's Dilemma Against Philosophers on Prophetic Knowledge
Refutes philosophers' claim that prophets could not express truths, and defends Companions' privileged knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's dilemma argument against the philosophers' claim that prophets could not convey metaphysical truths, and evaluate its internal validity.

Core Idea
If knowledge is accessible, prophets could access it; if not, philosophers cannot claim it – a perfect dilemma.

Ibn Taymiyya targets a philosophical position that undermines the epistemic authority of prophethood. The philosophers argued that prophets could not fully express divine truths because the common people could not bear them, thus delegating deeper understanding to later thinkers. Ibn Taymiyya exposes the self-contradiction in this claim: if these truths are knowable, the prophets must have known and expressed them; if not, the philosophers have no foundation for their own knowledge. This lesson unpacks his logical deconstruction and its implications for Islamic epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the philosophers' claim
The philosophers assert that prophets were unable to clarify metaphysical truths openly because the masses could not handle them, so they left deeper meanings to later rational interpreters.
This is the position Ibn Taymiyya attacks.
2
Extract the conditional dilemma
Ibn Taymiyya formulates a conditional: if knowledge of these truths is possible for humans, then it was possible for the prophets (as primary recipients of revelation); if it is impossible, then the philosophers themselves have no access to it. This forces a contradiction with the philosophers' claim to possess such knowledge.
This is a classic dilemma: whichever horn is chosen, the philosophers' position collapses.
3
Analyze the writing argument
Ibn Taymiyya adds: if the prophets could not express truths in speech or writing, then the philosophers' own books would be impossible. But since they do write, the premise that expression is impossible is false.
This reinforces the dilemma from another angle.
4
Examine the elite/commoner distinction
The philosophers retreat to claiming that truths can only be conveyed to an elite. Ibn Taymiyya counters that the prophets also had an elite circle of Companions who fully grasped their inner teachings. He lists them as the true elite (khassah).
The philosophers' distinction does not give them exclusive access; the Companions had it directly.
5
Identify Ibn Taymiyya's positive claim
He asserts that the people of hadith (traditionists) are the most knowledgeable and elite of the ummah, inheriting the Prophet's inner and outer knowledge through the Companions.
This shifts epistemic authority from philosophers to the prophetic tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوة نائبه صلاح الأمر ، أو فعل ذلك لهوى ورغبة في الرياسة لــه ولطائفته (۱) دون مَن هو أحقُّ بذلك منه وسلك مسلك المتغلبين بالعدوان= فمن المعلوم أن المؤمن بالله ورسوله لا يستجيز أن يقول في الرسالة: إنها عاجزة عن تحقيق العلم وبيانه حتى يكون الإقرار بها مع تحقيق العلم الإلهي من غيرها موجبا لصلاح الدين، ولا يستجيز أن يعتدي عليها بالتقدم بين يدي الله ورسوله ويقدّم علمه وقوله على علم الرسول وقوله، ولا يستجيز أن يسلّط عليها التأويلات العقلية ويدَّعي أن ذلك من كمال الدين وأن الدين لا يكون كاملا إلا بذلك. وأحسن أحواله أن يدعي أن الرسول [كان] عالما بأن ما أخبر به أن له تأويلات وتبيانًا غير ما يدلّ عليه ظاهرُ قوله ومفهومه، وأنه مـا تـرك ذلك إلا [لأنه ] (٢) ما كان يُمْكِنُه بين تلك الأعراب ونحوهم، وأنه وكل ذلك إلى عقول المتأخرين (۳). وهذا هو الواقع؛ فإن المتفلسفة تقول: إن الرُّسل لم يتمكنوا من بيان الحقائق؛ لأن إظهارها يُفْسِدُ الناس ولا تحتمل ذلك عقولهم. ثم قد :يقولون إنهم عرفوها، وقد يقول بعضهم: لم يعرفوها، أو أنا أعرَفُ بها منهم. ثم يبينونها هُم بالطرق القياسية الموجودة عندهم. ولم يَعْقِلوا أنه إن كان العلم بها ممكناً فهو ممكن لهم كما يدعون أنه ممكن لهم، وإلا فلا سبيل لهم إلى معرفتها بإقرارهم. وكذلك التعبير وبيان العلم بالخطاب والكتاب إن لم يكن ممكنا فـلا يُمْكِنُكم (۱) ذلك، وأنتم تتكلمون وتكتبون علمكم في الكتب، وإن كان ذلك ممكنا فلا يصح قولكم: «لم يُمْكِن الرُّسلَ ذلك». وإن قلتم : يمكنُ الخطابُ بها مع خاصَّة الناس دون عامتهم ـــ وهذا قولهم ، فمن المعلوم أن علم الرُّسل يكون عند خاصتهم كما يكون علمكم عند خاصتكم. ومن المعلوم أن كـل مـن كـان بكـلام المتبوع وأحوالـه وبـواطـن أمـوره وظواهرها أعلم، وهو بذلك أقوم كان أحق بالاختصاص به وعبد ولا ريب أن أهل الحديث أعلمُ الأمَّة وأخَصُّها بعلم الرسول وعلم خاصته (۲)، مثل: الخلفاء الراشدين، وسائر العشرة، ومثل: أُبي بن كعب، الله بن مسعود، ومعاذ بن جبل، وعبد الله بن سلام، وسلمان الفارسي، وأبي الدرداء، وعُبادة بن الصامت، وأبي ذر الغفاري، وعمار بن ياسر، وحذيفة بن اليمان. ومثل : سعد بن معاذ، وأُسَيْد بن حُضَيْر، وسعد بن عُبادة، وعَبَّاد بن بشر، وسالم مولى أبي حذيفة، وغير هؤلاء ممَّن كان أخص الناس بالرسول وأعلمهم بباطن أموره وأتبعهم لذلك.
And the power of his deputy is the rectification of affairs, or he does that out of desire and craving for leadership for himself and his party (1) instead of the one who is more deserving of it than him, and he follows the path of the overpowering aggressors. So it is known that the believer in Allah and His Messenger cannot permit himself to say about the message: that it is incapable of achieving knowledge and clarifying it, so that acknowledgment of it along with achieving divine knowledge from other than it would be a cause for the rectification of religion. Nor can he permit himself to transgress against it by advancing before Allah and His Messenger and giving priority to his own knowledge and statement over the knowledge and statement of the Messenger. Nor can he permit himself to subject it to rational interpretations and claim that this is part of the perfection of religion and that religion is not perfect except by that. The best of his states is that he claims that the Messenger was knowledgeable that what he informed about has interpretations and clarifications other than what is indicated by the apparent meaning and gist of his statement, and that he did not leave that except because it was not possible for him among those Bedouins and their like, and that he delegated that to the minds of later generations (3). And this is what actually happens; for the philosophers say that the messengers were not able to clarify the truths because revealing them would corrupt the people and their minds cannot bear that. Then sometimes they say that they (the messengers) knew them, and sometimes some of them say that they did not know them, or that I know them better than they do. Then they clarify them themselves by the syllogistic methods found with them. And they did not realize that if knowledge of them is possible, then it is possible for them as they claim it is possible for them, otherwise there is no way for them to know them by their own admission. Likewise, expression and clarification of knowledge by speech and writing: if it is not possible, then it is not possible for you, while you speak and write your knowledge in books; and if that is possible, then your statement 'the messengers could not do that' is invalid. And if you say: 'Speech about them is possible with the elite among people, not the commoners' — and this is their statement — then it is known that the knowledge of the messengers is with their elite just as your knowledge is with your elite. And it is known that whoever is more knowledgeable of the speech of the followed one, his states, his inner affairs and his outer aspects, and is more upright in that, is more deserving of being singled out for it. And there is no doubt that the people of hadith are the most knowledgeable of the ummah and the most elite of them in the knowledge of the Messenger and the knowledge of his elite (2), such as: the rightly guided caliphs, the rest of the ten, and like Ubayy ibn Ka'b, Abdullah ibn Mas'ud, Mu'adh ibn Jabal, Abdullah ibn Salam, Salman al-Farisi, Abu al-Darda', Ubadah ibn al-Samit, Abu Dharr al-Ghifari, Ammar ibn Yasir, Hudhayfah ibn al-Yaman, and like Sa'd ibn Mu'adh, Usayd ibn Hudayr, Sa'd ibn Ubadah, Abbad ibn Bishr, Salim the freed slave of Abu Hudhayfah, and others among those who were the most elite of the people with the Messenger and the most knowledgeable of his inner affairs and the most following of that.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation by Ibn Taymiyya of the philosophers' epistemological claims regarding prophethood. He begins by establishing that a true believer cannot accept the idea that the prophetic message is insufficient for achieving true knowledge or that rational interpretations are needed to perfect religion. He then describes the philosophers' position: they claim that prophets could not fully express metaphysical truths because the common people could not bear them, so the prophets left the deeper meanings to later thinkers. The philosophers then arrogate to themselves the role of explaining these truths through their syllogistic methods. Ibn Taymiyya points out the contradiction: if such knowledge is possible at all (as the philosophers imply by claiming to possess it), then it was possible for the prophets as well; if it is impossible, then the philosophers have no access to it either. He further argues that if the prophets could not express these truths verbally or in writing, then the philosophers' own written works would be impossible; but since they do write, their excuse collapses. The philosophers may retreat to claiming that such truths can only be conveyed to an elite, not the commoners. Ibn Taymiyya responds by noting that the prophets also had an elite circle of companions who fully grasped their inner teachings. He then lists the Companions who were most knowledgeable and close to the Prophet, including the rightly guided caliphs, the ten promised paradise, and other prominent sahaba, asserting that the people of hadith (the traditionists) are the true inheritors of this elite knowledge.

Key Terms
الرسالة
العلم
الخاصة
العامة
التأويلات العقلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya's argument, if the philosophers claim that knowledge of metaphysical truths is impossible for prophets to express to the common people, what follows about the philosophers' own knowledge?
A) The philosophers must have received this knowledge through divine revelation.
B) The philosophers' knowledge is either possible (so prophets could have had it) or impossible (so philosophers have no valid means to it).
C) The philosophers must admit that prophets were ignorant of these truths.
D) The philosophers should keep their knowledge secret from the common people.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's dilemma shows that the philosophers' claim about prophetic ignorance is internally inconsistent: if truth is knowable, prophets knew it; if not, philosophers cannot know it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a similar dilemma argument against someone who claims that the sunnah (prophetic traditions) are not reliable for deriving legal rulings. Your dilemma should show that either the sunnah is reliable or the alternative source (e.g., pure reason) cannot be considered authoritative.
?
List five Companions mentioned by Ibn Taymiyya and explain why he considers them the true elite (khassah) in terms of knowledge. Then argue why this refutes the philosophers' claim that only they possess esoteric truths.
