Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Philosophers and Theologians
Ibn Taymiyya contrasts the ahl al-hadith with philosophers and mutakallimun, highlighting the latter's ignorance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze Ibn Taymiyya's critique of those who venerate philosophy and kalam while being ignorant of Quran and Hadith, and identify the epistemological criteria he uses to judge their inadequacy.

Core Idea
Those who prioritize Greek logic over divine revelation are epistemologically bankrupt in Ibn Taymiyya's view.

Ibn Taymiyya is often portrayed as a critic of logic, but his deeper target is the substitution of prophetic knowledge with human speculation. In this passage from Naqd al-Mantiq, he contrasts the ahl al-hadith—defined by their commitment to the Quran and Sunnah—with those who venerate philosophy and kalam. He argues that the latter are so ignorant of the primary sources that they cannot even distinguish between a Quranic verse and a hadith. This lesson will walk you through his argument and its foundations.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the positive definition of ahl al-hadith
Identify how Ibn Taymiyya broadens the term to include all who preserve and follow the Prophet's teachings outwardly and inwardly. Note the emphasis on both knowledge and practice.
This definition is broader than the usual technical meaning.
2
Extract the contrast with philosophers and mutakallimun
Find the sentence where he states that those who venerate philosophy and kalam are farthest from knowledge of hadith. Notice the epistemic claim: this is 'palpable' (mahsus), not theoretical.
The contrast is presented as empirical observation.
3
Deconstruct the specific accusations
List the ignorance charges: they cannot distinguish between authentic and fabricated hadith, they do not know the Prophet's meanings, many do not know the Quran's meanings or even memorize it. How does Ibn Taymiyya support these claims?
4
Identify the underlying principle
Observe his general rule: the closer a group is to Allah and His Messenger, the greater their care for Quran and Hadith. This is the epistemological standard for all knowledge.
This principle ties knowledge of revelation to closeness to Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
روايته، بل نعني بهم : كلَّ من كان أحق بحفظه ومعرفته وفهمه ظاهرا وباطنا، واتباعه باطنا وظاهرًا، وكذلك أهل القرآن. وأدنى خصلة في هؤلاء محبةُ القرآن والحديث، والبحث عنهما وعن معانيهما، والعمل بما علموه من مُوجَبهما. ففقهاء الحديث أخبَرُ بالرسول من فقهاء غيره، وصوفيتُهم أتبعُ للرسول من صوفيَّة غيرهم، وأمراؤهم أحقُّ بالسياسة النبوية من غيرهم، وعامَّتُهم أحق بموالاة الرسول من غيرهم. ومن المعلوم أن المعظَّمين للفلسفة والكلام المعتقدين لمضمونهما أبعد عن معرفة الحديث وأتباعه من هؤلاء. هذا أمر محسوس، بل إذا كشفتَ أحوالهم وجدتهم من أجهل الناس بأقواله وأحوالـه وبــواطـن أموره وظاهرها، حتى تجد كثيرًا من العامة أعلم بذلك منهم. ولا يميزون بين ما قاله وما لم يَقُله، بل قد لا يفرقون بين حديث متواتر عنه وحديث مكذوب موضوع عليه، وإنما يعتمدون في موافقته عـلـى مـا يوافق قولهم سواء كان موضوعاً أو غير موضوع، فيَعْدِلُون إلى أحاديثَ يعلمُ خاصة الرسول بالضرورة اليقينية أنها مكذوبة عليه عن أحاديث يعلم خاصَّتُه بالضرورة اليقينية أنها قوله. ولا يعلمون مرادَه ، بل غالب هؤلاء لا يعلمون معاني القرآن فضلا عن الحديث، بل كثير منهم لا يحفظون القرآن أصلا ! فمَن لا يحفظ القرآنَ ولا يعرفُ معانيه، ولا يعرفُ الحديث ومعانيه، من أين يكون عارفًا بالحقائق المأخوذة عن الرسول؟! وإذا تدبر العاقل وَجَد الطوائف كلَّما كانت الطائفة إلى الله ورسوله أقرب كانت بالقرآن والحديث أعظم عناية، وإذا كانت عن الله وعن رسوله أبعد كانت عنهما أنأى، حتى تجد في أئمة علماء هؤلاء من لا يميز بين القرآن وغيره، بل ربَّما ذُكرت عنده آيةٌ فقال: لا نسلم صحة الحديث! وربَّما قال: لقوله عليه السلام كذا، وتكون آيةً من كتاب الله ! وقد بلغنـا مـن ذلـك عجائب، وما لم يبلغنا أكثر. وحدثني ثقةٌ أنه تولى مدرسة مَشْهَد الحسين بمصر بعض أئمة المتكلمين رجل يسمى شمس الدين الأصبهاني شيخ الأيكي
Rather, by them we mean everyone who is most deserving of preserving it (the Sunnah), knowing it, and understanding it outwardly and inwardly, and following it inwardly and outwardly, and likewise the people of the Qur'an. The least quality among these is love of the Qur'an and Hadith, researching them and their meanings, and acting according to what they know of their implications. So the jurists of Hadith are more knowledgeable about the Messenger than other jurists, their Sufis are more following of the Messenger than other Sufis, their rulers are more entitled to prophetic governance than others, and their common people are more deserving of the loyalty of the Messenger than others. It is known that those who venerate philosophy and dialectical theology, who believe in their content, are farther from knowledge of the Hadith and its followers than these. This is a palpable matter; rather, if you examine their conditions, you find them among the most ignorant of people regarding his sayings, his states, his inner and outer affairs, so much so that you find many common people more knowledgeable of that than them. They do not distinguish between what he said and what he did not say; indeed, they may not differentiate between a widely transmitted hadith from him and a fabricated lie ascribed to him. They only rely, in following him, on what agrees with their opinion, whether it is fabricated or not. So they turn away from hadiths that the special companions of the Messenger know with certain necessity are lies about him, to hadiths that his special companions know with certain necessity are his words. And they do not know his intended meaning; rather, most of these people do not know the meanings of the Qur'an, let alone the Hadith; indeed, many of them do not memorize the Qur'an at all! So one who does not memorize the Qur'an and does not know its meanings, and does not know the Hadith and its meanings, how can he be knowledgeable of the truths taken from the Messenger? If the intelligent person reflects, he finds that the closer a group is to Allah and His Messenger, the greater their care for the Qur'an and Hadith; and the farther they are from Allah and His Messenger, the more distant they are from them. So much so that you find among the leading scholars of these people one who cannot distinguish between the Qur'an and other things; indeed, perhaps a verse is mentioned in his presence and he says: 'We do not accept the authenticity of the hadith!' and perhaps he says: 'Because of his saying, peace be upon him, such and such,' and it is a verse from the Book of Allah! We have heard of wonders of that, and what has not reached us is more. And a trustworthy person told me that some leading theologian, a man called Shams al-Din al-Asfahani, the teacher of al-Ayki, took charge of the madrasa of the Mashhad al-Husayn in Egypt.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is a sharp critique by Ibn Taymiyya of those who venerate philosophy (falsafa) and speculative theology (kalam) while being ignorant of the Quran and Hadith. He begins by defining 'ahl al-hadith' broadly as those who are most deserving of preserving, understanding, and following the Prophet's teachings inwardly and outwardly, as well as the people of the Quran. The minimal characteristic of this group is love for the Quran and Hadith, researching their meanings, and acting upon their implications. Ibn Taymiyya then contrasts different categories: the jurists among the hadith scholars (fuqaha' al-hadith) are more knowledgeable about the Prophet than other jurists; their Sufis are more obedient to the Prophet than other Sufis; their rulers are more entitled to prophetic governance; and their common people are more deserving of the Prophet's loyalty. Next, he states that it is well-known that those who venerate philosophy and kalam are farthest from knowledge of hadith and its followers. This is not a hidden matter but palpable. If one examines their state, they are among the most ignorant of people regarding the Prophet's sayings, states, inner and outer affairs. Even many common people are more knowledgeable than them. They cannot distinguish between what the Prophet said and what he did not say, nor between an authentic mutawatir hadith and a fabricated one. They only accept what agrees with their own opinions, whether authentic or fabricated, preferring false hadiths that the Prophet's close companions know with certainty are lies. Moreover, they do not understand the intended meanings of the Prophet; most of them do not know the meanings of the Quran, let alone hadith. Many do not even memorize the Quran. Ibn Taymiyya poses a rhetorical question: how can someone who does not memorize or understand the Quran and hadith know the truths taken from the Prophet? He then presents a general principle: the closer a group is to Allah and His Messenger, the more they care for the Quran and hadith; the farther they are, the more distant. He gives an example: some leading scholars among these people cannot distinguish between the Quran and other texts; when a verse is mentioned, they might say 'we do not accept the authenticity of the hadith' when it is actually a Quranic verse, or attribute a saying to the Prophet that is actually from Allah. He ends with an anecdote about Shams al-Din al-Asfahani, a leading theologian who was appointed as head of a madrasa in Egypt, implying his high status despite his ignorance.

Key Terms
أهل الحديث
المعظَّمين للفلسفة والكلام

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya argues that genuine knowledge of Islamic truths is inseparable from direct engagement with the Quran and Sunnah, and that those who prioritize speculative reasoning over these sources are epistemically deficient.

The Logical Disjunction for Prophetic Knowledge
A logical argument defending prophetic knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct and evaluate a disjunctive argument that refutes the denial of prophetic knowledge.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya uses a logical disjunction to show that denying prophetic knowledge is self-refuting and heretical.

This passage from Ibn Taymiyya's Naqd al-Mantiq presents a powerful logical argument known as 'sabr wa taqsim' (division and exhaustion). By systematically breaking down the possibilities regarding prophetic knowledge, the author forces the opponent into an untenable position. This lesson will guide you through the logical steps and show how this method defends the perfection of prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main disjunction
The author begins with a fundamental either/or: either the messengers knew the true realities (haqa'iq) or they did not. This is the primary division.
The true realities encompass both beliefs (khabariyya) and actions (talabiyya).
2
Explore the consequences of knowing
If they knew, the next disjunction is: either they were able to explain these realities through speech and writing, or they were not able.
This second disjunction assumes prophetic capability.
3
Refine the audience
If they were able to explain, the third disjunction is: either it was possible for the common people (al-‘amma) to understand, or only for the elite (al-khassa).
This addresses the accessibility of prophetic knowledge.
4
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent claims: the messengers did not know the true realities, and philosophers and theologians (mutakallimun) are more knowledgeable and better at explaining them.
This claim contradicts the first branch of the main disjunction.
5
Evaluate the refutation
Ibn Taymiyya declares that this claim is without doubt the saying of heretics and hypocrites. Why? Because it denies the perfection of prophethood and implies divine guidance is inferior to human intellect.
The refutation uses the logical form of modus tollens: if the prophets did not know, then humans are superior (which is false), so they must have known.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل وتلخيص النكتة : أن الرُّسل إما أنهم عَلِموا الحقائق الخبرية والطلبية، أو لم يَعْلَمُوها. وإذا عَلِمُوها، فإما أنه كان يُمْكِنُهم بيانها بالكلام والكتاب أو لا يُمْكِنُهم ذلك. وإذا أمكنهم ذلك، فإما أن يُمْكِنَ للعامة أو للخاصة. فإن قال: إنهم لم يَعْلَمُوها ، وأن الفلاسفة والمتكلمين أعلم بها منهم، وأحسنُ بيانًا لها منهم = فلا ريب أن هذا قول الزنادقة المنافقين، وسنتكلم معهم بعد هذا ، إذ الخطاب هنا لبيان أن هذا قول الزنادقة، وأنه لا يقوله إلا منافق أو جاهل.
Chapter: And the summary of the point: The messengers either knew the true realities (al-haqa'iq al-khabariyya wa al-talabiyya) or they did not know them. If they knew them, then they were either able to explain them through speech and writing or not able. If they were able, then it was either possible for the common people or only for the elite. If someone says that they did not know them, and that the philosophers and theologians (mutakallimun) are more knowledgeable of them and better at explaining them, then there is no doubt that this is the saying of heretics and hypocrites. We will speak with them after this. For the discourse here is to clarify that this is the saying of the heretics, and that only a hypocrite or an ignorant person says it.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage presents a logical argument (talkhis al-nukta) concerning prophetic knowledge of true realities (haqa'iq). The author sets up a disjunction: either the messengers (rusul) knew the true realities of both informative and prescriptive matters (khabariyya and talabiyya) or they did not. If they knew, they could either explain them or not. If they could explain, it was either accessible to the general public or only to the elite. Having laid out all possibilities, the author then considers the claim that the messengers did not know these realities, and that philosophers and theologians (mutakallimun) are more knowledgeable and articulate. He declares such a claim without doubt to be the statement of heretics (zanadiqa) and hypocrites (munafiqun). This is because it inherently denies the perfection of prophethood and implies that divine guidance is inferior to human intellect. The author concludes by stating that this discourse is to establish that such a belief is heretical and uttered only by a hypocrite or an ignorant person. The logical structure multiplies the cost of denying prophetic knowledge: at each disjunction, the critic is forced to concede either that prophets were ignorant or that they were unable to communicate effectively, both of which contradict the purpose of prophethood.

Key Terms
الحقائق الخبرية والطلبية
الفلاسفة والمتكلمين
الزندقة
النفاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) It is the saying of heretics and hypocrites.
C) It is permissible if supported by evidence.
D) It is an exaggeration.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya demonstrates that denying prophetic knowledge logically leads to heretical conclusions, thereby affirming the perfection of prophethood through a disjunctive argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Construct a similar disjunctive argument to defend the completeness of the Quran's guidance. Start with: 'Either the Quran explains everything needed for guidance or it does not.' Then explore the consequences and refute the claim that it is incomplete.

Identify the modus tollens structure in this passage: state the conditional and the negation of the consequent.

Ibn Taymiyya's Dilemma on Prophetic Knowledge
Author presents a dilemma refuting the philosophical theory of prophecy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's dilemma against the philosophers' theory of prophecy and identify the two horns of the argument.

Core Idea
The philosophers' view makes prophets either ignorant or deceivers—both unacceptable.

This passage from Ibn Taymiyya's Naqd al-Mantiq refutes the claim that prophets used imaginative imagery to convey truths to the masses. He constructs a dilemma to show that the philosophers' position is self-defeating. Let's unpack the logical structure of this critique.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunction (taqsīm)
Ibn Taymiyya begins with an exhaustive division: The prophets either knew the truths (haqā'iq) or they did not. This is the first fork.
2
Follow the first horn: Prophets did not know
If prophets did not know the truths, then philosophers and theologians are more knowledgeable—a heretical conclusion (qawl al-zanādiqa).
Ibn Taymiyya dismisses this as hypocritical.
3
Follow the second horn: Prophets knew the truths
If prophets knew, then either they could explain them or not. If they could not, that is false. If they could, then either common people or elite could understand.
This sub-dilemma refines the second horn.
4
Expose the philosophers' claim
The philosophers (al-Farabi, Ibn Sina) take the position that prophets could explain but common people cannot understand, so they used imaginative representations (takhyeel). Their aim is public welfare, not truth conveyance.
Ibn Taymiyya records this view to critique it.
5
Assess the logical force
The dilemma forces the opponent into either heresy (first horn) or a theory that reduces prophecy to imagery (second horn). Ibn Taymiyya's critique is that the philosophers' choice still undermines the prophetic mission.
The critique is epistemological and theological.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل وتلخيص النكتة : أن الرُّسل إما أنهم عَلِموا الحقائق الخبرية والطلبية، أو لم يَعْلَمُوها. وإذا عَلِمُوها، فإما أنه كان يُمْكِنُهم بيانها بالكلام والكتاب أو لا يُمْكِنُهم ذلك. وإذا أمكنهم ذلك، فإما أن يُمْكِنَ للعامة أو للخاصة. فإن قال: إنهم لم يَعْلَمُوها ، وأن الفلاسفة والمتكلمين أعلم بها منهم، وأحسنُ بيانًا لها منهم = فلا ريب أن هذا قول الزنادقة المنافقين، وسنتكلم معهم بعد هذا، إذ الخطاب هنا لبيان أن هذا قول الزنادقة، وأنه لا يقوله إلا منافق أو جاهل. وإن قال: إن الرُّسلَ مقصودهم صلاح عموم الخلق، وعموم الخلق لا يُمْكِنُهم فهم هذه الحقائق الباطنة، فخاطبوهم بضرب الأمثال لينتفعوا بذلك، وأظهروا الحقائق العقلية في القوالب الحِسّية، فتضمن خطابهم عن الله وعن اليوم الآخر من التَّخييل والتمثيل للمعقول بصورة المحسوس ما ينتفع به عموم الناس في أمر الإيمان بالله وبالمعاد، وذلك يقرر في النفوس عظمة الله وعظمة اليوم الآخر ما يَحُضُّ النفوس على عبادة الله وعلى الرجاء والخوف، فينتفعون بذلك وينالون السعادة بحسب إمكانهم واستعدادهم، إذ هذا الذي فعلته الرُّسُلُ هو غاية الإمكان في كشف الحقائق لعموم النوع البشري، ومقصود الرُّسل حِفْظُ النَّوع البشري وإقامة مصلحة معاشه ومعاده = فمعلوم أن هذا قول حُذَاق الفلاسفة، مثل الفارابي وابن سينا وغيرهما، وهو قول كلّ حاذق وفاضل من المتكلمين في القدر الذي يخالف فيه أهل الحديث. فالفارابي يقول: «إن خاصة النبوَّة جودة تخييل الأمور المعقولة في الصُّوَر المحسوسة» أو نحو هذه العبارة. وابن سينا يذكرُ هذا المعنى في مواضع، ويقول: ما كان يمكنُ موسى بن عمران مع أولئك العبرانيين، ولا يمكنُ محمدا مع أولئك العرب الجفاة، أن يبينا لهم الحقائق على ما هي عليه فإنهم كانوا يعجزون عن فهم ذلك، وإن فَهِمُوه على ما هم عليه أنحلَّت عَزَماتُهم عن أتباعه؛ لأنهم لا يرون من العلم ما يقتضي العمل.
Section and summary of the point: That the prophets either knew the truths of information and demands, or they did not know them. And if they knew them, then either it was possible for them to explain them with speech and writing, or it was not possible for them to do so. And if it was possible for them, then either it was possible for the common people or for the elite. If one says: They did not know them, and that the philosophers and theologians are more knowledgeable of them than them, and better in explaining them than them, then there is no doubt that this is the saying of heretical hypocrites, and we will speak with them after this, for the discourse here is to show that this is the saying of heretics, and that none says it except a hypocrite or an ignorant person. And if one says: The prophets' aim is the reform of the general creation, and the general creation cannot understand these inner truths, so they addressed them by striking similitudes so that they might benefit thereby, and they manifested intellectual truths in sensory molds, so their speech about God and the Last Day included imaginative representation and likening of the intelligible to the form of the sensible, such that the general people benefit from it in the matter of faith in God and the Hereafter, and that establishes in souls the greatness of God and the greatness of the Last Day, which urges souls to worship God and to hope and fear, so they benefit thereby and attain happiness according to their capacity and preparedness. Since this which the prophets did is the utmost possibility in revealing truths to the general human species, and the prophets' aim is to preserve the human species and establish the welfare of their livelihood and afterlife — then it is known that this is the saying of the leading philosophers, such as al-Farabi and Ibn Sina and others, and it is the saying of every leading and virtuous person among the theologians to the extent that he contradicts the people of hadith. Al-Farabi says: 'The specific property of prophecy is the excellence of imagining intelligible matters in sensory forms' or something similar to this expression. And Ibn Sina mentions this meaning in places, and says: 'It was not possible for Musa ibn Imran with those Hebrews, nor was it possible for Muhammad with those rude Arabs, to explain to them the truths as they are, because they were incapable of understanding that, and if they understood them as they are, their determinations would weaken from following them, because they do not possess the knowledge that necessitates action.'
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation by Ibn Taymiyya of the philosophical view that prophets did not convey exact truths but only imaginative representations for the benefit of the common people. The author structures his refutation as a dilemma: either the prophets knew the truths or they did not. If they did not know them, then the philosophers and theologians would be more knowledgeable, which is a heretical position. If they knew them, could they explain them? If they could not, again that is false. If they could, then either it was possible for the common people or only for the elite. The philosophers claim that the common people cannot understand the truths, so prophets used similitudes and sensory forms. Ibn Taymiyya then presents the philosophical position as articulated by al-Farabi and Ibn Sina, but clearly implies its falsehood. He says that this view is the saying of 'leading philosophers' and those theologians who contradict the people of hadith. Al-Farabi explicitly defines prophecy as the ability to imagine intelligible matters in sensory forms. Ibn Sina argues that prophets like Moses and Muhammad could not have explained truths directly because their audiences were incapable, and if they did, the people would have abandoned following them. Ibn Taymiyya thus records these views to critique them, though in this passage he does not provide a full counter-argument but rather identifies the position as heretical or misguided.

Key Terms
الرسل
الحقائق الخبرية والطلبية
التخييل
الفلاسفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the specific property of prophecy according to al-Farabi?
A) The ability to perform miracles.
B) The excellence of imagining intelligible matters in sensory forms.
C) The knowledge of hidden truths.
D) The power to legislate laws.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's dilemma exposes the epistemological flaw in the philosophers' theory: it forces a choice between prophetic ignorance and deceptive imagery, both of which contradict the Islamic understanding of revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a similar dilemma against the claim that mutawātir (mass transmission) is not necessary for certainty. Use the format: either the reports are certain or they are not; if certain, then ...; if not, then ...
?
Identify the logical structure (taqsīm) in another passage from Ibn Taymiyya's 'al-Radd 'ala al-Mantiqiyyīn' where he critiques the definition of knowledge.

Critique of Philosophical Theory of Prophethood as Imaginative Representation
Ibn Taymiyya critiques philosophical theory of prophethood as imagination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the philosophical claim that prophethood is imaginative representation and reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's critique of its elitist implications.

Core Idea
Reducing prophethood to imagination undermines its universal truth and obligation.

Ibn Taymiyya reports and critiques the influential philosophical theory that prophethood is essentially the capacity to represent intellectual truths in sensible forms for the common people. This theory, advocated by al-Farabi, Ibn Sina, and others, implies that religion is only for the masses, not the elite. Ibn Taymiyya exposes this as a reduction of prophetic revelation and a threat to its universal validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target doctrine
Find the exact claim made by al-Farabi and Ibn Sina about prophethood. Al-Farabi says it is 'the excellence of imagining intellectual matters in sensible forms'; Ibn Sina says prophets could not convey pure truths to their people because they would either fail to understand or lose determination.
Focus on the phrase 'تخييل الأمور المعقولة في الصور المحسوسة'.
2
Trace Ibn Taymiyya's reporting
Notice that Ibn Taymiyya first describes the actual benefit of prophethood for the common people, then states that this is also the view of the philosophers. He quotes them to show that they agree on the method but differ on the underlying truth.
He uses this agreement to set up his critique.
3
Analyze the grounds of critique
Ibn Taymiyya points out that this theory implies that religion is only for the common people, not the elite. He distinguishes groups like the Ittihadiyya who deny all apparent religion, and others who reserve it for the masses. This contradicts the universal call of Islam.
The critique is epistemological and theological: revelation is truth, not mere representation.
4
Evaluate the implications
Consider the consequence: if prophethood is only imaginative, then the elite are not bound by the law (namus). Ibn Taymiyya notes that even Ibn Sina, when repentant, followed the nomistic obligations, implying that this theory undermines itself.
The critique shows the philosophers' position is unstable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ينتفع به عموم الناس في أمر الإيمان بالله وبالمعاد، وذلك يقرر في النفوس عظمة الله وعظمة اليوم الآخر ما يحض النفوس على عبادة الله وعلى الرجاء والخوف، فينتفعون بذلك وينالون السعادة بحسب إمكانهم واستعدادهم، إذ هذا الذي فعلته الرسل هو غاية الإمكان في كشف الحقائق لعموم النوع البشري، ومقصود الرسل حفظ النوع البشري وإقامة مصلحة معاشه ومعاده = فمعلوم أن هذا قول حذاق الفلاسفة، مثل الفارابي وابن سينا وغيرهما، وهو قول كل حاذق وفاضل من المتكلمين في القدر الذي يخالف فيه أهل الحديث. فالفارابي يقول: إن خاصة النبوة جودة تخييل الأمور المعقولة في الصور المحسوسة أو نحو هذه العبارة. وابن سينا يذكر هذا المعنى في مواضع، ويقول: ما كان يمكن موسى بن عمران مع أولئك العبرانيين، ولا يمكن محمدا مع أولئك العرب الجفاة، أن يبينا لهم الحقائق على ما هي عليه فإنهم كانوا يعجزون عن فهم ذلك، وإن فهموه على ما هم عليه أنحلت عزماتهم عن اتباعه؛ لأنهم لا يرون من العلم ما يقتضي العمل. وهذا المعنى يوجد في كلام أبي حامد وأمثاله ومن بعده، طائفة منه في الإحياء وغير الإحياء، وكذلك في كلام الرازي. وأما الاتحادية ونحوهم من المتكلمين فعليه مدارهم ومبنى كلام الباطنية والقرامطة عليه، لكن هؤلاء ينكرون ظواهر الأمور العملية والعلمية جميعا، وأما غير هؤلاء فلا ينكرون العمليات الظاهرة المتواترة، لكن قد يجعلونها لعموم الناس لا لخصوصهم، كما يقولون مثل ذلك في الأمور الخبرية، ومدار كلامهم على أن الرسالة متضمنة لمصلحة العموم علما وعملا، وأما الخاصة فلا. وعلى هذا يدور كلام أصحاب رسائل إخوان الصفا وسائر فضلاء المتفلسفة. ثم منهم من يوجب اتباع الأمور العملية من الأمور الشرعية، وهؤلاء كثيرون في متفقهتهم ومتصوفتهم وعقلاء فلاسفتهم. وإلى هنا كان ينتهي علم ابن سينا إذا تاب والتزم القيام بالواجبات الناموسية؛ فإن قدماء الفلاسفة كانوا يوجبون أتباع النواميس التي وضعها
The common people benefit from it in matters of belief in God and the Last Day, and it establishes in souls the greatness of God and the greatness of the Last Day, which urges souls to worship God and to hope and fear. They benefit by that and attain happiness according to their capacity and preparedness. For this that the messengers did is the utmost possible in revealing truths to the entire human species, and the purpose of the messengers is to preserve the human species and establish the welfare of their livelihood and afterlife. So it is known that this is the saying of the expert philosophers, such as al-Farabi, Ibn Sina, and others, and it is the saying of every expert and virtuous theologian to the extent that he differs from the People of Hadith. Al-Farabi says: 'The specific property of prophethood is the excellence of imagining intellectual matters in sensible forms,' or something like this expression. Ibn Sina mentions this meaning in places and says: 'It was not possible for Moses son of Imran with those Hebrews, nor possible for Muhammad with those rough Arabs, to explain to them the truths as they are, for they were incapable of understanding that. And if they understood them as they are, their resolve would have weakened from following them, because they do not see knowledge that necessitates action.' This meaning is found in the speech of Abu Hamid (al-Ghazali) and his likes and those after him, a part of it in the Ihya and other than the Ihya, and likewise in the speech of al-Razi. As for the Ittihadiyya and their like among the theologians, their discourse revolves around it, and the discourse of the Batiniyya and Qaramita is based on it. But these deny the apparent aspects of practical and theoretical matters entirely. As for others, they do not deny the apparent and widely transmitted practices, but they may make them for the common people, not for the elite, as they say similar things about reports. The pivot of their discourse is that the message includes the welfare of the common people in knowledge and action, but not the elite. Upon this revolves the discourse of the authors of the Epistles of the Brethren of Purity and other virtuous philosophizers. Then among them are those who obligate following the practical matters from the legal matters, and these are many among their jurisprudents, their Sufis, and the intellectuals of their philosophers. Up to here reached the knowledge of Ibn Sina when he repented and committed to performing the nomistic obligations; for the ancient philosophers used to obligate following the nomoi that they established.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage is a critical exposition by Ibn Taymiyya of the philosophical theory of prophethood. He begins by describing the actual benefit of prophethood for the common people: it establishes belief in God and the Last Day, urges worship, hope, and fear, leading to happiness. The messengers' method is the best possible for conveying truths to humanity, and their aim is human welfare in this world and the next. Ibn Taymiyya then states that this view (that the messengers' method is suitable for the masses) is actually the same as the saying of leading philosophers like al-Farabi and Ibn Sina, though they differ from the People of Hadith in their interpretation. Al-Farabi is quoted: the essence of prophethood is the excellent imagining of intelligibles in sensible forms. Ibn Sina adds that prophets like Moses and Muhammad could not convey pure truths to their people because the people would either fail to understand or lose determination due to lack of knowledge inspiring action. Ibn Taymiyya notes that this idea appears in al-Ghazali and al-Razi as well. He then distinguishes among groups: the Ittihadiyya (pantheists) deny all apparent religious matters, while others only deny them for the elite, reserving religion for the masses. This is the position of the Brethren of Purity and other philosophizers. Some among them still obligate following practical religious laws. Ibn Taymiyya ends by remarking that Ibn Sina himself, in his repentant state, adhered to these nomoi, just as the ancient philosophers required following the laws (namus) they established. The critique is implicit: by reducing prophethood to imaginative representations, these philosophers undermine the universal truth and practical obligation of religion, treating the masses as incapable of true knowledge.

Key Terms
تخييل (takhyeel): In this passage, takhyeel refers to the philosophical theory that prophets represent intellectual truths in sensible and imaginative forms for the benefit of the common people who cannot grasp pure abstractions. [Note: In standard Islamic philosophy (e.g., al-Farabi), takhyeel is a technical term for the imaginative faculty; Ibn Taymiyya uses it critically to expose the reduction of prophethood.]
النبوة (al-nubuwwa): Prophethood; here the philosophers define it primarily as a capacity for imaginative representation of intelligibles, while Ibn Taymiyya critiques this as an incomplete and elitist view. [Note: In orthodox theology, prophethood is primarily about revelation (wahy) and law-giving, not primarily imaginative representation. Ibn Taymiyya emphasizes the universal guidance aspect.]
الناموس (al-namus): In this passage, namus refers to the religious law or nomos, the legal and political code established by prophets; the ancient philosophers required following these laws. [Note: The term is borrowed from Greek nomos; in Islamic contexts, it is sometimes synonymous with shari'a, but Ibn Taymiyya uses it to highlight the philosophers' external adherence without true belief.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Farabi as quoted in the passage, what is the specific property of prophethood?
A) The ability to perform miracles
B) The excellence of imagining intellectual matters in sensible forms
C) The direct reception of divine revelation without mediation
D) The capacity to legislate laws for the elite

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's critique reveals that the philosophical theory reduces prophethood to a mere pedagogical tool, undermining the universal truth and obligation of revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Read the passage carefully. Identify the philosophical claim about prophethood and write it in your own words. Then, using Ibn Taymiyya's critique, explain why this claim is problematic from an Islamic perspective, focusing on the implications for the elite and the common people.
?
Consider a contemporary debate: Some modern thinkers argue that religious texts are symbolic and not literally true. How would Ibn Taymiyya's critique apply to such a view? Write a short paragraph.

Philosophers' Contradictory Claim about Prophets
Reporting philosophers' contradictory stance on prophetic superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the contradictory claim made by philosophers regarding the superiority of prophets versus themselves.

Core Idea
Philosophers admit the Muhammadan law is best yet reduce prophets to sages and call themselves prophets.

Ibn Taymiyya begins by citing the philosophers' own agreement on the superiority of the Muhammadan law, then reveals their contradictory claim that prophets are merely great sages while philosophers are minor prophets. This exposes their logical inconsistency, which he then uses to argue that opponents of hadith scholars fall into ignorance or hypocrisy.

Guided Path
1
Locate the philosophers' admission
Find the sentence where Ibn Taymiyya reports that philosophers agreed no law is better than the Muhammadan law. This shows they acknowledge its superiority.
This admission is the foundation of the contradiction.
2
Find the contradictory claim
Identify the claim that prophets are 'great sages' and philosophers are 'minor prophets'. This redefines the prophetic status downward and elevates the philosophical status upward.
The contradiction: if the Muhammadan law is best, then its source (prophets) should be superior, not equal or inferior to philosophers.
3
Analyze the inconsistency
Determine why these two statements are contradictory. If prophets are only sages, then their law cannot be divinely superior. If philosophers are prophets, they should be judged by the same standard.
Ibn Taymiyya uses this inconsistency to undermine the philosophers' position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أكابر حكماء البلاد، فلآن يوجبوا أتباع نواميس الرسل أولى، فإنهم ـ كما قال ابن سينا - : أتفق فلاسفة العالم على أنه لم يقرع العالم ناموس أفضل من هذا الناموس المحمدي، وكل عقلاء الفلاسفة متفقون على أنه أكمل النوع وأفضل النوع البشري، وأن جنس الرسل أفضل من جنس الفلاسفة المشاهير، ثم قد يزعمون أن الرسل والأنبياء حكماء كبار، وأن الفلاسفة الحكماء أنبياء صغار، وقد يجعلونهم صنفان، وليس هذا موضع شرح ذلك، فقد تكلمنا على ذلك في غير هذا الموضع. وإنما الغرض أن هؤلاء الأساطين من الفلاسفة والمتكلمين غاية ما يقولون هذا القول، ونحن ذكرنا الأمر على وجه التقسيم العقلي الحاصر لئلا يخرج عنه قسم، ليتبين أن المخالف لعلماء الحديث علمًا وعملا إما جاهل وإما منافق، والمنافق جاهل وزيادة كما سنبينه إن شاء الله، والجاهل هنا فيه شعبة نفاق وإن كان لا يعلم بها، فالمنكر لذلك جاهل منافق.
The great sages of the region, then they make it necessary to follow the laws of the messengers more worthy, for they — as Ibn Sina said — agreed the philosophers of the world that no law has struck the world better than this Muhammadan law, and all rational philosophers agree that it is the most complete of its kind and the best of the human kind, and that the genus of messengers is better than the genus of famous philosophers. Then they claim that the messengers and prophets are great sages, and that the philosopher-sages are minor prophets, and they may make them two categories. This is not the place to explain that, for we have spoken about it elsewhere. The purpose is that these masters of philosophers and theologians, the utmost they say is this saying, and we have mentioned the matter on the basis of a comprehensive rational division so that no category escapes, to make clear that the one who opposes the scholars of hadith in knowledge and action is either ignorant or a hypocrite. The hypocrite is ignorant and more, as we will explain if God wills. The ignorant here has a branch of hypocrisy even if he does not know it. So the denier of that is an ignorant hypocrite.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage is a critical exposition by Ibn Taymiyya of the philosophers' contradictory stance. He begins by citing Ibn Sina's agreement among philosophers that the Muhammadan law is the best. Then he notes that despite this, they claim prophets are great sages and philosophers are minor prophets, effectively equating or even reversing the hierarchy. Ibn Taymiyya says his aim is to show, through a rational exhaustive division, that anyone who opposes the scholars of hadith is either ignorant or a hypocrite. The hypocrite is worse, and even the ignorant here has an element of hypocrisy. This sets up the logical argument that follows.

Key Terms
ناموس (nāmūs): Law or divine dispensation, here referring to the prophetic law of Muhammad. [Note: In Greek philosophy, nomos means law; in Islamic context, it refers to divine revelation.]
رسل (rusul): Messengers or prophets, considered by Ibn Taymiyya as superior to philosophers. [Note: Stable term across Islamic texts.]
فلاسفة (falāsifa): Philosophers, often referred to as 'minor prophets' by some, but Ibn Taymiyya criticizes this. [Note: Ibn Taymiyya uses this term polemically to distinguish from prophets.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what contradictory claim do the philosophers make?
A) They deny the excellence of the Muhammadan law.
B) They admit the Muhammadan law is the best but then claim prophets are 'great sages' and philosophers are 'minor prophets'.
C) They claim all prophets are philosophers.
D) They reject all divine laws.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' claim is not merely wrong but internally contradictory, exposing their irrationality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find a contemporary Islamic argument that contains a contradiction (e.g., someone claiming to follow the Sunnah but rejecting authentic hadith). Write a brief analysis showing the contradiction.

Ibn Taymiyya's Exhaustive Division: Opponent is Ignorant or Hypocrite
Applying a rational division to classify opponents of hadith scholars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's exhaustive division (taqsim) of opponents of the hadith scholars into ignorant or hypocrite.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya uses a rational exhaustive division: all opponents of hadith scholars are either ignorant or hypocrite.

After exposing the philosophers' contradiction, Ibn Taymiyya applies a rational exhaustive division to categorize all opponents of the scholars of hadith. This logical classification claims to cover every possibility, leaving no room for a valid objection. He then explains that the hypocrite is a worse form of ignorance, and even the ignorant has a touch of hypocrisy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Find the two terms Ibn Taymiyya uses to classify opponents: 'jahil' (ignorant) and 'munafiq' (hypocrite).
These are the only two possibilities in his division.
2
Understand the relationship between them
Ibn Taymiyya says the hypocrite is 'ignorant and more'. This means hypocrisy includes ignorance but adds intentional deception.
This is a hierarchical relationship.
3
Note the additional nuance
He adds that even the ignorant person has 'a branch of hypocrisy' (shu'bat nifaq), even if unaware. This strengthens the division.
This implies that ignorance in this context is not innocent but linked to hypocrisy.
4
Recognize the exhaustive nature
Ibn Taymiyya claims this division is 'comprehensive' (hāsir), meaning no category escapes. All opponents must be one of these two.
This is a common rational method in Islamic sciences to ensure complete coverage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أكابر حكماء البلاد، فلآن يوجبوا أتباع نواميس الرسل أولى، فإنهم ـ كما قال ابن سينا - : أتفق فلاسفة العالم على أنه لم يقرع العالم ناموس أفضل من هذا الناموس المحمدي، وكل عقلاء الفلاسفة متفقون على أنه أكمل النوع وأفضل النوع البشري، وأن جنس الرسل أفضل من جنس الفلاسفة المشاهير، ثم قد يزعمون أن الرسل والأنبياء حكماء كبار، وأن الفلاسفة الحكماء أنبياء صغار، وقد يجعلونهم صنفان، وليس هذا موضع شرح ذلك، فقد تكلمنا على ذلك في غير هذا الموضع. وإنما الغرض أن هؤلاء الأساطين من الفلاسفة والمتكلمين غاية ما يقولون هذا القول، ونحن ذكرنا الأمر على وجه التقسيم العقلي الحاصر لئلا يخرج عنه قسم، ليتبين أن المخالف لعلماء الحديث علمًا وعملا إما جاهل وإما منافق، والمنافق جاهل وزيادة كما سنبينه إن شاء الله، والجاهل هنا فيه شعبة نفاق وإن كان لا يعلم بها، فالمنكر لذلك جاهل منافق.
The great sages of the region, then they make it necessary to follow the laws of the messengers more worthy, for they — as Ibn Sina said — agreed the philosophers of the world that no law has struck the world better than this Muhammadan law, and all rational philosophers agree that it is the most complete of its kind and the best of the human kind, and that the genus of messengers is better than the genus of famous philosophers. Then they claim that the messengers and prophets are great sages, and that the philosopher-sages are minor prophets, and they may make them two categories. This is not the place to explain that, for we have spoken about it elsewhere. The purpose is that these masters of philosophers and theologians, the utmost they say is this saying, and we have mentioned the matter on the basis of a comprehensive rational division so that no category escapes, to make clear that the one who opposes the scholars of hadith in knowledge and action is either ignorant or a hypocrite. The hypocrite is ignorant and more, as we will explain if God wills. The ignorant here has a branch of hypocrisy even if he does not know it. So the denier of that is an ignorant hypocrite.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage is a critical exposition by Ibn Taymiyya of the philosophers' contradictory stance. He begins by citing Ibn Sina's agreement among philosophers that the Muhammadan law is the best. Then he notes that despite this, they claim prophets are great sages and philosophers are minor prophets, effectively equating or even reversing the hierarchy. Ibn Taymiyya says his aim is to show, through a rational exhaustive division, that anyone who opposes the scholars of hadith is either ignorant or a hypocrite. The hypocrite is worse, and even the ignorant here has an element of hypocrisy. This sets up the logical argument that follows.

Key Terms
جَاهِل (jāhil): Ignorant person, one without knowledge, used as a category of opponent. [Note: Stable term.]
مُنَافِق (munāfiq): Hypocrite, one who outwardly agrees but inwardly disbelieves, considered worse than ignorant. [Note: Stable term but with strong theological connotations.]
تَقْسِيم عَقْلِيّ حَاصِر (taqsīm 'aqlī hāsir): Rational exhaustive division, a logical method that categorizes all possibilities without overlap. [Note: Technical term in Islamic logic.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what are the two categories of those who oppose the scholars of hadith?
A) Believer and disbeliever.
B) Ignorant and hypocrite.
C) Philosopher and theologian.
D) Scholar and layman.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's exhaustive division of opponents into ignorant or hypocrite aims to logically preclude any legitimate opposition to the hadith scholars.

Ibn Taymiyya's Method of Exposing Hidden Affinities
Author exposes common traits and contradictions among deviant groups.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how Ibn Taymiyya exposes hidden ideological affinities between seemingly different groups by analyzing their common traits and internal contradictions.

Core Idea
Deviant groups unite in attacking the Sunnah and claiming exclusive truth, yet they bitterly divide.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya performs a critical refinement: he identifies a hidden resemblance between groups that outwardly appear distinct—philosophers, Rafidah, Qaramita, and Ittihadiyya. By pinpointing their shared characteristics (defaming the Companions and claiming inner truth) and their internal contradictions (mutual hatred), he demonstrates that their falsehood is evident. This method is central to his refutation of deviant sects and can be applied to evaluate any group that claims exclusivity while opposing mainstream Islam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the groups mentioned
List the four groups or currents Ibn Taymiyya links: the philosophers (Ibn Sina), the Rafidah (Shi'a who reject the first two caliphs), the Qaramita (extremist Ismailis), and the Ittihadiyya (pantheists/unionists like Ibn Arabi's followers).
2
Extract the common traits
Find the three common traits Ibn Taymiyya lists: (1) defaming the best of the Ummah (the Companions) and the established Sunni principles, (2) claiming a hidden truth exclusive to themselves, (3) their connection to philosophy (especially the Qaramita influence on Ibn Sina).
3
Observe the internal contradictions
Notice that despite these commonalities, the groups are full of mutual curses, hatred, and differences. Ibn Taymiyya cites the Qur'anic verse about Christians to underscore that such division is a sign of deviation.
Supporting evidence: the Qur'an (5:14) about enmity among Christians after they forgot their covenant.
4
Assess the rhetorical purpose
Ibn Taymiyya uses this exposure to undermine the authority of these groups: if they claim a special truth yet cannot agree among themselves, their claim is false. This is a classical argument in Sunni polemics.
This method is used extensively in refutations like Minhaj al-Sunnah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَكَذَلِكَ ابْنُ سِينَا وَغَيْرُهُ يَذْكُرُونَ مِنَ التَّنَقُّصِ بِالصَّحَابَةِ مَا وَرِثَهُ عَنْ أَبِيهِ وَشِيعَتِهِ الْقَرَامِطَةِ، حَتَّى تَجِدَهُمْ إِذَا ذَكَرُوا فِي آخِرِ الْفَلْسَفَةِ حَاجَةَ النَّوْعِ الْبَشَرِيِّ إِلَى الْإِمَامَةِ عَرَّضُوا بِقَوْلِ الرَّافِضَةِ الضُّلَّالِ، لَكِنْ أُولَئِكَ يُصَرِّحُونَ مِنَ السَّبِّ بِأَكْثَرَ مِمَّا يُصَرِّحُ بِهِ هَؤُلَاءِ. وَلِهَذَا تَجِدُ بَيْنَ الرَّافِضَةِ وَالْقَرَامِطَةِ وَالِاتِّحَادِيَّةِ اقْتِرَانًا وَاشْتِبَاهًا، تَجْمَعُهُمْ أُمُورٌ، مِنْهَا: الطَّعْنُ فِي خِيَارِ هَذِهِ الْأُمَّةِ، وَفِيمَا عَلَيْهِ أَهْلُ السُّنَّةِ وَالْجَمَاعَةِ، وَفِيمَا اسْتَقَرَّ مِنْ أُصُولِ الْمِلَّةِ وَقَوَاعِدِ الدِّينِ. وَيَدَّعُونَ بَاطِنًا امْتَازُوا بِهِ وَاخْتَصُّوا بِهِ عَمَّنْ سِوَاهُمْ. ثُمَّ هُمْ مَعَ ذَلِكَ مُتَلَاعِنُونَ مُتَبَاغِضُونَ مُخْتَلِفُونَ، كَمَا رَأَيْتُ وَسَمِعْتُ مِنْ ذَلِكَ مَا لَا يُحْصَى، كَمَا قَالَ اللَّهُ عَنِ النَّصَارَى: ﴿وَمِنَ الَّذِينَ قَالُوا إِنَّا نَصَارَى أَخَذْنَا مِيثَاقَهُمْ فَنَسُوا حَظًّا مِمَّا ذُكِرُوا بِهِ فَأَغْرَيْنَا بَيْنَهُمُ الْعَدَاوَةَ وَالْبَغْضَاءَ إِلَى يَوْمِ الْقِيَامَةِ﴾ [الْمَائِدَةِ: ١٤].
Likewise, Ibn Sina and others mention of disparagement of the Companions what he inherited from his father and his Qaramita followers, so that you find them when they mention at the end of philosophy the need of the human species for the Imamate, they allude to the statement of the misguided Rafidah, but those (Rafidah) explicitly express more abuse than these (philosophers) do. And for this reason you find between the Rafidah, the Qaramita, and the Ittihadiyya a close connection and resemblance, matters that unite them, among them: defamation of the best of this Ummah, and of what the Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah are upon, and of what has been established from the foundations of the Millah and the principles of the religion. And they claim an inner (truth) by which they are distinguished and specialized from others. Then despite that, they curse each other, hate each other, and differ, as I have seen and heard of that which cannot be counted, just as Allah said about the Christians: 'And from those who say, "We are Christians," We took their covenant, but they forgot a portion of that of which they were reminded, so We caused among them enmity and hatred until the Day of Resurrection.' [Al-Ma'idah: 14].
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is a critical exposé by Ibn Taymiyya. He first points out that the philosophers (like Ibn Sina) inherited their tendency to disparage the Companions from their Qaramita background. He notes that when they discuss the need for an imam in philosophy, they allude to the Rafidah (Shi'a) position, but the Rafidah are more explicit in their abuse. This suggests a hidden link. He then asserts that one finds a close connection and resemblance between three groups: Rafidah, Qaramita, and Ittihadiyya (pantheists/unionists). They are united by several factors: (1) They attack the best of the Ummah (the Companions) and the established Sunni doctrines and principles. (2) They claim a hidden truth exclusive to themselves. Yet, despite these commonalities, they are full of mutual curses, hatred, and differences, to an extent that cannot be counted. Ibn Taymiyya supports this observation with Quranic verses about the Christians and Jews being divided due to their deviation. The argument is that these groups are not a unified front; their inner contradictions are evidence of their falsehood.

Key Terms
الرافضة
القرامطة
الاتحادية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They all follow the same fiqh school.
B) They all defame the best of the Ummah and claim a hidden truth.
C) They all reject the Qur'an.
D) They all accept the caliphate of the four rightly-guided caliphs.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's method of revealing hidden affinities between deviant groups is a powerful tool for refuting false claims by exposing both shared errors and internal contradictions.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of al-Razi's Epistemological Skepticism
Author critiques al-Razi's epistemological skepticism as heretical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two premises of heresy as articulated by Ibn Taymiyya and explain how they undermine religious epistemology.

Core Idea
The two premises of heresy are rejecting linguistic proofs and denying reports' certainty.

Ibn Taymiyya does not merely list heretical groups; he exposes a common epistemological disease. In this passage, he targets Fakhr al-Din al-Razi for attacking the certainty of linguistic proofs and the epistemic value of reports—calling these the two premises of heresy. Understanding this critique reveals Ibn Taymiyya's deep concern with foundational skepticism that erodes all revealed knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the heretical groups
Locate the three groups Ibn Taymiyya mentions: Rafidah, Qaramitah, and Ittihadiyyah. Note how he claims they share common traits: slandering the best of the Ummah, rejecting Ahl al-Sunnah, and claiming exclusive inner knowledge.
2
Recognize the pattern of mutual hostility
Despite their similarities, these groups curse each other. Ibn Taymiyya cites Quranic verses about Christians and Jews to show that this internal enmity is a divine pattern among deviant sects.
3
Locate the critique of al-Razi
Find where Ibn Taymiyya mentions 'Abu Abdullah al-Razi' and his attitude toward linguistic proofs and reports. Understanding this is the core of the lesson.
4
Extract the two premises of heresy
Ibn Taymiyya states that al-Razi 'attacks the indication of linguistic proofs for certainty, and the benefit of reports for knowledge.' These are the two premises of heresy (muqaddimata al-zandaqah). Recognize that they are epistemological: one denies certainty from language, the other denies knowledge from transmitted reports.
5
Identify the contradiction in al-Razi's position
Despite this skepticism, al-Razi still relies on 'what is known by necessity from the religion of Islam' for basic matters like worship and resurrection. This inconsistency is Ibn Taymiyya's key point: one cannot reject the main sources of Islamic knowledge while accepting its conclusions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك أبن سينا وغيره يذكرون من التنقص بالصحابة ما ورثه عن أبيه وشيعته القرامطة، حتى تجدهم إذا ذكروا في آخر الفلسفة حاجة النوع البشري إلى الإمامة عرَّضوا بقول الرافضة الضُّلال، لكن أولئك يصرحون من السَّبُ بأكثر مما يصرح به هؤلاء. ولهذا تجد بين الرافضة والقرامطة والاتحادية أقترانا واشتباها، تجمعهم أمور، منها: الطعن في خيار هذه الأمة، وفيما عليه أهل السنة والجماعة، وفيما استقر من أصول المِلَّة وقواعد الدين. ويدعون باطنا امتازوا به واختصّوا به عمن سواهم. ثم هم مع ذلك متلاعنون متباغضون، مختلفون، كما رأيتُ وسمعت من ذلك ما لا يحصى، كما قال الله عن النصارى: ﴿وَمِنَ الَّذِينَ قَالُوا إِنَّا نَصَدَرَى أَخَذْنَا مِيثَقَهُمْ فَنَسُوا حَظًّا مِمَّا ذُكِرُوا بِهِ، فَأَغْرَيْنَا بَيْنَهُمُ الْعَدَاوَةَ وَالْبَغْضَاءَ إِلَى يَوْمِ الْقِيَمَةِ ﴾ [المائدة: ١٤]، وقال عن اليهود: ﴿وَالْقَيْنَا بَيْنَهُمُ الْعَدَاوَةَ وَالْبَغْضَاءَ إِلَى يَوْمِ الْقِيَمَةِ كُلَّمَا أَوْقَدُوا نَارًا لِلْحَرْبِ أَطْفَاهَا اللهُ ﴾ [المائدة: ٦٤]. وكذلك المتكلمون المخلطون الذين يكونون تارة مع المسلمين وإن كانوا مبتدعين، وتارة مع الفلاسفة الصابئين، وتارة مع الكفار المشركين، وتارة يقابلون بين الطوائف وينظرون لمن تكون الدائرة، وتارةً يتحيَّرون بين الطوائف. وهذه الصفة كَثُرت في كثير ممن أنتسب إلى الإسلام من العلماء والأمراء وغيرهم، لاسيَّما لما ظهر المشركون من التُّرك على أرض الإسلام بالمشرق في أثناء المئة السابعة، وكان كثير ممن ينتسب إلى الإسلام فيه من النفاق والردّة ما أوجب تسليط المشركين وأهل الكتاب. فتجد أبا عبد الله الرازي يطعن في دلالة الأدلة اللفظية على اليقين، وفي إفادة الأخبار للعلم، وهذان هما مقدمتا الزندقة كما قدمناه، ثم يعتمد فيما أقر به من أمور الإسلام على ما عُلِمَ بالاضطرار من دين الإسلام، مثل العبادات والمحرمات الظاهرة، وكذلك الإقرار بمعاد الأجساد.
And likewise Ibn Sina and others mention from the belittlement of the Companions what he inherited from his father and his Qaramitah sect, so that you find them when they mention at the end of philosophy the need of the human species for the Imamate, they allude to the saying of the misguided Rafidah, but those explicitly slander more than these do. And for this reason you find between the Rafidah, the Qaramitah, and the Ittihadiyyah a close connection and resemblance; matters unite them, among them: attacking the best of this Ummah, and what Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah are upon, and what has been established from the principles of the Millah and the foundations of the religion. And they claim an inner [knowledge] with which they are distinguished and specialized apart from others. Then despite that, they curse each other and hate each other, differing, as I have seen and heard of that what cannot be counted, as Allah said about the Christians: {And from those who say 'We are Christians' We took their covenant, but they forgot a portion of what they were reminded of, so We caused between them enmity and hatred until the Day of Resurrection} [Al-Ma'idah: 14], and He said about the Jews: {And We cast between them enmity and hatred until the Day of Resurrection, every time they kindled a fire for war, Allah extinguished it} [Al-Ma'idah: 64]. And likewise the confused dialectical theologians who are sometimes with the Muslims even if they are innovators, sometimes with the Sabian philosophers, sometimes with the polytheist disbelievers, sometimes confronting the sects and seeing to whom the turn will be, and sometimes perplexed between the sects. And this characteristic increased in many of those who are attributed to Islam among the scholars, princes, and others, especially when the polytheist Turks appeared on the land of Islam in the East during the seventh century, and many of those attributed to Islam had in them hypocrisy and apostasy that necessitated the domination of the polytheists and the People of the Book. So you find Abu Abdullah al-Razi attacking the indication of linguistic proofs for certainty, and the benefit of reports for knowledge, and these two are the premises of heresy as we have previously mentioned, then he relies in what he affirms of the matters of Islam on what is known by necessity from the religion of Islam, such as apparent acts of worship and prohibitions, and likewise the affirmation of the resurrection of bodies.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is a critical exposition by Ibn Taymiyya linking the philosophical tradition (exemplified by Ibn Sina) with heretical sects (Rafidah, Qaramitah, Ittihadiyyah) and then criticizing the theological method of Fakhr al-Din al-Razi. The author begins by noting that Ibn Sina inherited slander of the Companions from his father and the Qaramitah, and that they covertly allude to the Rafidah's doctrine of the Imamate while the Rafidah openly slander more. He then highlights three groups—Rafidah, Qaramitah, and Ittihadiyyah—sharing common traits: attacking the best of the Ummah and the established principles of Ahl al-Sunnah, and claiming an exclusive inner knowledge. Despite this, they are mutually hostile, and Ibn Taymiyya cites Quranic verses about Christians and Jews to illustrate that such enmity among deviant groups is a divine pattern. Next, he describes 'confused dialectical theologians' who oscillate between Muslims, Sabian philosophers, and polytheists, uncertain and perplexed. He connects this to the historical context of the Mongol invasions in the seventh century, noting that hypocrisy and apostasy among those claiming Islam enabled the domination of polytheists and People of the Book. Finally, he targets Fakhr al-Din al-Razi specifically for attacking the certainty of linguistic proofs and reports—calling these the two premises of heresy—yet paradoxically relying on necessary knowledge of Islam for matters like worship and resurrection. This critique underscores Ibn Taymiyya's view that skepticism toward revealed texts undermines the foundations of faith.

Key Terms
الرافضة
القرامطة
الاتحادية
المتكلمون المخلطون
مقدمتا الزندقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Rejecting the prophethood of Muhammad and the resurrection of bodies.
B) Attacking the certainty of linguistic proofs and rejecting the epistemological value of reports.
C) Denying the existence of Allah and the authority of the Quran.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya identifies the rejection of linguistic and report-based certainty as the foundational premises of heresy, warning that such skepticism destroys the very basis of Islamic knowledge.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Al-Razi's Contradiction
Ibn Taymiyya criticizes al-Razi's contradictory stance on linguistic evidence and necessary knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two premises Ibn Taymiyya calls 'the premises of heresy' and explain why al-Razi's position is contradictory.

Core Idea
Al-Razi denies textual proofs certainty yet relies on necessary knowledge derived from texts—a fatal contradiction.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya exposes a devastating inconsistency in Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's epistemology. Al-Razi attacks the certainty of verbal proofs and reports, yet he turns around and affirms core Islamic doctrines based on knowledge that comes precisely from those sources. Ibn Taymiyya calls this inconsistency the 'premises of heresy' and uses it to illustrate the danger of selective skepticism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of critique
Who is Ibn Taymiyya criticizing? Note his general characterization of 'confused theologians' and their shifting allegiances.
2
Extract the two premises attacked by al-Razi
What does al-Razi deny regarding 'verbal proofs' (al-adilla al-lafziyya) and 'reports' (al-akhbar)? These are the 'premises of heresy'.
3
Find al-Razi's own affirmations
What Islamic beliefs does al-Razi still affirm, and on what basis does he claim to know them? Look for 'what is known by necessity from the religion of Islam'.
4
Analyze the contradiction
Why does Ibn Taymiyya consider this a contradiction? How does al-Razi's necessary knowledge depend on the very sources he attacks?
Ibn Taymiyya notes that this necessary knowledge is actually derived from commentaries and hadiths.
5
Evaluate the label 'premises of heresy'
Why does Ibn Taymiyya equate al-Razi's stance with heresy (zandaqa)? What is the logical consequence of denying textual certainty while affirming core doctrines?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك المتكلمون المخلطون الذين يكونون تارة مع المسلمين وإن كانوا مبتدعين، وتارة مع الفلاسفة الصابئين، وتارة مع الكفار المشركين، وتارة يقابلون بين الطوائف وينظرون لمن تكون الدائرة، وتارةً يتحيَّرون بين الطوائف. وهذه الصفة كَثُرت في كثير ممن أنتسب إلى الإسلام من العلماء والأمراء وغيرهم، لاسيَّما لما ظهر المشركون من التُّرك على أرض الإسلام بالمشرق في أثناء المئة السابعة، وكان كثير ممن ينتسب إلى الإسلام فيه من النفاق والردّة ما أوجب تسليط المشركين وأهل الكتاب. فتجد أبا عبد الله الرازي يطعن في دلالة الأدلة اللفظية على اليقين، وفي إفادة الأخبار للعلم، وهذان هما مقدمتا الزندقة كما قدمناه، ثم يعتمد فيما أقر به من أمور الإسلام على ما عُلِمَ بالاضطرار من دين الإسلام، مثل العبادات والمحرمات الظاهرة، وكذلك الإقرار بمعاد الأجساد، مع أن الاطلاع على التفاسير والأحاديث يجعل العلم بذلك مستفادا من أمور كثيرة. فلا يعطّلُ تعطيل الفلاسفة الصَّابئين، ولا يُقِرُّ إقرار الحنفاء العلماء المؤمنين.
And likewise the confused theologians who are sometimes with the Muslims even if they are innovators, sometimes with the Sabian philosophers, sometimes with the polytheist disbelievers, and sometimes they confront the sects and see to whom the circle (of dominance) belongs, and sometimes they are perplexed among the sects. And this characteristic abounded in many of those who ascribe to Islam from the scholars, princes, and others, especially when the polytheist Turks appeared on the land of Islam in the east during the seventh century, and many of those who ascribe to Islam had within them hypocrisy and apostasy which necessitated the giving of power to the polytheists and the People of the Book. So you find Abu Abdullah al-Razi attacking the indication of verbal proofs for certainty, and the conveying of reports for knowledge—and these two are the premises of heresy as we have mentioned—then he relies in what he affirms of the matters of Islam upon what is known by necessity from the religion of Islam, such as the apparent acts of worship and prohibitions, and likewise the affirmation of the resurrection of bodies, even though consulting the commentaries and hadiths makes knowledge of that derived from many things. So he does not nullify [completely] like the nullification of the Sabian philosophers, nor does he affirm like the affirmation of the upright believing scholars.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage is a critical exposition by Ibn Taymiyya about a category of people he calls 'confused theologians' (المتكلمون المخلطون). He first characterizes them as having shifting allegiances—sometimes siding with Muslims, sometimes with Sabian philosophers, sometimes with polytheists. This confusion became widespread among those claiming Islam, especially after the Mongol invasions in the 7th century Hijri, which Ibn Taymiyya attributes to hypocrisy and apostasy among some Muslims. He then gives the prime example: Abu Abdullah al-Razi (Fakhr al-Din al-Razi). Ibn Taymiyya points out a glaring contradiction: al-Razi attacks the certainty of verbal proofs (الأدلة اللفظية, i.e., textual evidence from Qur'an and Hadith) and denies that reports (الأخبار, i.e., prophetic traditions) yield knowledge. Ibn Taymiyya calls these the 'premises of heresy' (مقدمتا الزندقة). Yet, al-Razi relies on what is known by necessity (العلم بالاضطرار) from Islam, such as the obligation of worship and prohibition of sins, and affirms the resurrection of bodies. Ibn Taymiyya argues that even this necessary knowledge is actually derived from consulting commentaries and hadiths, so al-Razi's position is incoherent: he neither fully rejects like the Sabian philosophers nor fully accepts like the upright scholars (الحنفاء العلماء المؤمنين). This shows al-Razi's inconsistency and the danger of his selective skepticism.

Key Terms
الأدلة اللفظية
الأخبار
العلم بالاضطرار
الزندقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what two things does al-Razi attack that are the 'premises of heresy'?
A) The necessity of prayer and fasting
B) The indication of verbal proofs for certainty and the conveying of reports for knowledge
C) The existence of God and the angels
D) The authority of the sunna and ijma

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya shows that denying the certainty of revelation while relying on necessary knowledge derived from it is an untenable contradiction that leads to heresy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify one instance in contemporary Islamic discourse where someone selectively dismisses certain hadiths yet affirms core beliefs. Explain how Ibn Taymiyya's critique would apply.
?
Construct a brief argument showing the contradiction in someone who says 'We cannot be certain of the Qur'anic text historically' but then asserts that 'The Qur'an commands us to pray.'

The Meaning of Ijtihad in Seeking Knowledge
Author defines ijtihad and cites hadith on the virtue of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the general meaning of ijtihad (striving in seeking knowledge) from its later technical meaning in Usul al-Fiqh.

Core Idea
Ijtihad in this passage means striving in knowledge, not legal reasoning.

This passage from Ibn Taymiyya's critique of logic opens by defining the term 'mujtahid' in the context of seeking knowledge. He uses Qur'anic and hadith evidence to show that the one who pursues knowledge properly is called a mujtahid, and even a mujahid. Understanding this broader usage is crucial because later Islamic sciences (Usul al-Fiqh) restricted ijtihad to legal interpretation, which can cause confusion when reading earlier texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key term being defined
Locate the term 'ijtihad' in the Arabic and note that the author directly links it to seeking knowledge. The seeker is called 'mujtahid' because he strives (ijtihad) in the ways (turuq) of knowledge.
The author uses the same root j-h-d to connect effort in knowledge to effort in worship and jihad.
2
Parse the evidence
The author cites four pieces of evidence: (1) a saying of the Salaf, (2) a statement of Ubayy and Ibn Mas'ud, (3) the famous hadith of the judge's ijtihad, and (4) the long hadith of Mu'adh on virtues of knowledge. Identify each in the Arabic and translation.
These are not all strictly prophetic, but the author uses them to build a picture of ijtihad as general exertion.
3
Contrast the usage
Notice that here 'mujtahid' applies to any serious student of knowledge, not a qualified jurist. The author emphasizes that the seeker of knowledge is even called a mujahid. This is broader than later technical definitions.
In Usul al-Fiqh, ijtihad is a specific procedure for deriving rulings; here it is simply 'striving'.
4
Assess the author's purpose
The author is laying the groundwork for his critique: he wants to show that true knowledge comes from following the ways of the Salaf, not from Greek logic. By defining ijtihad broadly, he elevates traditional scholarship.
The passage appears early in the work, setting the tone for the rest of the argument.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العلم يجتهد في طلبه من طرقه، ولهذا سُمِّي : مجتهدا ، كما يسمى المجتهد في العبادة وغيرها: مجتهدا ، كما قال بعض السلف: «ما المجتهد فيكم إلا كاللاعب فيهم» (۱). وقال أُبي بن كعب وابن مسعود: «اقتصاد في سُنَّةٍ خيرٌ من أجتهاد في بدعة» (۲) وقد قال النبي : إذا أجتهد الحاكم فأصابَ فله أجران، وإذا أجتهد فأخطأ فله أجر » (۳). وقال معاذ بن جبل - ويروى مرفوعًا، وهو محفوظ عن معاذ : عليكم بالعلم، فإن تعلمه خشية، وطلبه عبادة، ومذاكرته تسبيح، والبحث عنه جهاد، وتعليمه لمن لا يَعْلَمُه صدقة، وبذله لأهله قربة » (٤)، فجَعَل الباحث عنه مجاهدا في سبيل الله.
Knowledge is strived for in its ways, and for this it is called 'mujtahid' (one who strives), just as the one who strives in worship and other things is called 'mujtahid'. As some of the Salaf said: 'The mujtahid among you is like a player among them.' (1) And Ubayy ibn Ka'b and Ibn Mas'ud said: 'Moderation in a Sunnah is better than exertion in an innovation.' (2) And the Prophet said: 'When a judge strives (ijtihad) and is correct, he has two rewards; when he strives and errs, he has one reward.' (3) And Mu'adh ibn Jabal – and it is reported as marfu', but it is preserved from Mu'adh – said: 'Upon you is knowledge, for learning it is fear (of Allah), seeking it is worship, discussing it is glorification, searching for it is jihad, teaching it to one who does not know is charity, and giving it to its people is a means of closeness (to Allah).' (4) So he made the seeker of knowledge a mujahid in the path of Allah.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'mujtahid' in the context of knowledge-seeking. The author begins by stating that knowledge is sought through its proper means, and hence the seeker is called 'mujtahid', just as a person who exerts effort in worship is called 'mujtahid'. He then supports this with quotes from the Salaf and hadith. The first quote highlights that without proper striving, one's knowledge is like a game. The second emphasizes that following the Sunnah with moderation is better than extreme exertion in innovation. The third is the famous hadith about the judge's reward for ijtihad. The fourth, from Mu'adh ibn Jabal, enumerates the virtues of seeking knowledge, culminating in the seeker being considered a mujahid. The author uses these to establish the high status of the one who pursues knowledge properly.
