Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Al-Mathna Interpretations
Author critiques various interpretations of al-mathna and clarifies its true meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four main interpretations of al-mathna and explain Ibn Taymiyya's critique and preferred meaning.

Core Idea
Al-mathna is the Jewish Mishnah, and its warning applies to Muslims who might create rival sources.

The term 'al-mathna' appears in a hadith of Abdullah ibn Amr, sparking diverse interpretations. Ibn Taymiyya systematically examines these views—from Abu Ubayd to al-Albani—exposing errors and clarifying the true meaning. Understanding his method sharpens your ability to critically evaluate hadith interpretation and avoid distorting prophetic teachings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hadith and term
Locate the hadith of Abdullah ibn Amr that mentions al-mathna. Note that Ibn Taymiyya presents it as a warning about books that divert from the Quran.
The hadith context is crucial for evaluating the interpretations.
2
List the four interpretations
Record each view: (1) Abu Ubayd: a Jewish book after Moses; (2) al-Jawhari: Persian poetry (dubayt); (3) al-Qunawi: Rumi's Mathnawi; (4) al-Albani: madhhab jurisprudence books.
Note the sources: Ghareeb al-Hadith, al-Sihah, al-Habit al-Ghawiyy, al-Silsila al-Sahiha.
3
Analyze Ibn Taymiyya's critique
For each interpretation, identify his objection: e.g., al-Jawhari's poetry view lacks hadith connection; al-Qunawi relies on textual alteration; al-Albani's madhhab view is anachronistic. His main point is that the true referent is the Jewish Mishnah, but the hadith's lesson is about Muslims following similar patterns.
Ibn Taymiyya uses linguistic and contextual evidence, not mere opinion.
4
Extract the preferred meaning
Ibn Taymiyya asserts that Abdullah ibn Amr meant the Jewish Mishnah, but the warning is that Muslims will create their own 'mathna'—books that rival the Quran. This is part of the prophesied imitation of earlier scriptures.
He also notes the hadith may be marfu' (raised to Prophet) because it involves analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلف في المراد بالمثناة، فذهب أبو عبيد في غريب الحديث» (٢٨٢/٤) وابن تيمية هنا إلى أنها من كتب اليهود، قال أبو عبيد : سألت رجلا من أهل العلم بالكتب - الأول قد عرفها وقرأها عن المثناة، فقال: إن الأحبار والرهبان من بني إسرائيل بعــد موسیٰ وضعوا كتابًا فيما بينهم على ما أرادوا من غير كتاب الله تبارك وتعالى، فسموه المثناة، كأنه يعني أنهم أحلُّوا فيه ما شاؤوا وحرموا فيه ما شاؤوا عـلـى خـلاف كتاب الله ، فبهذا عرفتُ تأويل حديث عبد الله بن عمرو أنه إنما كره الأخذ عن أهل الكتاب لذلك المعنى، وقد كانت عنده كتب وقعت إليه يوم اليرموك، فأظنه قال هذا لمعرفته بما فيها، ولم يرد النهي عن حديث رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ، وسنته، وكيف ينهى عن ذلك وهو من أكثر الصحابة حديثًا عنه». وقال الجوهري في الصحاح» (٢٢٩٤): يقال: هي التي تسمى بالفارسية دوبيت، وهو الغناء». وذهب أبو الفضل القونوي في «الهابط الغوي من معاني (المثنوي ٦٧ - ٧٢) إلى أنها المثنوي » لجلال الدين الرومي وأن لفظ «المثناة) في الحديث مغيّر عنه. ومال الألباني في الصحيحة» (۲۸۲۱) إلى أن المقصود بها كتب الفقه المذهبي! والحقُّ مفرَّق بين هذه الأقوال، فعبد الله بن عمرو رضي اللَّهُ عَنْهُما يشير بلا ريب إلى مثناة اليهود التي زاحموا بها التوراة كتاب ربهم وألبسوها ثياب القداسة، إلا أن في سياق الحديث اختصارًا، وفقهه هو أن هذه الأمة ستتخذ من الكتب مثناة كمثناة اليهود تصرفها عن كتاب ربها ووحيه، لا أنها ستتخذ مثناة اليهود نفسها كما تأول أبو عبيد، وذلك دأب هذه الأمة في السير على سنن أهل الكتاب، ولذا ذكرتُ أن الحكم برفع الحديث محتمل؛ لأن فيه مجالا للقياس والاجتهاد.
There is disagreement about the intended meaning of al-mathna. Abu Ubayd in Ghareeb al-Hadith (4/282) and Ibn Taymiyya here hold that it is from the books of the Jews. Abu Ubayd said: I asked a man knowledgeable about the books—the first had known it and read it from the mathna—and he said: The rabbis and monks of the Children of Israel after Moses composed a book among themselves according to their own desire, apart from the Book of God, Blessed and Exalted, and they named it al-mathna. As if he meant that they permitted in it what they willed and forbade in it what they willed, contrary to the Book of God. Thus I understood the interpretation of the hadith of Abdullah ibn Amr: that he only disliked taking from the People of the Book for that reason, and he had books that came to him on the day of Yarmuk, so I think he said this because of his knowledge of what was in them, and he did not intend prohibition of the hadith of the Messenger of God, peace be upon him, and his sunna—how could he prohibit that when he was among the most prolific narrators of hadith from him? Al-Jawhari in al-Sihah (2294) said: It is said it is what is called in Persian 'dubayt', and it is singing. Abu al-Fadl al-Qunawi in al-Habit al-Ghawiyy min Ma'ani al-Mathnawi (67-72) held that it is al-Mathnawi of Jalal al-Din al-Rumi and that the word 'al-mathna' in the hadith is altered from it. Al-Albani in al-Silsila al-Sahiha (2821) inclined to the view that it refers to books of madhhab jurisprudence! The truth is distinct among these sayings: Abdullah ibn Amr, may God be pleased with him, undoubtedly refers to the mathna of the Jews with which they crowded the Torah, the Book of their Lord, and clothed it in garments of sanctity. However, the context of the hadith is abridged, and its understanding is that this nation will take books as a mathna like the mathna of the Jews, turning them away from the Book of their Lord and His revelation, not that it will take the very mathna of the Jews as Abu Ubayd interpreted. And this is the habit of this nation in following the ways of the People of the Book. Therefore, I mentioned that the ruling of raising the hadith (to the Prophet) is plausible, because it contains room for analogy and ijtihad.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of the term 'al-mathna' as it appears in a hadith of Abdullah ibn Amr. Ibn Taymiyya presents several interpretations and rejects them, affirming the correct meaning. He begins by stating that there is disagreement about the referent of 'al-mathna'. He then cites Abu Ubayd's view that it refers to a Jewish book composed by rabbis after Moses, in which they permitted and forbade as they wished, contrary to God's Book. Abu Ubayd uses this to interpret Abdullah ibn Amr's hadith: the Companion disliked taking knowledge from the People of the Book because of this book, not to prohibit narrating hadith of the Prophet. Ibn Taymiyya then mentions al-Jawhari's opinion that 'al-mathna' is a Persian poetic form (dubayt), i.e., singing. Next, he cites Abu al-Fadl al-Qunawi's claim that the word is a corruption of 'al-Mathnawi' (the title of Rumi's work). Finally, he notes al-Albani's view that it refers to books of madhhab jurisprudence. Ibn Taymiyya then asserts the correct position: some of these views are mistaken. He clarifies that Abdullah ibn Amr undoubtedly meant the Jewish Mishnah, which they placed alongside the Torah as a rival source of holiness. However, the hadith's context is abridged; its deeper lesson is that this Muslim community will create books that distract from the Quran, similar to how the Jews did with their Mishnah. It does not mean Muslims will literally adopt the Jewish Mishnah, as Abu Ubayd thought. Ibn Taymiyya adds that this is part of the pattern of Muslims following the ways of earlier scripture communities. Thus, the hadith can be considered raised to the Prophet (marfu') because it involves analogy and reasoning. The passage demonstrates Ibn Taymiyya's critical method: he surveys opinions, identifies errors, and clarifies the intended meaning based on textual evidence and context.

Key Terms
المثناة
أبو عبيد
الجوهري
القونوي
الألباني

Checkpoint

A) The poetry of Jalal al-Din al-Rumi
B) The Jewish Mishnah, an oral law collection
C) Books of Islamic jurisprudence
D) A type of Persian song

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya teaches us to critically evaluate hadith interpretations, distinguishing historical referents from timeless warnings.

Benefiting from Non-Muslim Knowledge: Analogical Argument and Critique
Author argues for permissibility of taking worldly knowledge from non-Muslims and criticizes philosophers' mixing truth with falsehood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's analogical argument for the permissibility of taking worldly knowledge from non-Muslims and identify the grounds of his critique against philosophical reasoning.

Core Idea
Prophetic precedent permits benefiting from non-Muslims in worldly matters; their philosophical reasoning is a mix of truth and falsehood.

In this passage from Naqd al-Mantiq, Ibn Taymiyya addresses a key question: Can Muslims benefit from the knowledge of non-Muslims, especially in fields like medicine? He uses examples from the Prophet's life to argue that it is permissible, even for worldly knowledge. Yet he sharply distinguishes between trustworthy worldly knowledge and the philosophical reasoning of Greek thinkers, which he argues is a mixture of truth and falsehood. This lesson will walk you through his argument and critique.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prophetic precedents
List the examples Ibn Taymiyya gives from the Prophet's life that involve trusting non-Muslims in worldly matters. Note each example and how it demonstrates trustworthiness.
Examples: hiring a polytheist guide during Hijra, accepting counsel from Khuza'ah (including disbelievers), Abu Talib's support despite his polytheism.
2
Extract the underlying principle
From these examples, derive the general rule that Ibn Taymiyya establishes about permissibility of dealing with non-Muslims. Consider the condition he adds about trustworthiness and absence of overriding harm.
3
Apply the analogy to medical books
Observe how Ibn Taymiyya extends the principle to the case of medical books written by non-Muslims. Why does he claim this is even better than hiring a non-Muslim guide? Identify the 'illa (effective cause) that connects the cases.
The 'illa: trustworthiness and impersonal nature of books.
4
Distinguish worldly from religious knowledge
Notice how he treats religious knowledge claimed by philosophers. What two possibilities does he mention regarding their sources? How does he use the Quran to evaluate their rational arguments?
Possibilities: transmission from prophets (worse than People of the Book) or intellectual reasoning (qiyas 'aqli).
5
Evaluate the critique
Summarize Ibn Taymiyya's final verdict on philosophers like Aristotle. What is the overall character of their output? How does the Quran provide a superior alternative?
He brands them as deviant Sabians; their work is a mixture of truth and falsehood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكما استأجر النبي وهو وأبو بكر لما خرجا من مكة مهاجرين رجلا من بني الديل هاديًا خريتا، والخرِّيتُ الماهر بالهداية، وائتمناه على أنفسهما ودوابهما، وواعداه غار ثور صُبحَ ثالثة. وكانت خزاعةُ عَيْبةً نُصْح رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم مسلمهم وكافرهم وكان يقبل نصحهم. وكلُّ هذا في «الصحيحين». وكان أبو طالب ينصر النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ويذب عنه مع شركه. وهذا كثير، فإن المشركين وأهل الكتاب فيهم المؤتمن ، كما قال تعالى: وَمِنْ أَهْلِ الْكِتَابِ مَنْ إِن تَأْمَنْهُ بِقِنطَارٍ يُؤَدِّهِ إِلَيْكَ وَمِنْهُم مِّنْ إِن تَأْمَنَهُ بِدِينَارٍ لَّا يُؤَدِّهِ إِلَيْكَ إِلَّا مَا دُمْتَ عَلَيْهِ قَائِمًا [آل عمران: ٧٥]، ولهذا جاز ائتمان أحدهم على المال، وجاز أن يُسْتَطَبَّ الكافر إذا كان ثقة، نص على ذلك الأئمة كأحمد وغيره؛ إذ ذاك قبول لخبرهم فيما يَعْلَمُونه من أمر الدنيا وائتمان لهم على ذلك، وهو جائز إذا لم يكن فيه مفسدة راجحة مثل ولايته على المسلمين وعلوه عليهم ونحو ذلك. فأخذُ عِلم الطب من كتبهم مثل الاستدلال بالكافر على الطريق واستطبابه، بل هذا أحسن؛ لأن كتبهم لم يكتبوها لمعيَّن من المسلمين حتى تدخل فيها الخيانة، وليس فيها حاجة إلى أحد منهم بالحياة، بل هي مجرَّدُ انتفاع بآثارهم، كالملابس والمساكن والمزارع والسلاح ونحو ذلك. وإن ذَكَر ما يتعلَّق بالدين، فإن نقلوه عن الأنبياء كانوا فيه كأهل الكتاب وأسوأ حالا، وإن أحالوا معرفته على القياس العقلي، فإن وافق ما في القرآن فهو حقٌّ، وإن خالفه ففي القرآن بيان بطلانه بالأمثال المضروبة، كما قال تعالى: وَلَا يَأْتُونَكَ بِمَثَلٍ إِلَّا جِئْنَاكَ بِالْحَقِّ وَأَحْسَنَ تَفْسِيرًا [الفرقان: 33]، ففي القرآن الحق والقياسُ البيِّن الذي يبين بطلان ما جاؤوا به من القياس. وإن كان ما يذكرونه مجملا فيه الحق وفيه الباطل، وهو الغالب على الصَّابئة المبدلين مثل أرسطو وأتباعه وعلى من أتبعهم من الآخرين.
And just as the Prophet, he and Abu Bakr, when they left Mecca as emigrants, hired a man from Banu al-Dil as a skilled guide—and al-kharrīt is the one skilled in guidance—and entrusted him with themselves and their mounts, and they appointed a meeting at the Cave of Thawr on the morning of the third night. And Khuza'ah was a repository of sincere counsel for the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, both their Muslims and disbelievers, and he accepted their counsel. All of this is in the two Sahihs. And Abu Talib used to support the Prophet, peace be upon him, and defend him despite his polytheism. And this is abundant, for among the polytheists and People of the Book are those who are trustworthy, as Allah said: 'And among the People of the Book is he who, if you entrust him with a great amount, he will return it to you, and among them is he who, if you entrust him with a single dinar, he will not return it to you unless you are constantly standing over him' [Al 'Imran: 75]. For this reason, it is permissible to entrust them with wealth, and it is permissible to seek medical treatment from a disbeliever if he is trustworthy; the imams, such as Ahmad and others, ruled accordingly. For that is accepting their report in what they know of worldly matters and entrusting them with that, and it is permissible as long as there is no outweighing harm, such as their being in authority over Muslims or their domination over them, and the like. So taking medical knowledge from their books is like seeking guidance from a disbeliever on the road and seeking medical treatment from him; rather, it is better, because their books were not written for specific Muslims such that betrayal might enter into them, and there is no need for any of them in terms of personal interaction; rather, it is merely benefiting from their artifacts, like clothing, dwellings, farms, weapons, and the like. And if he mentions something related to religion—if they transmit it from the prophets, they are in that regard like the People of the Book, or worse; and if they refer knowledge of it to intellectual reasoning, then if it agrees with the Quran, it is true, and if it contradicts it, then in the Quran is an explanation of its falsity through striking of examples, as Allah said: 'And they do not come to you with an example except that We bring you the truth and the best explanation' [Al-Furqan: 33]. So in the Quran is truth and clear reasoning that explains the falsity of the reasoning they bring. And if what they mention is summary that contains truth and falsehood—and that is the prevailing characteristic of the deviant Sabians, like Aristotle and his followers, and those who followed them among others.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage forms part of Ibn Taymiyya's critical argument against the claim that Greek logic is epistemologically superior. He begins by establishing the permissibility of benefiting from non-Muslims in worldly matters through prophetic precedent. The Prophet hired a polytheist guide during the Hijra, accepted advice from Khuza'ah (both Muslims and disbelievers), and was supported by his polytheist uncle Abu Talib. This demonstrates that trustworthiness exists among non-Muslims, and the Quran itself acknowledges there are trustworthy People of the Book. Therefore, entrusting them with wealth or seeking medical treatment from them is permissible, as imams like Ahmad ruled, provided no overriding harm exists. Ibn Taymiyya then extends this analogy to medical books written by non-Muslims: taking knowledge from them is even better than hiring a non-Muslim guide, because books are impersonal and do not involve ongoing reliance on the author. They are simply artifacts we benefit from, like clothing or weapons. However, he then distinguishes religious matters. If the philosophers transmit religion from prophets, they are worse than the People of the Book. If they rely on rational analogy, then any agreement with the Quran is true, but any contradiction is refuted by the Quran's own superior reasoning. The Quran provides clear analogy (qiyas bayyin) that exposes the falsity of their reasoning. Finally, he notes that most of what philosophers like Aristotle and his followers produce is a mixture of truth and falsehood, characterizing them as deviant Sabians. This passage thus critically undermines the authority of Greek philosophical reasoning in matters of religion and knowledge.

Key Terms
استطباب
قياس عقلي
صابئة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because medical knowledge is more beneficial than guidance on a road.
B) Because books are impersonal and do not involve ongoing reliance on the author.
C) Because non-Muslim guides are always untrustworthy.
D) Because the Quran explicitly permits medical knowledge from books.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya permits benefiting from non-Muslim worldly knowledge through analogical reasoning from prophetic precedent, but denies the epistemic authority of philosophical reasoning in religious matters, insisting on the Quran's sufficiency.

Philosophers' Intellects and Souls vs. Angels as Messengers
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the equation of intellects/souls with angels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key difference between the philosophers' concept of intellects/souls and the Islamic concept of angels as messengers of God, and evaluate Ibn Taymiyya's refutation.

Core Idea
Angels are messengers; philosophers' intellects lack this essential function.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya first presents the falasifa's definitions of intellect and soul, then rejects their attempt to equate these with angels. He argues that the Qur'anic term 'angel' implies being a messenger of God, a role absent in the philosophers' metaphysical hierarchy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the philosophers' definitions
Find how the philosophers define 'aql (intellect) and nafs (soul). Intellect is spirit abstracted from matter; soul is spirit managing the body.
2
Understand the celestial hierarchy
Notice that each celestial sphere has a soul (for voluntary motion) and each soul has an intellect (for perfection without motion).
3
Locate the philosophers' claim about angels
Identify the claim that the intellects and souls are the angels according to the prophets. This is the target of Ibn Taymiyya's refutation.
4
Analyze Ibn Taymiyya's Qur'anic argument
Examine how Ibn Taymiyya cites Qur'anic verses to show that angels are messengers (rusul) of God, executing His commands.
The essential function of messengers is absent in the philosophers' concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيقولون: العقل هو الرُّوح المجرَّدة عن المادة، وهي الجسد وعلائقها، سموه عقلا ويسمونه مفارقا، ويسمون تلك المفارقات للمواد لأنها مفارقة للأجساد، كما أن روح الإنسان إذا فارقت جسده كانت مفارقة للمادة التي الجسد. والنفس هي الروح المدبرة للجسم، مثل نفس الإنسان إذا كانت في جسمه، فمتى كانت في الجسم كانت محرّكةً له، فإذا فارقته صارت عقلا محضا، أي: يَعْقِلُ العلوم من غير تحريك بشيء من الأجسام. فهذه العقول والنفوس، وهذا الذي ذكرناه مِن أحسن الترجمة عن معنى العقل والنفس، وأكثرهم لا يحصلون ذلك. قالوا: وأثبتنا لكل فلك نفسًا لأن الحركة اختيارية فلا تكون إلا لنفس، ولكل نفس عقلا لأن العقل كامل لا يحتاج إلى حركة، والمتحرك يطلب الكمال فلا بد أن يكون فوقه ما يتشبه به وما يكون علة له، ولهذا كانت حركة أنفسنا للتشبه بما فوقنا من العقول، وكلُّ ذلك تشبه بواجب الوجود بحسب الإمكان. والأول لا يَصْدر عنه إلا عقل؛ لأن النفس تقتضي جسما، والجسم فيه كثرة، والصَّادرُ عنه لا يكون إلا واحدًا ولهم في الصدور اختلاف كثير ليس هذا موضعه. قيل لهم: أما إثباتكم أن في السماء أرواحا فهذا يُشْبِهُ ما في القرآن وغيره من كتب الله، ولكن ليست هي الملائكة كما يقول الذين يزعمون منكم أنهم آمنوا بما أُنزِلَ على الرسول وما أُنزِل من قبله ويقولون: ما أردنا إلا الإحسان والتوفيق بين الشَّريعة والفلسفة، فإنهم قالوا: العقول والنفوس عند الملائكة عند الأنبياء. وليس كذلك، لكن تُشْبهها من بعض الوجوه. فإن اسم الملائكة والمَلَك يتضمَّنُ أنهم رُسُلُ الله، كما قال تعالى: ﴿جَاعِلِ الْمَلَائِكَةِ رُسُلًا﴾ [فاطر: ١]، وكما قال: ﴿وَالْمُرْسَلَاتِ عُرْفًا﴾ [المرسلات: ١]، فالملائكة رسل الله في تنفيذ أمره الكوني الذي يدبر به السموات والأرض، كما قال: ﴿حَتَّى إِذَا جَاءَ أَحَدَكُمُ الْمَوْتُ تَوَفَّتْهُ رُسُلُنَا وَهُمْ لَا يُفَرِّطُونَ﴾ [الأنعام: ٦١]، وكما قال: ﴿بَلَى وَرُسُلُنَا لَدَيْهِمْ يَكْتُبُونَ﴾ [الزخرف: ٨٠]، وأمره الديني الذي...
They say: The intellect is the spirit abstracted from matter, which is the body and its attachments. They call it intellect and call it separate. And they call those separate from matters because they are separate from bodies, just as when the human spirit leaves its body it becomes separate from the matter which is the body. And the soul is the spirit that manages the body, like the human soul when it is in its body. When it is in the body, it moves it, and when it leaves it, it becomes pure intellect, i.e., it knows sciences without moving any bodies. So these are the intellects and souls, and what we have mentioned is among the best translations of the meaning of intellect and soul, and most of them do not attain that. They said: We established for each celestial sphere a soul because motion is voluntary and therefore can only be from a soul, and for each soul an intellect because the intellect is perfect and does not need motion, and the moving thing seeks perfection so there must be above it something to imitate and something that is its cause. For this reason, the motion of our souls is to imitate the intellects above us, and all that is imitation of the Necessary Existence as much as possible. And the First only emanates an intellect because the soul requires a body, and the body has multiplicity, and what emanates from Him must be only one, and they have much disagreement about emanation which is not this place. They were told: As for your establishing that there are spirits in the heavens, this resembles what is in the Qur'an and other books of God, but they are not the angels as claimed by those among you who claim to have believed in what was revealed to the Messenger and what was revealed before him and say: We only intended goodness and reconciliation between the Sharia and philosophy. For they said: The intellects and souls according to the prophets are the angels. But it is not like that, rather they resemble them in some respects. For the name 'angels' (malā'ika) and 'angel' (malak) implies that they are the messengers of God, as He said: "Making the angels messengers" [Fatir:1], and as He said: "By those sent forth in succession" [al-Mursalat:1]. So the angels are God's messengers in executing His cosmic command by which He manages the heavens and the earth, as He said: "Until when death comes to one of you, Our messengers take him, and they do not fail" [al-An'am:61], and as He said: "Yes, and Our messengers are with them writing" [al-Zukhruf:80], and His religious command which...
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This is a critical passage from Ibn Taymiyya's Naqd Mantiq where he first presents the philosophers' (falasifa) theory of intellects ('uqul) and souls (nufus) and then refutes their attempt to equate these with the angels of Islamic revelation. The passage begins by explaining the philosophers' definitions: 'aql (intellect) is the spirit abstracted from matter (the body), also called 'mufariq' (separate). The soul (nafs) is the spirit that manages the body while in it, but upon separation becomes pure intellect. This is their 'best translation' of these terms. They then argue for a celestial hierarchy: each sphere has a soul (because its motion is voluntary and requires a soul) and each soul has an intellect (because the intellect is perfect and unmoving, and the moving thing imitates the perfect). The first emanation from the Necessary Existence is only an intellect, not a soul, because soul implies body and multiplicity. Ibn Taymiyya then presents the objection (qīla lahum): their claim that there are spirits in the heavens resembles what is in the Qur'an, but these are not the angels, contrary to those who claim reconciliation between philosophy and Sharia (like Ibn Rushd, Ibn Sina). The philosophers say that intellects and souls are the angels according to the prophets, but Ibn Taymiyya denies this. He argues that the name 'angels' implies they are messengers of God, citing Qur'anic verses (Fatir:1, al-Mursalat:1, al-An'am:61, al-Zukhruf:80). The angels execute God's cosmic command (amr kawni) and religious command (amr dini). The passage cuts off mid-sentence. The core refutation is that while the philosophers' spirits resemble angels in some ways, they lack the essential attribute of being messengers sent by God. Thus, the philosophers' attempt to harmonize their metaphysics with revelation fails because they misidentify the nature of angels.

النفس
الروح
المفارق
الملائكة

Key Takeaway

Critique of Reducing Angels to Intellects and Souls
Ibn Taymiyya criticizes philosophers who limit angels to intellects and souls.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and articulate Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the philosophers' identification of angels with intellects and souls, and explain why he considers it a reduction.

Core Idea
Angels are not intellects or souls; reducing them ignores their true attributes and numbers as revealed in scripture.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya targets a core claim of the falasifa who identified angels with the ten intellects and the souls of the spheres. He argues that this identification fails on linguistic, metaphysical, and scriptural grounds. By comparing the philosophers' view to that of the Samaritans (who reject most prophets), he underscores the extreme limitation of this philosophical reduction. As you work through this lesson, you will dissect a critical argument from the Islamic intellectual tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claim
The philosophers assert that angels are ten intellects or nineteen keepers of Saqar, equating them with intellects and souls. Identify this claim in the Arabic text: 'فمن جعلهم عشرة، أو تسعة عشر، أو زعم أن التسعة عشر الذين على سَقَر هم العقول والنفوس'.
2
Analyze the linguistic argument
Ibn Taymiyya argues that the names 'angel' and 'intellect' do not agree in description or magnitude. They share only the attribute of being spirits attached to the heavens. Why is this not enough for synonymy?
Reflect on the difference between synonymy (alfaz mutaradifa) and partial overlap.
3
Examine the Samaritan analogy
The Samaritans believe in only a few prophets (Moses and Joshua). Similarly, the philosophers believe in only a fraction of the angels' attributes. How does this analogy strengthen Ibn Taymiyya's critique?
The analogy highlights the extreme reduction: just as Samaritans reject most prophets, philosophers deny most angelic attributes.
4
Identify the epistemological gap
The philosophers attribute to angels only what they know from themselves: knowledge for intellects, voluntary motion for souls. Ibn Taymiyya points out that angels have far more: sciences, states, wills, actions. What does this reveal about the philosophers' methodology?
The philosophers rely on rational deduction alone, ignoring revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمن جعلهم عشرة، أو تسعة عشر، أو زعم أن التسعة عشر الذين على سَقَر هم العقول والنفوس = فهذا جهله بما جاء عن الله ورسوله وضلاله في ذلك بيّن؛ إذ لم يتفق الاسمان في صفة المسمى ولا في قدره كما تكونُ الألفاظ المترادفة، وإنما أتفق المسمَّيان في كون كل منهما روحًا متعلقا بالسَّماوات، وهذا من بعض صفات ملائكة السماوات. فالذي أثبتوه بعض الصفات لبعض الملائكة، وهو بالنسبة إلى الملائكة وصفاتهم وأقدارهم وأعدادهم في غاية القِلَّة، أقل مما يؤمن به السَّامِرَة من الأنبياء بالنسبة إلى الأنبياء؛ إذ لا يؤمنون بعد موسى ويوشع بنبي. كيف وهم لم يثبتوا للملائكة من الصفة إلا مجرَّدَ ما عَلِمُوه من نفوسهم، مجرَّد العلم للعقول، والحركة الإرادية للنفوس. ومن المعلوم أن الملائكة لهم من العلوم والأحوال والإرادات والأعمال
So whoever makes them ten, or nineteen, or claims that the nineteen who are over Saqar are the intellects and souls – this shows his ignorance of what has come from Allah and His Messenger, and his misguidance in that is clear; because the two names do not agree in the description of the named nor in its magnitude, as synonymous expressions do. Rather, the two named things agree only in that each is a spirit attached to the heavens, and this is one of the attributes of the angels of the heavens. So what they have affirmed is some of the attributes of some of the angels, and it is, in relation to the angels, their attributes, their magnitudes, and their numbers, extremely little – less than what the Samaritans believe of the prophets in relation to the prophets; for they do not believe in any prophet after Moses and Joshua. How [can it be otherwise] when they have not affirmed for the angels any attribute except what they have known from themselves: mere knowledge for the intellects, and voluntary motion for the souls. And it is known that the angels have sciences, states, wills, and actions...
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the philosophers' doctrine that identifies angels with the ten intellects or the nineteen keepers of Hell (Saqar). Ibn Taymiyya begins by stating that anyone who holds such a view is ignorant of what Allah and His Messenger have revealed. He argues that the terms 'angel' and 'intellect' (or 'soul') are not synonymous; they do not share the same description or magnitude. The only commonality is that both are spirits connected to the heavens, but this is merely one attribute of the angels. The philosophers have only affirmed a tiny fraction of the angels' true attributes, qualities, numbers, and magnitudes. He compares this to the Samaritans, who reject all prophets after Moses and Joshua — just as the Samaritans have an extremely limited view of prophethood, the philosophers have an extremely limited view of angels. The philosophers only attribute to angels what they know from themselves: intellects have knowledge, souls have voluntary motion. But in reality, angels have far more extensive knowledge, states, wills, and actions.

Key Terms
العقول
النفوس
الملائكة
سَقَر
السَّامِرَة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, why is the philosophers' claim that angels are ten intellects misguided?
A) Because the Qur'an states angels are only nine.
B) Because the names 'angel' and 'intellect' do not agree in description or magnitude, only in being spirits attached to the heavens.
C) Because the philosophers have no knowledge of angels at all.
D) Because the Samaritans have ten prophets.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's critique demonstrates that philosophical redefinitions of Islamic concepts must be evaluated against revelation, and that reducing angels to intellects and souls ignores the vast majority of their revealed attributes.

?

Scriptural Evidence for Angels as Epistemological Proof
Scriptural citations supporting angelic realities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how scriptural evidence (Quran and hadith) functions as an epistemological proof in Islamic theology, and contrast it with purely rational proofs.

Core Idea
Revelation provides certain knowledge about the unseen that syllogistic reasoning cannot establish.

In Naqd al-Mantiq, Ibn Taymiyya argues that revelation is a superior source of knowledge for matters of the unseen, such as angels. This passage compiles Quranic verses and hadiths that directly describe angels and their actions. Your task is to see how this scriptural evidence functions as a proof (dalil) in kalam, and why it is considered more foundational than logical deduction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the types of scriptural evidence
Note which citations are Quranic verses and which are Prophetic hadiths. The Quran is considered the literal word of Allah, while hadith are inspired sayings of the Prophet. Both are sources of certain knowledge in Islam.
The passage also includes a narration from the Companions about the bayt al-ma'mur.
2
Categorize the functions of angels mentioned
List the different roles: supporting believers, punishing disbelievers, taking souls peacefully, descending with glad tidings, recording deeds, and worshipping. This shows the comprehensive nature of angelic activity as revealed.
The hadith about lining up in prayer and the bayt al-ma'mur emphasize their worship.
3
Recognize the epistemological claim
The author presents these texts as direct testimony from the Divine, yielding certainty (yaqin). Contrast this with human logical reasoning about the unseen, which is conjectural (zanni) at best.
This is a key argument in Naqd al-Mantiq: revelation trumps rational speculation in metaphysical matters.
4
Contrast with syllogistic reasoning
Consider how a rationalist might try to prove the existence of angels through syllogisms (e.g., every effect has a cause, the world is temporally originated, hence there must be spiritual intermediaries). Note the added assumptions and potential fallacies compared to direct scriptural assertion.
Ibn Taymiyya would argue that such syllogisms are unnecessary and less certain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال: ﴿يَتَأَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا اذْكُرُوا نِعْمَةَ اللَّهِ عَلَيْكُمْ إِذْ جَاءَ تَكُمْ جُنُودُ فَأَرْسَلْنَا عَلَيْهِمْ ريحا وَجُنُودًا لَّمْ تَرَوْهَا) [الأحزاب: 9]، وقوله: ﴿وَلَوْ تَرَى إِذْ يَتَوَفَّى الَّذِينَ كَفَرُوا الْمَلَيْكَةُ يَضْرِبُونَ وُجُوهَهُمْ وَأَدْبَرَهُمْ وَذُوقُوا عَذَابَ الْحَرِيقِ ﴾ [الأنفال: ٥٠] وقوله و الَّذِينَ نَتَوَفَّهُمُ الْمَلَيْكَةُ طَيِّبِينَ يَقُولُونَ سَلَام عَلَيْكُم ﴾ [النحل: ۳۲] وقوله: ﴿إِنَّ الَّذِينَ قَالُوا رَبُّنَا اللهُ ثُمَّ اسْتَقَامُوا تَتَنَزَّلُ عَلَيْهِمُ الْمَلَيكَةُ أَلَّا تَخَافُوا وَلَا تَحْزَنُوا وَأَبْشِرُوا بِالْجَنَّةِ الَّتِي كُنتُمْ تُوعَدُونَ ) [فصلت: ۳۰] وقوله : حَيَّ إِذَا جَاءَ أَحَدَكُمُ الْمَوْتُ تَوَفَّتْهُ رُسُلُنَا وَهُمْ لَا يُفَرِطُونَ ﴾ [الأنعام: ٦١] ، وقوله: ﴿قُلْ يَتَوَفَّنَكُم مَّلَكُ الْمَوْتِ الَّذِى وَكَلَ بِكُمْ ﴾ [السجدة: ١١]، وقوله: في صحف لمكرمة (٣) مَرْفُوعَةٍ مُطَهَرَ بِأَيْدِى سَفَرَةٍ كرام برروي [عبس: ١٦.١٣). وقوله: وَإِنَّ عَلَيْكُمْ لَحَنفِظِينَ كِرَامًا كَبِينَ (١) يَعْلَمُونَ مَا تَفْعَلُونَ) [الانفطار : ۱۰ - ۱۲]، وقوله: أَمْ يَحْسَبُونَ أَنَّا لَا نَسْمَعُ سِرَهُمْ وَنَجْوَنهُمْ بَلَى وَرُسُلُنَا لَدَيْهِمْ يَكْتُبُونَ ﴾ [الزخرف: (۸]، وقوله: ﴿ما يَلْفِظُ مِن قَوْلٍ إِلَّا لَدَيْهِ رَقِيبٌ عَتِيدٌ ) [ق: ۱۸]، وقوله: (وَالصَّفَتِ فَالرَّجِرَاتِ زَجْرا م فَالتَّلِيَتِ ذكرًا ﴾ [الصافات: ١-٣]، وقوله: فَاسْتَفْتِهِمْ الرَبِّكَ الْبَنَاتُ وَلَهُمُ البَنُونَ )) أَمْ خَلَقْنَا الْمَلَيكَةَ إِننا وَهُمْ شَهِدُونَ ) أَلَا إِنَّهُم مِّنْ إِفْكِيهِمْ لَيَقُولُونَ وَلَدَ اللهُ وَإِنَّهُمْ لَكَذِبُونَ ﴾ إلى قوله: ﴿ وَإِنَّا لَنَحْنُ الصَّافُونَ ) وَإِنَّا لَنَحْنُ المُسبِّحُونَ ﴾ [الصافات: ١٤٩ - ١٦٦]. وفي الصحيحين» (۱) عن جابر بن سمرة عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال: «ألا تَصُفُّون كما تَصُفُ الملائكة عند ربها؟ قالوا: وكيف تصف الملائكة عند ربها؟ قال: «يسدُّون الأوّل [فالأوَّل]، ويتراصون في الصف». و في «الصحيحين» (۲) عن قتادة عن أنس، عن مالك بن صعصعة في حديث المعراج، عن النبي الله لما ذكر صعوده إلى السماء السابعة، قال: فرُفِعَ لي البيت المعمور ، فسألتُ جبريل، فقال: هذا البيت المعمور يصلي فيه كل يوم سبعون ألفَ مَلَك، إذا خرجوا لم يعودوا آخر ما عليهم». وقال البخاري (۳) : وقال همام، عن قتادة عن الحسن، عن أبي هريرة، عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم في البيت المعمور. فهذا أمر لا يحصيه إلا الله . و في «الصحيحين » (٤) عن النبي الله أنه قال: «إذا أمن القارئ فأمنوا؛ فإنه من وافق تأمينه تأمين الملائكة غُفِرَ له ما تقدم من ذنبه»، و في الـروايـة الأخرى في الصحيحين» (٥): «إذا قال: آمين فإن الملائكة في السماء تقول: آمین».
He said: 'O you who have believed, remember the favor of Allah upon you when armies came to you and We sent upon them a wind and armies you did not see' [Al-Ahzab:9] and His saying: 'If you could see when the angels take the souls of those who disbelieve, striking their faces and their backs and tasting the punishment of the burning Fire' [Al-Anfal:50] and His saying: 'Those whom the angels take in a good state, saying, Peace be upon you' [An-Nahl:32] and His saying: 'Indeed, those who have said, Our Lord is Allah, and then remained steadfast, the angels descend upon them, [saying], Fear not nor grieve, and receive good tidings of Paradise which you were promised' [Fussilat:30] and His saying: 'When death comes to one of you, Our messengers take him, and they do not fail' [Al-An'am:61] and His saying: 'Say, The angel of death, who has been entrusted with you, will take you' [As-Sajda:11] and His saying: 'In honored sheets, exalted, purified, in the hands of scribes, noble and righteous' [Abasa:13-16] and His saying: 'And indeed, over you are keepers, noble and recording, knowing what you do' [Al-Infitar:10-12] and His saying: 'Or do they think that We hear not their secrets and their private conversations? Yes, indeed, and Our messengers are with them recording' [Az-Zukhruf:80] and His saying: 'He utters not a word except that with him is an observer prepared' [Qaf:18] and His saying: 'By those who line up in rows, and those who drive away with reproof, and those who recite the reminder' [As-Saffat:1-3] and His saying: 'Then inquire of them: Are there for your Lord daughters while for them sons? Or did We create the angels female while they were witnesses? Is it not from their falsehood that they say, Allah has begotten? And indeed they are liars' up to His saying: 'And indeed we are those who line up in ranks, and indeed we are those who exalt Allah' [As-Saffat:149-166]. And in the two Sahihs from Jabir ibn Samurah from the Prophet, peace be upon him, he said: 'Will you not line up as the angels line up before their Lord?' They said: 'And how do the angels line up before their Lord?' He said: 'They complete the first rows and close ranks.' And in the two Sahihs from Qatadah from Anas from Malik ibn Sa'sa'ah in the hadith of the Ascension, from the Prophet, peace be upon him, when he mentioned his ascent to the seventh heaven, he said: 'The frequented House was raised for me, so I asked Gabriel, and he said: This is the frequented House; seventy thousand angels pray in it every day, when they leave they do not return, that is their last time.' And al-Bukhari said: And Hammam said, from Qatadah from al-Hasan from Abu Hurayrah from the Prophet, peace be upon him, concerning the frequented House. So this is a matter that only Allah can count. And in the two Sahihs from the Prophet, peace be upon him, that he said: 'When the recitor says Ameen, then say Ameen, for whoever's Ameen coincides with the Ameen of the angels, his previous sins will be forgiven.' And in the other narration in the two Sahihs: 'When he says Ameen, the angels in heaven say Ameen.'
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage is a collection of Quranic verses and Prophetic hadiths that the author presents as proofs for the existence, roles, and attributes of angels. The author is using revelation as the primary source of knowledge about the unseen, contrasting with the rationalist approach of the logicians. The verses cover angels' actions such as supporting believers (Al-Ahzab:9), punishing disbelievers at death (Al-Anfal:50), taking souls peacefully (An-Nahl:32), descending upon the steadfast (Fussilat:30), acting as messengers of death (Al-An'am:61, As-Sajda:11), recording deeds (Al-Infitar:10-12, Az-Zukhruf:80, Qaf:18), and their worship and rank (Abasa:13-16, As-Saffat:1-3, 149-166). The hadiths further describe how angels line up in prayer, the frequented House (al-bayt al-ma'mur) where they worship, and the affirmation of Ameen. The author concludes that the number of angels is uncountable except by Allah, emphasizing the vastness of the unseen realm. This section functions as a scriptural argument against those who might deny or doubt angelic realities based on rational criteria alone.

Key Terms
الملائكة
البيت المعمور

Key Takeaway
Scriptural evidence provides direct, certain knowledge about the unseen, whereas logical arguments about angels are derivative and fallible.

Hadith as Epistemological Proof for Angels
Author presents multiple authentic hadith detailing the roles and appearances of angels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze how authentic hadith provide certain knowledge about the existence, roles, and sensory forms of angels, and distinguish this scriptural epistemology from rational speculation.

Core Idea
Authentic hadith give us direct knowledge of the unseen world of angels.

In this passage from Naqd al-Mantiq, Ibn Taymiyya marshals multiple authentic hadith to demonstrate that the existence and activities of angels are known through revelation, not through abstract reason. These hadith describe angels managing natural phenomena, supporting prophets, and appearing in sensory forms. For the logician, this underscores that empirical (here, scripturally reported) evidence can provide certain knowledge about metaphysical realities. We will examine how each hadith functions as evidence and what epistemological claim it supports.

Guided Path
1
Catalog the hadith cited
List each hadith mentioned in the passage. Note the key details: the angel involved, what action is described, and the context (e.g., Day of Judgment, incident at 'Aqaba, revelation modes).
There are at least five distinct hadith: forgiveness of sinful companion, angel of mountains, hadith of the fetus (four words), Jibril supporting Hassan, and modes of revelation.
2
Identify the epistemological function of each hadith
For each hadith, ask: what knowledge about angels does it convey? Does it prove existence, a specific role (e.g., recording deeds, delivering revelation), or the possibility of sensory perception (e.g., Jibril appearing as a Bedouin)?
The hadith of the angel of mountains proves angels have will and can act physically; the revelation hadith shows how angels communicate with prophets.
3
Analyze the author's method of argumentation
Notice that Ibn Taymiyya does not construct a syllogism. Instead, he presents multiple independent reports that collectively establish a body of certain knowledge. This is an inductive-cum-testimonial approach (tawatur).
This contrasts with the Aristotelian reliance on deductive demonstration for metaphysical claims.
4
Evaluate the strength of the evidence
Assess why these hadith are considered certain: they are from the 'Two Sahihs' (Bukhari and Muslim) or agreed-upon, meaning they are mutawatir or at least highly authentic.
Ibn Taymiyya is implicitly arguing that scriptural evidence can yield yaqin (certainty) about matters of the unseen.

And they seek forgiveness for you. He said: He says: I have forgiven them, and given them what they asked, and granted them refuge from what they sought refuge from. And in the Sahih, the hadith of al-Bara' ibn 'Azib: the Prophet, peace be upon him, said to Hassan: Satirize them – or attack them – and Jibril is with you. And Jibril comes to the Prophet, peace be upon him, sometimes in the form of a Bedouin, and sometimes in the form of
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage presents a collection of authentic hadith as evidence for the existence, functions, and sensory manifestations of angels. It begins with a hadith about the Prophet's intercession for his followers on the Day of Judgment, where even sinful companions are forgiven. Then it recounts the incident at 'Aqaba when the angel of the mountains offered to crush the Quraysh between two mountains, but the Prophet declined out of hope for their descendants. The passage then generalizes that many authentic hadith mention angels in various realms. It proceeds to cite the hadith of the truthful and believed one (often referring to the hadith of the fetus) where an angel is commanded to write four aspects of a person's destiny. Further hadith include Jibril supporting Hassan ibn Thabit in satire, the prompting to respond on behalf of the Prophet, and Anas's narration of seeing dust from Jibril's procession. Finally, it describes the modes of revelation: like the ringing of a bell or Jibril appearing as a man, and mentions Jibril's appearances in different forms, such as a Bedouin. Since this is a critique of logic (Naqd Mantiq), these hadith likely serve to ground knowledge of the unseen in revelation rather than rational speculation, demonstrating that scripture provides certain knowledge of angels' activities.

مَلَك
جبريل
وحي

Refuting Emanation: The Necessity of Pairing
Author refutes the principle of emanation using the necessity of pairing in creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the emanation principle and evaluate its logical strength.

Core Idea
Every effect requires an agent and a receiver; the philosophers' analogies actually disprove their claim.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya takes on the philosophers' claim that from one simple cause only one effect can proceed. He uses Quranic exegesis and empirical examples to show that in the actual world, every generated thing requires both an agent and a receiver. This critique is foundational for understanding the Islamic rejection of Neoplatonic emanation and the affirmation of a Creator who acts freely.

Guided Path
1
Identify the philosophers' principle under attack
What exactly is the claim that Ibn Taymiyya is refuting? It is the principle that from one thing only one thing can proceed (emanation from the One).
2
Examine the Quranic foundation
How does Ibn Taymiyya use Mujahid's tafsir of al-shaf' and al-watr to frame the critique? The pairing (shaf') in creation is contrasted with Allah's uniqueness (watr).
3
Dissect the causal structure required for generation
What two principles does Ibn Taymiyya argue are necessary? Every effect requires an agent (father) and a receiver (mother), like sun and earth, fire and wood.
4
Analyze the refutation of the philosophers' analogies
How does he show that rays and sound actually require two causes, not one? The ray from the sun either is an attribute of the sun or requires the earth as receiver; sound requires two bodies striking.
5
Evaluate the overall argument
Does Ibn Taymiyya's critique successfully undermine the emanation principle? He provides concrete counterexamples and turns their analogies against them, concluding that Allah's revelation is the ultimate truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولهذا قال مجاهد - وذكره البخاري في صحيحه (۱) ـ في «الشفع والوَتْر»: إن الشَّفْع هو الخَلق، فكل مخلوق له نظير، والوَثر هو الله الذي لا شبيه له (۲) فقال: أنَّ يَكُونُ لَهُ وَلَدٌ وَلَمْ تَكُن لَهُ صَحِبَةٌ ﴾ [الأنعام: ١٠١]، وذلك أن الآثار الصَّادرة عن العلل والمتولدات في الموجودات لا بد فيها من شيئين: أحدهما يكونُ كالأب، والآخر يكون كالأم القابلة، وقد يسمون ذلك الفاعل» و«القابل»، كالشَّمس مع الأرض، والنار مع الحطب، فأمـا صـدورُ شيء واحد عن شيء واحد فهذا لا وجود له في الوجود أصلا (۳). و أما تشبيههم لذلك بالشُّعاع مع الشمس، وبالصوت ـ كالطنين ــ مـع الحركة والنفر، فهو أيضًا حجة الله ورسوله والمؤمنين عليهم. وذلك أن الشُّعاع إن أريد به نفس ما يقومُ بالشَّمس، فذلك صفةٌ من صفاتها، وصفاتُ الخالق ليست مخلوقةً ولا هـي مـن العـالم الـذي فيه الكلام. وإن أريد بالشُّعاع ما ينعكس على الأرض، فذلك لا بد فيـه مـن شيئين، وهما: الشَّمس التي تجري مجرى الأب الفاعل، والأرض التي تجري مجرى الأم القابلة وهي الصَّاحبة للشمس. وكذلك الصَّوتُ لا يتولَّد إلا عن جسمين يقرعُ أحدهما الآخر أو يُقْلَع عنه، فيتولَّد الصوتُ الموجود في أجسام العالم عن أصلين يقرعُ أحدهما الآخر أو يُقلع عنه (۱). فمهما احتجوا به من القياس فالذي جاء الله به هو الحقُّ وأحسن تفسيرًا وأحسنُ بيانًا وإيضاحا للحق وكشفا له.
And for this reason Mujahid said – and al-Bukhari mentioned it in his Sahih (1) – regarding 'al-shaf' and al-watr': Indeed, al-shaf' is creation, so every created thing has a counterpart, and al-watr is Allah who has no likeness. He said: 'How can He have a child when He has no female companion?' [Al-An'am 101]. And that is because the effects emanating from causes and generated things in existents necessarily involve two things: one is like the father, and the other is like the receptive mother. They sometimes call these the 'agent' and the 'receiver', like the sun with the earth, and fire with kindling. As for the emanation of one thing from one thing, this has no existence in existence at all (3). And their analogy of that with the ray from the sun, and with sound – like a ringing – from motion and striking, is also a proof against them from Allah, His Messenger, and the believers. That is because the ray: if what is meant is what inheres in the sun itself, that is one of its attributes, and the attributes of the Creator are not created nor are they from the world under discussion. And if what is meant by the ray is what is reflected on the earth, then it necessarily involves two things: the sun which runs in the course of the agent father, and the earth which runs in the course of the receptive mother, and she is the companion of the sun. Likewise, sound is not generated except from two bodies, one of which strikes the other or is separated from it; thus the sound existing in the bodies of the world is generated from two origins, one striking the other or being separated from it. So whatever analogy they argue with, what Allah has brought is the truth, and best in interpretation, and best in explanation and clarification of the truth and uncovering it.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the philosophers' claim that one thing can proceed from one thing alone. Ibn Taymiyya begins by citing Mujahid's interpretation of 'al-shaf' and al-watr' from the Quran, where shaf' (even/pair) refers to creation—every creature has a counterpart—and watr (odd) refers to Allah who has no equal. He then argues that all effects in the world require two principles: one acting as father (agent) and one as mother (receiver), such as the sun and earth, or fire and wood. He denies that anything proceeds from a single cause alone. The philosophers compare this to rays from the sun or sound from motion, but Ibn Taymiyya dissects these analogies: the ray that inheres in the sun is an attribute, not a separate created thing; the ray reflected on earth requires both the sun and the earth. Similarly, sound requires two bodies striking or separating. Thus, these examples actually refute the philosophers' claim. The author concludes that any analogy they bring is inferior to the truth brought by Allah.

Key Terms
الشفع (al-shaf'): That which is paired; interpreted by Mujahid as creation, because every created thing has a counterpart. [Note: Used generally in Quranic exegesis; here specifically applied to the argument of pairing in creation.]
الوتر (al-watr): The odd/unique; interpreted as Allah, who has no likeness. [Note: Stable in tafsir as referring to Allah's uniqueness.]
العلة (al-'illa): Cause; here referring to the efficient and material causes in the philosophers' scheme, which the author critiques as requiring a pair. [Note: In this context, the author uses 'illa broadly to mean any cause, not limited to the technical sense in logic.]
الفاعل (al-fa'il): The agent or efficient cause, analogous to the father in generation. [Note: Standard term in falsafah and kalam; no significant shift here.]
القابل (al-qabil): The receiver or material cause, analogous to the mother. [Note: Standard term; no shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because sound is an attribute of the striker alone.
B) Because sound is generated from two origins: a striker and a struck body.
C) Because sound is a separate substance created directly by Allah.
D) Because sound is a necessary emanation from a single cause.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya demonstrates that the philosophers' emanation principle contradicts observable reality, where every effect results from pairing an agent with a receiver.

Critique of Philosophers' Deification of Intellects and Souls
Author condemns philosophers for treating separate intellects and souls as complete causes and objects of worship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key accusations in Ibn Taymiyya's critique against philosophers treating separate intellects and souls as complete causes and objects of worship, and assess the theological basis for his charge of kufr.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya exposes that philosophical emanationism leads to shirk by treating secondary causes as independent deities.

This passage from Ibn Taymiyya's Naqd al-Mantiq targets the metaphysical core of Islamic philosophy: the emanationist hierarchy of intellects and souls. He argues that the philosophers do not merely describe causal chains but elevate these entities to the status of lords and gods, contradicting tawhid. This lesson dissects his critique and examines its theological foundations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target doctrine
Determine what the philosophers claim about the separate intellects and celestial souls. They treat them as principles (mabadi'), complete causes ('illa tammah) that necessitate their effects, and even as parents (father/mother).
Focus on the terms 'complete cause' and 'parents' as key indicators of the deification.
2
Extract the key accusations
List the specific accusations Ibn Taymiyya levels: making them complete causes, producers, necessitators, calling them fathers/mothers (intellect as father, soul as mother, or intellect and nature as parents), and labeling them 'lords and little gods' whom they worship.
Ibn Taymiyya uses polemical language to highlight the perceived shirk.
3
Analyze the evidence cited
Examine Ibn Taymiyya's citation of Ibn Arabi's Fusus al-Hikam, where Noah's prayer for his parents is reinterpreted as referring to the intellect and nature. This shows how philosophical interpretations distort scripture to support their cosmology.
The citation serves as evidence that philosophers themselves acknowledge these entities as creative principles.
4
Evaluate the theological charge
Assess why Ibn Taymiyya declares this kufr. He bases it on the message of all prophets, which demands exclusive worship of Allah alone (tawhid). Attributing creative power and worship to any being besides Allah violates this core principle.
This step distinguishes metaphysical speculation from revealed truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضًا، فجعلوها (۲) علَّةً تامَّةٌ لما تحتها (۳) ومُحْدِثةٌ له (٤) ومُوجبة له، حتى يجعلونها مبادئنا ويجعلونها لنا كالآباء والأمهات، وربما جعلوا العقل هو الأب والنفس هي الأم ، وربما قال بعضهم: الوالدان العقل والطبيعة، كما قال صاحب الفصوص في قول نوح رَبِّ اغْفِرْ لِي وَلِوَلِدَى ﴾ [نوح: ۲۸] : «أي: من كنتُ نتيجةً عنهما، وهما العقل والطبيعة» (٥). وحتى يسمونها الأرباب والآلهة الصغرى» (٦)، ويعبدونها، وهو كفر مخالف لما جاءت به الرسل.
And also, they made them (2) a complete cause for what is under them (3) and a producer of it (4) and a necessitator of it, such that they make them our principles and make them for us like fathers and mothers. And sometimes they make the intellect the father and the soul the mother, and sometimes some of them say: the two parents are the intellect and nature, as the author of Fusus said regarding the saying of Noah: 'My Lord, forgive me and my parents' [Nuh 28]: 'Meaning: those from whom I originated, and they are the intellect and nature.' And they even call them 'lords and little gods' (6), and worship them, and that is disbelief contrary to what the messengers brought.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage is a scathing critique of the philosophers' metaphysical hierarchy. Ibn Taymiyya accuses them of treating the ten separate intellects and nine celestial souls (which they equate with angels) as complete and efficient causes that necessitate their effects. They elevate these entities to the status of principles and liken them to parents—sometimes calling intellect the father and soul the mother, or intellect and nature as parents. The author cites Ibn Arabi's Fusus al-Hikam, where Noah's prayer for his parents is reinterpreted as referring to intellect and nature. Ibn Taymiyya then charges that the philosophers go so far as to call these entities 'lords and little gods' and worship them, which constitutes disbelief (kufr) that contradicts the message of all prophets. The critique is directed against the emanationist cosmology that attributes creative power to secondary causes rather than directly to Allah.

Key Terms
العلة التامة
العقل
النفس
الطبيعة
الأرباب والآلهة الصغرى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does Ibn Taymiyya accuse the philosophers of doing with the separate intellects and souls?
A) Treating them as mere metaphors for natural processes.
B) Making them complete causes and worshipping them as little gods.
C) Denying their existence altogether.
D) Confusing them with the angels of revelation.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's charge is that philosophical emanationism, by treating intellects and souls as complete causes, effectively sets up 'little gods' alongside Allah, which is the core of kufr.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of 'Cause' and Preference for 'Generation'
Author argues that 'علة' is inappropriate for God and 'تولد' better fits revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze Ibn Taymiyya's linguistic and theological critique of the term 'علة' and defend his preference for 'تولد' using evidence from the passage.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya argues that 'علة' implies change, making it unfit for God; 'تولد' is more fitting.

This lesson examines Ibn Taymiyya's critical refinement of key theological terms. He argues that the common term 'cause' (علة) carries connotations of change that are inappropriate for God, and that the term 'generation' (تولد) better captures the relation between God and creation without such implications. By analyzing his linguistic and theological reasoning, you will understand how terminology shapes Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of critique
Find where the author explicitly states that the term 'علة' (cause) is problematic. What does he say about its original meaning?
Look for the phrase 'فإن العلة أصلها هو التغير'.
2
Trace the linguistic origin of 'علة'
The author provides a linguistic etymology: 'علة' originally means change, like illness. He also notes that 'معلول' (effect) was originally used only for drinking. Understand why this makes the term inappropriate for God.
Consider how the term's origin contrasts with God's immutability.
3
Analyze the alternative: 'تولد'
The author argues that 'تولد' (generation) is more apparent and common in everyday speech. He gives examples from naturalists about elements and generators. Identify why he thinks this term is more suitable for divine action.
Note that 'تولد' does not carry the same connotation of change.
4
Extract theological conclusions
The author ties the linguistic preference to theology: God's negation of generating anything refutes claims of Him having offspring. Find the specific refutations mentioned (Christians, Jews, philosophers).
This shows how terminology directly impacts creedal issues.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن تنزيهه نفسه عن الولد والولادة واتخاذ الولد أعم وأقوم من نفيه بلفظ «العلة»؛ فإن العلة أصلها هو التغير، كالمرض الذي يجيل البدن عن صحته، والعليل ضد الصحيح. وقد قيل: إنه لا يقال: «معلول» إلا في الشرب، يقال: شرب الماء علّا بعد نهل، وعللته: إذا سقيته مرة ثانية. وأما استعمال اسم «العلة» في الموجب للشيء أو المقتضي له فهو من عرف أهل الكلام، وهي وإن كان بينها وبين العلة اللغوية مناسبة من جهة التغير فالمناسبة في لفظ «التولد» أظهر؛ ولهذا كان في الخطاب أشهر، يقول الناس: هذا الأمر يتولد عنه كذا، وهذا يولد كذا، وقد تولد عن ذلك الأمر كيت وكيت، لكل سبب اقتضى مسببا من الأقوال والأعمال، حتى أهل الطبائع يقولون: «الأركان والمولدات»، يريدون ما يتولد عن الأصول الأربعة التراب والماء والهواء والنار = من معدن ونبات وحيوان. فنفيه سبحانه عن نفسه أن يلد شيئًا اقتضى ألا يتولد عنه شيء، ونفيه أن يتخذ ولدا يقتضي أنه لم يفعل ذلك بشيء من خلقه على سبيل التكريم، وأن العباد لا يصلح أن يتخذ شيئًا منهم بمنزلة الولد، وهذا يبطل [ دعوى] من يدعي مثل ذلك في المسيح وغيره، ومن يقول: ﴿ نَحْنُ ابْنَتَوُا اللَّهِ ﴾ [المائدة: ١٨]، ومن يقول : الفلسفة هي التشبه بالإله.
For His glorifying Himself from offspring, children, and taking a son is more general and more upright than negating it by the word 'cause'; for the cause originally is change, like illness that alters the body from its health, and the sick person is opposite of the healthy. And it has been said that 'effect' (ma'lul) is only used in drinking: it is said 'he drank water after quenching' and 'I gave him to drink a second time'. As for using the name 'cause' for that which necessitates something or requires it, it is from the convention of the theologians. And although there is a connection between it and the linguistic cause in terms of change, the connection in the word 'generation' (tawallud) is more apparent; and for this reason it is more common in discourse. People say: 'This matter generates such-and-such', and 'this produces such-and-such', and 'such-and-such has emerged from that matter' for every cause that necessitates an effect from statements and actions, even the naturalists say: 'the elements and generators', meaning that which is generated from the four principles (earth, water, air, fire) — mineral, plant, and animal. So His glorifying Himself from generating anything entailed that nothing is generated from Him, and His negation of taking a child entails that He did not do that with any of His creation as a way of honoring, and that the servants are not suitable to be taken as a child. And this invalidates the claim of those who claim such in the case of the Messiah and others, and those who say 'We are the children of Allah', and those who say 'Philosophy is assimilation to God'.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of the term 'cause' (علة) as used in philosophical theology and its inadequacy compared to the term 'generation' (تولد) in describing God's relation to creatures. The author begins by arguing that the Quran's way of negating offspring and children from God is more comprehensive and precise than negating by the word 'cause'. He explains that the root meaning of 'cause' in language is change, like illness, and that the word 'effect' (معلول) is originally used only in the context of drinking. The term 'cause' as used by theologians (متكلمون) to mean that which necessitates something is a technical convention, and while it has some connection to the linguistic sense via change, the term 'generation' (تولد) is more fitting because it is commonly used in everyday speech for causes producing effects. The naturalists also use 'generation' to refer to the production of minerals, plants, and animals from the four elements. The author then ties this to theology: God's negation of generating anything (نفيه أن يلد) implies that nothing is generated from Him, and His negation of taking a child implies that He does not treat any creature as a child for honor. This refutes the Christian claim about the Messiah and the Jewish claim from Quran 5:18. Finally, the author refutes the philosophical maxim that philosophy is assimilation to God, implying that such a claim is invalid because it improperly attributes generation or divine relation to creatures.

Key Terms
العلة
التولد
المعلول
الولد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does Ibn Taymiyya argue that 'التولد' is more appropriate than 'العلة' for describing God's relation to creation?
A) Because 'التولد' is used in the Quran, while 'العلة' is not.
B) Because 'التولد' has a stronger connection to change than 'العلة'.
C) Because 'التولد' is more common in everyday language and does not imply change, unlike 'العلة' which originally means change.
D) Because 'التولد' is a term used by naturalists, making it more scientific.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's critique shows that even technical theological terms must be scrutinized linguistically to ensure they do not imply inappropriate attributes for God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In the Quran, Allah says 'لَمْ يَلِدْ وَلَمْ يُولَدْ' (He begets not, nor was He begotten). Compare this Quranic expression with Ibn Taymiyya's argument about 'تولد'. Explain how the Quranic wording supports his preference.
?
Identify a theological claim in contemporary Islamic discourse that uses 'علة' for God. Apply Ibn Taymiyya's critique to evaluate whether the term is appropriate, and suggest an alternative phrasing using 'تولد'.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Innovators Using the Hadith of Jesus
Ibn Taymiyya critiques innovators using a hadith about Allah granting knowledge to the Muslim ummah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that the Muslim ummah's knowledge is a divine gift and explain how this refutes innovators who rely on personal opinion.

Core Idea
True knowledge is a divine gift for those who follow the Prophet, not a product of human reasoning.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya targets innovators (ahl al-bid‘a) who introduce religious opinions without precedent from the Salaf. He cites a prophetic hadith in which Allah tells Jesus that the Muslim ummah, despite lacking inherent knowledge and forbearance, will be given them by Allah. This directly challenges the innovator's claim that personal reasoning can be a valid source of religious knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of critique
Find the opening line where Ibn Taymiyya condemns 'the innovators and invented opinions'. He says they have no predecessor except foolishness from those with evil intent.
This sets up the opposition between following revelation and following personal whim.
2
Locate the prophetic hadith cited
Read the hadith where Allah speaks to Jesus: 'I will create a nation that I will prefer over every nation, and they have no knowledge nor forbearance.' Jesus questions this, and Allah responds: 'I bestow upon them from My knowledge and My forbearance.'
The hadith is presented as a proof-text.
3
Extract the logical core
The key point: the ummah is preferred despite lacking knowledge inherently. Allah gives them knowledge and forbearance as a gift. This implies that true knowledge comes from divine bestowal, not from human intellect.
Contrast this with the innovators who claim authority for their own opinions.
4
Apply to the refutation of bid'a
Innovators rely on their own reasoning and opinions. The hadith shows that the ummah's distinction is based on receiving divine knowledge through following the Prophet. Therefore, any innovation that departs from the prophetic path is illegitimate.
This is a critique of those who prioritize reason over revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبتدعة والآراء المخترعة، لم يكن لهم في ذلك سلف إلا رعونات النفوس المتلقاة ممَّن ساء قصده في الدين. ويروى أن الله سبحانه قال للمسيح : إني سأخلقُ أُمَّةً أفضّلُها على كل أمَّةٍ وليس لها علم ولا حِلم، فقال المسيح : أي ربِّ، كيف تفضّلهم على جميع الأمم وليس لهم علمٌ ولا حِلم؟ قال: أهبُهم من علمي وحلمي (۱). (1) أخرجه أحمد (٢٧٥٤٥) ، والبخاري في التاريخ الكبير (٣٥٥/٨)، والخرائطي في فضيلة الشكر» (۱۹)، والطبراني في «الأوسط» (٣٢٥٢) وغيرهم من حديث يزيد بن ميسرة عن أم الدرداء عن أبي الدرداء رضي اللهُ عَنْهُ مرفوعا: «إن الله عز وجل يقول: يا عيسى إني باعث من بعدك أمةً إن أصابهم ما يحبون حمدوا الله وشكروا، وإن أصابهم ما يكرهون احتسبوا وصبروا، ولا حلم ولا علم، قال: يا رب كيف هذا لهم ولا حلم ولا علم ؟ قال : أعطيهم من حلمي وعلمي». ولا بأس بإسناده، وصححه الحاكم (٣٤٨/١) ولم يتعقبه الذهبي، وحسنه البزار ٤۰۸۸) ، وابن حجر في الأمالي المطلقة» (٤٩) ، إلا أن البزار وهم في اسم راويه يزيد فجعله أخاه يونس. وأعله الألباني في الضعيفة» (٤٠٣٨، ٤٩٩١) و محققو «المسند» بأن يزيد بن ميسرة مجهول الحال لم تثبت عدالته ولم يرو عنه إلا اثنان وليس كذلك، بل هو زاهد واعظ معروف من أتباع التابعين بالشام، له أخبار كثيرة وأقوال مأثورة في «الزهد » لأبي داود (٣٩٣ - ٣٩٦) ، و ( الحلية (٢٣٤/٥ - ٢٤٣)، و«تاريخ الإسلام (٣٤٠/٣) وغيرها، وروى عنه جماعة فوق العشرة، وأورده ابن حبان في «الثقات» (٦٢٧/٧) ، ولم يَرو ما يُنْكَر، فمثله مع تصحيح الحاكم له يحسّن حديثه ما لم ينفرد بما لا يحتمل، كما قال الذهبي في الموقظة» (۷۸) : «وإن صحح له كالدارقطني والحاكم فأقل أحواله حُسْنُ حديثه». لكن قد ذُكر أنه قرأ الكتب (يعني كتب أهـل الكتاب) كما في مصنف ابن أبي شيبة» (٣٥٤۳۱)، وروى عنها كثيرًا، فيتأنى في = مروياته المرفوعة التي من هذا اللون خاصَّة، فإنها مظنَّة الوهم، لاحتمال أن تكون مما قرأه من تلك الكتب ورفعها خطاً على سبيل التوهم، كما يقعُ لغيره ممن ليس الحديث من صناعته، إلا أن في حديثه هذا قرينة تدفع عنه الوهم وتدلُّ على ضبطه له، وهي أنه قال في روايته: سمعت أم الدرداء تقول: سمعت أبا الدرداء يقول: سمعت أبا القاسم - ما سمعته يكنيه قبلها ولا بعدها ـ يقول...» ثم ذكره، وقد قال الإمام أحمد: إذا كان في الحديث قصة دلّ على أن راويه حفظه»، انظر: «هدى الساري» (٣٦٣).
The innovators and invented opinions had no predecessor in that except the foolishness of souls received from those who had evil intent in religion. And it is narrated that Allah the Exalted said to Jesus: 'I will create a nation that I will prefer over every nation, and they have no knowledge nor forbearance.' Jesus said: 'O Lord, how will You prefer them over all nations while they have no knowledge nor forbearance?' He said: 'I bestow upon them from My knowledge and My forbearance (1).' (1) Ahmad (27545), al-Bukhari in al-Tarikh al-Kabir (8/355), al-Khara'iti in Fadilat al-Shukr (19), al-Tabarani in al-Awsat (3252) and others from the hadith of Yazid ibn Maysara from Umm al-Darda' from Abu al-Darda' may Allah be pleased with him raised: 'Indeed Allah Almighty says: O Jesus, I am sending after you a nation who, if they get what they love, praise Allah and thank, and if they get what they dislike, seek reward and are patient, and there is no forbearance nor knowledge. He said: O Lord, how is this for them while they have no forbearance nor knowledge? He said: I give them from My forbearance and My knowledge.' And its chain is acceptable, al-Hakim authenticated it (1/348) and al-Dhahabi did not comment, al-Bazzar (4088) and Ibn Hajar in al-Amali al-Mutlaqah (49) deemed it good, except that al-Bazzar erred in the name of its narrator Yazid making him his brother Yunus. And al-Albani in al-Da'ifah (4038, 4991) and the editors of al-Musnad declared it weak because Yazid ibn Maysara is unknown in status, his integrity not established, and only two narrated from him, but that is not correct; rather he is a known ascetic preacher from the Followers in Syria, with many reports and sayings in al-Zuhd by Abu Dawud (393-396), al-Hilyah (5/234-243), Tarikh al-Islam (3/340) etc., and more than ten narrated from him, and Ibn Hibban mentioned him in al-Thiqat (7/627), and he did not narrate anything objectionable, so his like with al-Hakim's authentication improves his hadith as long as he does not single out something improbable, as al-Dhahabi said in al-Muqiẓah (78): 'If al-Daraqutni and al-Hakim authenticate for him, the least of his status is that his hadith is good.' But it was mentioned that he read the books (meaning books of the People of the Book) as in Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah (35431), and narrated a lot from them, so one must be cautious regarding = his raised narratives of this type, for they are subject to illusion, due to the possibility that they are from what he read from those books and raised erroneously, as happens to others who are not hadith specialists, except that in this hadith there is an indication that repels the illusion and indicates his accuracy: that he said in his narration: 'I heard Umm al-Darda' say: I heard Abu al-Darda' say: I heard Abu al-Qasim - I never heard him use a kunya before or after - say...' then he mentioned it. And Imam Ahmad said: 'If there is a story in the hadith, it indicates that its narrator preserved it.' See: Huda al-Sari (363).
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage is a critique of innovators (ahl al-bid‘a) who rely on personal opinions rather than revelation. Ibn Taymiyya opens by stating that these innovators have no valid predecessors except the foolishness of those with evil intentions. He then cites a prophetic hadith in which Allah tells Jesus that He will create a Muslim ummah that, despite lacking intrinsic knowledge and forbearance, will be given them by Allah. This supports the idea that true knowledge and forbearance are divine gifts granted to those who follow the Prophet, not products of human intellect. The remainder of the passage is a detailed critical evaluation of the hadith's chain of narration. Ibn Taymiyya defends the narrator Yazid ibn Maysara against claims of weakness, citing multiple scholars who authenticated or deemed good the hadith, and addressing the issue that Yazid read scriptures of the People of the Book. Ibn Taymiyya argues that the hadith contains a clue (a story about hearing directly) that protects against the suspicion that it was mistakenly raised from those scriptures. Thus, the passage serves to ground the superiority of the Muslim ummah in sound prophetic tradition, undermining the claims of innovators who might rely on their own reasoning.

Key Terms
بدعة
متابعة الرسول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya's use of the hadith in this passage, why is the Muslim ummah preferred over previous nations?
A) Because they are naturally more intelligent.
B) Because Allah bestows upon them His knowledge and forbearance despite their lack of prior learning.
C) Because they have more prophets.
D) Because they have better philosophers.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya argues that the Muslim ummah's knowledge is a divine gift, not a human achievement, refuting innovators who rely on personal opinion.
